Building on a Corruptly Purchased House: The Buyer Pays Value
Ruling and proof for compensating a buyer who builds after a corrupt sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on building after a corrupt sale and explain the legal reasoning (dalil) that justifies payment of value instead of return of the property.

Core Idea
A buyer who builds on a corruptly purchased house must pay its value because building is intended for permanence, not demolition.

When a sale is corrupt (fasid), the general principle is that the property must be returned. But what if the buyer has made permanent improvements like building? This lesson explores a specific Hanafi ruling: the buyer keeps the property and compensates the seller with its market value. The proof relies on the customary understanding that building is meant to last—a principle that overrides the usual return obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act: the buyer builds in a house purchased with a corrupt sale. The ruling: the buyer must pay the value (qimat) of the house to the seller and cannot be forced to return the house.
The ruling applies at the time the building is erected, not afterward.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is a customary principle: building is intended for permanence (ad-dawam), as expressed by 'building is not for demolition'.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof) based on custom (urf).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof yields the ruling)
Because building is for permanence, demolishing it to return the house would cause waste (israf). Therefore, the law awards the seller compensation (qimat) instead of restitution in kind.
This reasoning is a form of istihsan (juristic preference).
4
Examine the authority and transmission
The ruling is narrated by Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa, though Abu Yusuf later doubted his hearing. Despite this, the ruling is considered valid and part of the nawadir (rare transmissions).
The doubt does not negate the ruling's application in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو بنى في دار شراها شراء فاسداً لَزِمَهُ قيمتها ، وشَكٍّ أبو يوسف له فيها [١] قوله : ولو بنى...الخ ؛ يعني لو اشترى داراً شراء فاسداً فبناها ، لزم المشتري قيمة هذه الدار، وليس للبائع أن يأخذها ، وهذه المسألة رواها أبو يوسف له ثمَّ شَكٍّ بعد ذلك في سماعه إياها عن الإمام . [٢] قوله : لزمه قيمتها ؛ لأنَّ البناءَ مما يقصد به الدوام ؛ إذ البناء ليس للنقض، وإنما يفيد دليلاً للمسألة لا يرد عليه ما يرد على «الهداية»،
And if he builds in a house he bought with a corrupt purchase, he must pay its value, and Abu Yusuf had a doubt about hearing this from him. [1] His statement: 'And if he builds...etc.' means: If someone buys a house with a corrupt purchase and then builds on it, the buyer must pay the value of this house, and the seller does not have the right to take it. Abu Yusuf related this from him (Imam Abu Hanifa), but then later doubted whether he heard it from the Imam. [2] His statement: 'He must pay its value' because building is intended for permanence; indeed, building is not for demolition, and it only provides proof for the issue; nothing from al-Hidayah rebuts it.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Abu Hanifa narrated by Abu Yusuf: If someone buys a house with a corrupt sale (bay' fasid) and then erects a building on it, the buyer must pay the value (qimat) of the house to the seller, and the seller cannot reclaim the house itself. The proof given is that building is intended for permanence (ad-dawam); it is not meant to be demolished. Therefore, the buyer's building indicates a lasting use, so the seller is compensated with the value instead of being able to undo the transaction. The passage also notes that Abu Yusuf initially narrated this ruling from Abu Hanifa but later doubted whether he actually heard it from him. This is a point of historical transmission (riwayah) affecting the authority of the ruling. The commentator adds that this ruling serves as a proof for the issue and is not contradicted by al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
قيمتها (qimatiha): The monetary value (price) of the house at the time of the corrupt sale, to be paid by the buyer to the seller.
البناء ليس للنقض (al-bina' laysa li al-naqd): Building is intended for permanence, not for demolition; thus it creates a lasting change justifying compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why must the buyer pay the value (qimat) of the house rather than return the house itself after building on it in a corrupt sale?
A) Because the buyer owns the house by virtue of possession.
B) Because building is intended for permanence, so the seller cannot demand demolition.
C) Because the corrupt sale is void ab initio, so the buyer must return everything.
D) Because the seller agreed to the building.

Key Takeaway
In a corrupt sale, if the buyer builds on the property, the buyer keeps the property and must pay its value, because building is for permanence.

The Transmission of This Ruling: Abu Yusuf's Doubt
Intra-Hanafi transmission issue regarding Abu Yusuf's doubt about a specific ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the authority of a Hanafi ruling narrated by Abu Yusuf with a doubt and explain how the Hanafi school addresses such transmission issues.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf's doubt about hearing a ruling does not automatically invalidate it; the Hanafi school still applies it based on the original narration.

In the previous lesson, we examined the ruling on building after a corrupt sale. This lesson zooms in on the transmission footnote: Abu Yusuf, one of the founding disciples of Abu Hanifa, narrated the ruling but later doubted whether he heard it directly from the Imam. How do Hanafi jurists weigh such a doubt in the context of riwayah? Understanding this strengthens your ability to assess the authority of transmitted rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the narrator and his doubt
Abu Yusuf narrated this ruling from Abu Hanifa but later expressed doubt about whether he heard it (شَكٍّ في سماعه).
This is a personal doubt, not a defect in the chain.
2
Understand the effect of doubt in Hanafi riwayah
In Hanafi hadith methodology, the default is that a reliable narrator's report is accepted unless there is clear evidence of error. A narrator's own doubt, especially if stated after the narration, is considered a caution but does not necessarily negate the earlier certainty.
The doubt may reflect Abu Yusuf's meticulousness rather than actual error.
3
Apply this to the ruling's authority
Because the original narration from Abu Yusuf is preserved, the ruling is considered part of the Nawadir (rare transmissions) and is still used in fatwa. However, its authority is lower than Zahir al-Riwayah.
The commentary notes that the ruling is not contradicted by al-Hidayah, indicating it is accepted.
4
Practical implication for mufti
When encountering a ruling with a transmission doubt, a mufti checks if there is corroborating evidence or if the ruling is opposed by stronger transmissions. Here, the doubt does not prevent application.
This illustrates the hierarchy of riwayah in the Hanafi school.

Source Text

ولو بنى في دار شراها شراء فاسداً لَزِمَهُ قيمتها ، وشَكٍّ أبو يوسف له فيها [١] قوله : ولو بنى...الخ ؛ يعني لو اشترى داراً شراء فاسداً فبناها ، لزم المشتري قيمة هذه الدار، وليس للبائع أن يأخذها ، وهذه المسألة رواها أبو يوسف له ثمَّ شَكٍّ بعد ذلك في سماعه إياها عن الإمام . [٢] قوله : لزمه قيمتها ؛ لأنَّ البناءَ مما يقصد به الدوام ؛ إذ البناء ليس للنقض، وإنما يفيد دليلاً للمسألة لا يرد عليه ما يرد على «الهداية»،
And if he builds in a house he bought with a corrupt purchase, he must pay its value, and Abu Yusuf had a doubt about hearing this from him. [1] His statement: 'And if he builds...etc.' means: If someone buys a house with a corrupt purchase and then builds on it, the buyer must pay the value of this house, and the seller does not have the right to take it. Abu Yusuf related this from him (Imam Abu Hanifa), but then later doubted whether he heard it from the Imam. [2] His statement: 'He must pay its value' because building is intended for permanence; indeed, building is not for demolition, and it only provides proof for the issue; nothing from al-Hidayah rebuts it.

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Abu Hanifa narrated by Abu Yusuf: If someone buys a house with a corrupt sale (bay' fasid) and then erects a building on it, the buyer must pay the value (qimat) of the house to the seller, and the seller cannot reclaim the house itself. The proof given is that building is intended for permanence (ad-dawam); it is not meant to be demolished. Therefore, the buyer's building indicates a lasting use, so the seller is compensated with the value instead of being able to undo the transaction. The passage also notes that Abu Yusuf initially narrated this ruling from Abu Hanifa but later doubted whether he actually heard it from him. This is a point of historical transmission (riwayah) affecting the authority of the ruling. The commentator adds that this ruling serves as a proof for the issue and is not contradicted by al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
شَكٍّ (shakk): Doubt; here Abu Yusuf's doubt about hearing the ruling from Abu Hanifa.
نوادر (nawadir): Rare transmissions; rulings that are not part of the zahir al-riwayah but are still considered valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the Hanafi school treat a ruling narrated by Abu Yusuf with his own doubt about hearing it?
A) The ruling is rejected as unreliable.
B) The ruling is accepted as part of zahir al-riwayah.
C) The ruling is accepted as nawadir and still applied.
D) The ruling is only accepted if corroborated by a mutawatir report.

Key Takeaway
Abu Yusuf's doubt about hearing the ruling does not invalidate it; the Hanafi school still applies it, acknowledging its lower transmission authority (nawadir).

Building on Property from a Fasid Sale: The Abu Hanifa-Sahibayn Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on building in a fasid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on building in a fasid sale according to Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, explain the talil behind each position, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Building is intended for permanence; hence, the buyer who builds on fasid property pays its value and keeps it, per Abu Hanifa.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses a key intra-Hanafi dispute: what happens when someone buys a house through an invalid sale (shira' fasid) and then builds on it? Abu Hanifa rules that the building stands and the buyer pays the value; the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say the building must be demolished. The passage also discusses Abu Yusuf's doubt about narrating this ruling from Abu Hanifa, and lists six similar cases where Abu Yusuf denied his own narrations, underscoring the school's rigorous transmission verification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is building on a house acquired via a fasid sale. According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling?
The buyer becomes liable for the value of the house (qimah) and keeps the property.
2
Locate the ikhtilaf
Who disagrees with Abu Hanifa? The Sahibayn. What is their ruling?
They hold that the building must be demolished and the house returned to the seller.
3
Analyze the dalil for each side
Abu Hanifa's reason: building is intended for permanence; the seller empowered the buyer. Sahibayn's reason: the sale is invalid, so ownership did not transfer properly.
The key phrase is 'al-bina' mimma yuqsad bihi al-dawam' – building is for permanence.
4
Understand Abu Yusuf's doubt
Abu Yusuf initially narrated this ruling from Abu Hanifa but later doubted its authenticity. Does this invalidate the ruling?
No; the ruling is still relied upon because it is from the zahir riwayah and supported by other transmissions.
5
Connect to the six masail
The passage lists six other issues where Abu Yusuf denied his narrations. How do these cases relate to the building dispute?
They all involve Abu Yusuf's caution in transmitting rulings from Abu Hanifa, highlighting the importance of verifying narration chains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع/ البيع الفاسد والمكروه
ولو بنى في دار شراها شراء فاسداً لزمه قيمتها، وشك أبو يوسف له فيها. هذا عند أبي حنيفة له. وإنما يفيد دليلاً للمسألة لا يرد عليه ما يرد على «الهداية». بين كلامي «الهداية» فالوجه ما قال في «العناية»: إنّما يستقيم على الروايةِ الصَّحيحة، وهي أنها لا تتعين لا على الأصح، وهو ما مرّ أنها تتعين في البيع الفاسد. انتهى.
ويمكن الدفع بوجه آخر وهو أنَّ المراد بالعقود العقود الصحيحة؛ لأنَّ المطلق ينصرف إلى الكامل، فحينئذ عدم التعيين سواء كان في المغصوب أو ثمن المبيع بالبيع الفاسد إنما هو في العقد الثاني، فلا يضرُّ تعينه في الأول، فعلى هذا ينبغي أن يكون جواب صاحب «العناية» بلا حصر. تدبّر.
وقال في «الفرائد»: كلام صدرِ الشَّريعةِ يفيد دفع التناقض؛ لأنَّ حاصل التناقض أنَّ صاحب «الهداية» قال فيما سبق: الثمنُ في البيع الفاسد يتعين بالتعيين، وفي هذه المسألة لا يتعيّن، وحاصلُ الدَّفع أنّ التعينَ بالتعيين في حالة قيام الثّمن، وعدم التعين في حالة عدمه، ولا يتحقق التناقض إلا إذا اتحد الجهتان. انتهى.
هذا وجه لكنّه خلاف ما صرحوا به؛ لأنهم قالوا: ثمَّ إن كانت دراهمُ الثَّمن قائمة يأخذها بعينها؛ لأنها تتعيّن بالتعيين على رواية أبي سليمان له، وهو الأصح، وفي رواية أبي حفص له لا تتعين. كما في «العناية» وغيرها، فبهذا علم أن هذا التوجية ليس بدافع، تدبّر. كذا في «مجمع الأنهر».
قوله: ولو بنى...الخ؛ يعني لو اشترى داراً شراء فاسداً فبناها، لزم المشتري قيمة هذه الدار، وليس للبائع أن يأخذها، وهذه المسألة رواها أبو يوسف له ثمَّ شَكٍّ بعد ذلك في سماعه إياها عن الإمام.
قوله: لزمه قيمتها؛ لأنَّ البناءَ مما يقصد به الدوام؛ إذ البناء ليس للنقض، وعندهما يُنقض البناء.
وهذه المسألة من المسائل التي أَنْكَرَ أبو يوسف روايتها عن أبي حنيفة له والمشتري يتضرر بنقض البناء بلا جائز، والبائع يتضرر مع جائز، مع أنه حصل بتسليطه، فكان رعاية حق المشتري أولى فينقطعُ للبائع حق الاسترداد، كما لو باعه المشتري بيعاً صحيحاً، بخلاف الشفيع؛ لأنه لم يحصل البناء بتسليط الشفيع. كذا في «الكفاية».
قوله: وعندهما ينتقض البناء، ويردُّ الدَّارَ على البائع؛ لأنَّ حق الشفيع أضعف؛ فلهذا يحتاج إلى الرّضاء أو القضاء، ولا يورث، بخلاف حق البائع؛ ولهذا نصوا على أنها ست مسائل، وذكرها ابن نجيم نقلاً عن السراج الهندي لكن لم يذكر فيها هذه المسألة، وهذه المسائل هي: الأولى: رجل صلى التطوع أربعاً وقرأ في إحدى الأوليين وإحدى الأخريين لا غير، روى محمد أنه يقضي أربعاً، وقال أبو يوسف: إنما رويت له ركعتين، واعتمد مشايخنا رواية محمد. والثانية: مستحاضة توضأت بعد طلوع الشمس تصلي حتى يخرج وقت الظهر، قال أبو يوسف: إنما رويت لك حتى يدخل وقت الظهر. والثالثة: المشتري من الغاصب إذا أعتق ثم أجاز المالك البيع نفد العتق، قال: إنما رويت لك أنه لا ينفد. والرابعة: المهاجرة لا عدة عليها ويجوز نكاحها إلا أن تكون حبلى فحينئذ لا يجوز نكاحها، قال: إنما رويت لك أنه يجوز نكاحها ولكن لا يقربها زوجها حتى تضع الحمل. والخامسة: عبد بين اثنين قتل مولى لهما فعفا أحدهما بطل الدم كله عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا: يدفع ربعه إلى شريكه أو يفديه بربع الدية، وقال أبو يوسف: إنما حكيت لك عن أبي حنيفة كقولنا وإنما الاختلاف الذي رويته في عبد قتل مولاه عمداً وله ابنان فعفا أحدهما إلا أن محمداً ذكر الاختلاف فيهما، وذكر قول نفسه مع أبي يوسف في الأولى. السادسة: رجل مات وترك ابنا له وعبداً لا غير فادّعى العبد أن الميت كان أعتقه في صحته، وادعى رجل على الميت ألف دينار وقيمة العبد ألف فقال الابن: صدقتما. يسعى العبد في قيمته وهو حر، ويأخذها الغريم بدينه، وقال أبو يوسف: إنما رويت لك ما دام يسعى في قيمته أنه عبد. ينظر: البحر الرائق (2): (65)، و(العناية (6): (103)، و«النافع الكبير»(ص 33).
Book of Sale / Invalid and Disliked Sale. If he built in a house he bought with an invalid purchase, its value becomes obligatory upon him, and Abu Yusuf doubted about it [narrating it from Abu Hanifa]. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And it only provides evidence for the issue, not objection against it as against Al-Hidayah. Between the statements of Al-Hidayah, the correct view is what Al-Inayah said: It is only correct on the sound narration, which is that it is not specified in the more correct view, and what passed is that it is specified in an invalid sale. End. And it can be refuted in another way: that by contracts is meant valid contracts, because the absolute is directed to the complete. Then non-specification whether in usurped property or the price of the sold item in an invalid sale is only in the second contract, so its specification in the first does not harm. According to this, the answer of the author of Al-Inayah should be without restriction. Reflect. And he said in Al-Fara'id: The statement of Sadr al-Shari'ah refutes the contradiction because the sum of the contradiction is that the author of Al-Hidayah said previously: The price in an invalid sale is specified by specification, and in this issue it is not specified. And the sum of the refutation is that specification by specification is when the price is outstanding, and non-specification is when it is not, and contradiction only occurs if the two aspects are the same. End. This is a view but it contradicts what they explicitly stated, because they said: Then if the dirhams of the price are still in existence, he takes them specifically because they are specified by specification according to the narration of Abu Sulayman from him, which is the more correct, and according to the narration of Abu Hafs from him, it is not specified. As in Al-Inayah and others. By this it is known that this direction is not a refutation. Reflect. Thus in Majma' al-Anhur. His saying: 'And if he built...' means if he bought a house with an invalid purchase and built on it, the buyer is liable for its value, and the seller does not have the right to take it. This issue was narrated by Abu Yusuf from him, then he doubted whether he heard it from the Imam. His saying: 'Its value becomes obligatory upon him' because building is intended for permanence; building is not for demolition. According to them (the two Sahibayn), the building is demolished. And this issue is among the issues that Abu Yusuf denied narrating from Abu Hanifa. The buyer is harmed by the demolition of the building without compensation, and the seller is harmed with compensation, while it occurred by his empowerment. So giving precedence to protecting the buyer's right is more appropriate, and the seller's right of recovery is cut off, just as if the buyer sold it in a valid sale, unlike the pre-emptor because the building did not occur by the empowerment of the pre-emptor. Thus in Al-Kifayah. His saying: 'According to them (the two Sahibayn), the building is demolished and the house is returned to the seller' because the right of the pre-emptor is weaker; for this reason it needs consent or judgment, and it is not inherited, unlike the right of the seller. For this reason they explicitly stated that they are six issues, mentioned by Ibn Nujaym quoting from Al-Siraj al-Hindi, but he did not include this issue among them. These issues are: First: A man prayed supererogatory four rak'ahs and recited in one of the first two and one of the last two only. Muhammad narrated that he should repeat four, and Abu Yusuf said: I only narrated to you two rak'ahs, and our scholars relied on Muhammad's narration. Second: A mustahadah (woman with chronic bleeding) performed wudu after sunrise and prayed until the time of zuhr came out. Abu Yusuf said: I only narrated to you until the time of zuhr entered. Third: The buyer from a usurper if he frees and then the owner validates the sale, the emancipation is effective. Abu Yusuf said: I only narrated to you that it is not effective. Fourth: An emigrant woman has no waiting period and her marriage is permissible unless she is pregnant, then her marriage is not permissible. Abu Yusuf said: I only narrated to you that her marriage is permissible but her husband should not approach her until she delivers. Fifth: A slave owned by two men killed their master, and one of them forgave; all blood-money is nullified according to Abu Hanifa, and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He pays a quarter to his partner or ransoms with a quarter of the blood-money. Abu Yusuf said: I only reported from Abu Hanifa like our statement; the disagreement I narrated is in the case of a slave who killed his master intentionally and he had two sons and one forgave, except that Muhammad mentioned the disagreement in both, and mentioned his own (Muhammad's) statement with Abu Yusuf in the first. Sixth: A man died leaving a son and a slave only, and the slave claimed that the deceased had freed him during his health, and a man claimed a thousand dinars from the deceased and the value of the slave was a thousand. The son said: You both are truthful. The slave works for his value while he is free, and the creditor takes it for his debt. Abu Yusuf said: I only narrated to you that as long as he works for his value, he is a slave. Reference: Al-Bahr al-Raiq (2:65), and Al-Inayah (6:103), and Al-Nafi' al-Kabir (p.33).
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage, from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah, treats a specific intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a fasad (invalid) sale and subsequent construction on the purchased property. The author begins by stating the ruling: if someone buys a house through an invalid sale (shira' fasid) and then builds on it, he must pay the value of the house (qimat al-dar) and the seller cannot reclaim the property. This is the position of Abu Hanifa, and it is narrated that Abu Yusuf himself transmitted this ruling from Abu Hanifa, but later doubted whether he actually heard it from him. The reason for Abu Hanifa's ruling is given: building is intended for permanence (al-bina' mimma yuqsad bihi al-dawam); it is not meant to be demolished. Therefore, the buyer's right is given priority over the seller's right to recover the property, protecting the buyer from loss. In contrast, the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the building must be demolished (yunaqqad al-bina') and the house returned to the seller. The passage then enters a detailed discussion about possible contradictions between this ruling and a previous statement in al-Hidayah regarding the specification of the price in an invalid sale. The commentator defends coherence by citing al-'Inayah and al-Fara'id, noting that the specification applies when the price remains in existence, whereas here the buyer has built, altering the situation. The author then explicitly identifies this case as one of six well-known masail (issues) in which Abu Yusuf disputed his own narration from Abu Hanifa. A list of all six is provided, covering diverse fiqh areas: salah for a supererogatory prayer, rulings for a mustahadah, freeing a slave after purchase from a usurper, marriage of a muhajirah, blood-money for a slave killed by his master, and a case involving a freed slave and debt. This enumeration serves to contextualize the present ruling and shows the scholarly rigor in verifying transmissions.

Key Terms
شراء فاسد (shira' fasid): An invalid purchase, where the contract is defective due to a missing condition or element, but the ownership transfer is not automatically void and may require rectification.
قيمة الدار (qimat al-dar): The monetary value of the house, assessed at the time of the ruling, which the buyer must pay to the seller to retain the property.
مسائل أنكر أبو يوسف روايتها (masail ankara Abu Yusuf riwayataha): Issues that Abu Yusuf denied having narrated from Abu Hanifa; he doubted the transmission of these specific rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling if someone buys a house through an invalid sale (shira' fasid) and then builds on it?
A) The building must be demolished and the house returned to the seller.
B) The buyer pays the value of the house and keeps it; the seller cannot reclaim.
C) The buyer must demolish the building and pay compensation.
D) The sale is void from the beginning, so both parties return to original state.

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, if the buyer builds on the property, Abu Hanifa holds that the building stands and the buyer pays the value; the Sahibayn require demolition, showing the tension between preserving the buyer's act and restoring the seller's right.

Demolishing the Buyer's Building in a Fasid Sale
Intra-Hanafi dispute over buyer's building demolition upon return of fasid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on demolishing a buyer's building in a fasid sale and state the mufta bihi position with its evidence.

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, the buyer must demolish any building erected — the seller's shari'i right to reclaim property overrides authorization.

This passage addresses a critical intra-Hanafi dispute: when a fasid sale is rescinded, must the buyer demolish a building he erected on the property? Abu Hanifa is reported to have held that the buyer may keep the building by paying its value, but Abu Yusuf denied that narration. The two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) held that demolition is required. Later Hanafi commentators, including al-Fath and Ibn Abidin, weighed in, and the relied-upon position is demolition. This lesson unpacks the arguments and the final tarjih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Pinpoint the two positions: Abu Hanifa's alleged view (keeping by paying value) vs. the two students' view (demolition). Note that Abu Yusuf denies the first narration.
The dispute is not between madhhabs but within the Hanafi school.
2
Examine the key proof
Understand the core argument for demolition: the seller's right of recovery (haqq al-istirdād) is a shari‘i right, not nullified by the seller's authorization to possess. Authorization does not extinguish a shari‘i right.
Compare to valid sale: there, authorization leads to ownership transfer, cutting off the right. In fasid sale, no ownership transfer.
3
Analyze the analogies
The text uses analogies to a valid sale to a third party (cuts off seller's right) and to pre-emption (shuf‘a) where authorization does not cut off the pre-emptor's right. Distinguish each analogy.
Valid sale transfers ownership; building does not. Shuf‘a is a weaker right, not inherited, and the pre-emptor does not authorize the building.
4
Follow the commentary chain
Trace how al-Fath (in Fath al-Qadir) preferred the two students' view, al-Nahr objected, and Ibn Abidin responded. Note the shurrah's refinements.
Al-Nahr argued that building with authorization is intended for permanence, distinguishing it from lease. Ibn Abidin distinguished the seller's authorization (which can lead to sale) from the lessor's authorization (time-bound).
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is demolition (position of the two students). The seller's right is inherited and requires no court order, unlike shuf‘a.
Practical consequence: buyer loses investment; seller gets property in its original state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندهما يُنقض البناء. وهذه المسألة من المسائل () التي أَنْكَرَ أبو يوسف روايتها عن أبي حنيفة له والمشتري يتضرر بنقض البناء بلا [؟جائز؟] والبائع يتضرر مع [؟جائز؟] مع أنه حصل بتسليطه، فكان رعاية حق المشتري أولى فينقطع للبائع حق الاسترداد، كما لو باعه المشتري بيعاً صحيحاً، بخلاف الشفيع لأنه لم يحصل البناء بتسليط الشفيع. كذا في «الكفاية». فإن أبا يوسف له قال لمحمد الله : ما رويت لك عن أبي حنيفة له أنه يأخذها بقيمتها، بل رويت أنه ينقض البناء ثبت حق البائع في الاسترداد من غير قضاء ولا رضاء المشتري، ويورث هذا الحق، ولا يورث حق الشفعة، وكذا حق البائع انضم إليه حق الشرع حتى يجب عليهما نقض هذا البيع، ولا يجب على الشفيع الأخذ بالشفعة ثم ينقض بناء المشتري لحق الشفيع، فلحق البائع أولى كذا في «الكفاية». ورجح في «الفتح» مذهبهما، حيث قال: «قولهما أوجه، وكون البناء يقصد به الدوام يمنع للاتفاق في الإجارة على إيجاب القلع، فظهر أنه قد يراد للبقاء، وقد لا. فإن قيل : إن المستأجر يعلم أنه يكلّف القلع ففعله مع ذلك دليل على أنه لم يرد البقاء. قلنا : المشترى فاسداً أيضاً يكلّف القلع عندنا». انتهى وتعقبه في «النهر» حيث قال: «أقول : البناء الحاصل بتسليط البائع إنما يقصد به الدوام بخلاف الإجارة، وبهذا عرف أن محط الاستدلال إنما هو التسليط من البائع، وكل ما هو كذلك ينقطع به حق الاسترداد». انتهى. قلت : وفيه أن المؤجر أيضاً سلط المستأجر على الانتفاع بأرضه، والمستأجر يملك البناء، فالأحسن الجواب بالفرق بين التسليطين بأن البائع سلطه على المبيع على وجه قد ينقطع به حق الاسترداد بأن يخرجه عن ملكه ببيع ونحوها، أو بأن يفعل فيه ما يقصد به الدوام ; الجواز أن لا يطلب البائع الفسخ قبله، بخلاف المؤجر فإنه إنما سلطه في وقت خاص. وأما كون الفسخ حقاً للشرع فلا يبطل بتسليط البائع فينقض بأنه قد بطل بإخراجه عن ملكه ببيع ونحوه، وهو بتسليط البائع، فكذا هاهنا تقديماً لحق العبد لفقره، وكون البيع ونحوه تعلق به حق الغير فيقدم وهاهنا تعلق به حق العاقد العاصي فلا
And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the building is demolished. And this issue is among the issues that Abu Yusuf denied narrating from Abu Hanifa. The buyer is harmed by the demolition of the building without [consent?] and the seller is harmed with [consent?], even though it occurred by his authorization. So observing the buyer's right is more appropriate, thus the seller's right of recovery is cut off, just as if the buyer sold it in a valid sale, unlike the pre-emptor because the building did not occur by the authorization of the pre-emptor. Thus in al-Kifayah. For Abu Yusuf said to Muhammad: I did not narrate to you from Abu Hanifa that he takes it by its value, but I narrated that he demolishes the building. The seller's right of recovery is established without a court order or the buyer's consent, and this right is inherited, whereas the right of pre-emption is not inherited. Likewise, the seller's right is joined by the right of the Law such that both must annul this sale, whereas the pre-emptor is not obligated to take by pre-emption and then demolish the buyer's building for the pre-emptor's right. So the seller's right is more worthy. Thus in al-Kifayah. And in al-Fath he preferred their view, saying: 'Their view is more appropriate, and the fact that the building is intended for permanence prevents (the analogy) because of consensus regarding lease on obligating removal, so it appears that sometimes it is intended for permanence and sometimes not. If it is said: The lessee knows that he will be required to remove, yet he does it; this indicates that he did not intend permanence. We say: The buyer in a fasid sale is also required to remove according to us.' End. And al-Nahr commented on it, saying: 'I say: The building arising from the seller's authorization is intended for permanence, unlike lease, and by this it is known that the focus of the inference is the authorization from the seller, and everything like that cuts off the right of recovery.' End. I (Ibn Abidin) say: In it (the above) that the lessor also authorized the lessee to benefit from his land, and the lessee owns the building. So the best answer is to differentiate between the two authorizations: that the seller authorized him over the sold item in a way that may cut off the right of recovery, such as by his taking it out of ownership by sale or similar, or by his doing something in it intended for permanence, because it is permissible that the seller does not seek rescission before that, unlike the lessor who only authorized him for a specific time. And as for the rescission being a right of the Law, it is not nullified by the seller's authorization, because it is nullified by taking it out of his ownership by sale etc., and that is by the seller's authorization. So here too, giving precedence to the slave's right due to his poverty, and the sale etc. has the right of another attached to it, so it is given precedence, while here the right of the sinful contracting party is attached, so (it is not given precedence)...
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a buyer who constructs a building on property acquired through a fasid (invalid) sale must demolish the building when the sale is rescinded. The text first states the position of the two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) that the building must be demolished. It then notes that this issue is among those where Abu Yusuf denied narrating the opposing view from Abu Hanifa. The arguments revolve around whether the seller's authorization to the buyer to take possession (and thereby build) cuts off the seller's right to reclaim the property in its original state. The text draws an analogy to a valid sale to a third party, which does cut off the seller's right, and distinguishes the pre-emptor (shafi‘) who does not authorize the building. Abu Yusuf insists that the only narration he knows is that the building must be demolished. Later commentators like al-Fath and al-Nahr weigh in, with al-Fath preferring the demolition view (the students' position) because building is normally intended for permanence, unlike lease where removal is anticipated. Ibn Abidin then offers his own analysis, arguing that the key difference is that the seller's authorization can lead to the property being disposed of in a way that ends his right, whereas a lessor's authorization is time-limited. The overall relied-upon position is that the building must be demolished, as the seller's right is a shari‘i right that cannot be nullified by mere authorization.

Key Terms
نقض البناء (naqd al-binā'): Demolition of the building erected by the buyer on property sold through a fasid sale when the sale is rescinded.
حق الاسترداد (haqq al-istirdād): The seller's right to recover the property from the buyer when a fasid sale is rescinded.
شفعة (shuf‘a): Pre-emption: the right of a co-owner or neighbour to purchase property sold to an outsider.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position in this passage, what happens to a building erected by a buyer on property from a fasid sale when the seller reclaims the property?
A) The buyer may keep the building by paying its value.
B) The buyer must demolish the building at his own expense.
C) The seller must pay the buyer for the building.
D) The building becomes the property of the seller without compensation.

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, the buyer's building is always demolished upon rescission, even if authorized, because the seller's right to recover the property in its original state is a shari‘i right that authorization cannot defeat.

Effect of Buyer's Building on Voidable Sale: Dispute Between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute over whether building cuts off seller's right to reclaim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether the buyer's building cuts off the seller's right to reclaim in a voidable sale, and identify the proof for each position.

Core Idea
Building by the buyer may cut off the seller's right to reclaim, but only if intended for permanence—unless the Law requires otherwise.

This lesson dissects a nuanced debate within the Hanafi school: when a buyer builds on property acquired through a voidable sale, does the seller lose the right to demand demolition? Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) differ. Understanding their proofs—including analogies to pre-emption, lease, and the case of a usurper—will sharpen your ability to apply usul principles to specific contract disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The dispute centers on whether the seller's right to reclaim (istirdad) in a voidable sale is extinguished by the buyer's act of building. Examine the legal question precisely: does the building create a new right that overrides the seller's right?
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa holds that the seller's right is cut off; the seller can only claim the value, not demolish. His proof relies on the seller's authorization (taslit) and analogy to pre-emption (shuf'a) and the case of a usurper who builds a foundation.
Key reference: Book of Pre-emption where shuf'a is established when the seller's right is cut off.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
The Sahibayn maintain that the seller's right remains; the building must be demolished. Their proof invokes the right of the Law (haqq al-shar') joining the seller's right, and analogy to lease where demolition is required despite authorization.
The Sahibayn argue that since both parties are required to dissolve the sale, the building cannot stand.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response distinguishing lease from sale
The commentator (Ibn Abidin) distinguishes the two authorizations: in sale, the buyer's ownership is open-ended and the buyer could resell, so permanence is intended; in lease, authorization is time-limited. Also, a transgressor's right is not invalidated by the Law, as shown by the usurper example.
This step highlights the use of istihsan and qiyas in the Hanafi method.
5
Determine the practical consequence and mufta bihi
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Abu Hanifa, seller gets value only; under Sahibayn, seller can demolish. The later authorities differ on tarjih: al-Fath al-Qadir prefers Sahibayn, while Radd al-Muhtar defends Abu Hanifa. No definitive mufta bihi is given in this passage.
This illustrates that even within a school, some disputes may remain unresolved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أبا يوسف رحمه الله قال لمحمد رحمه الله: ما رويت لك عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله أنه يأخذها بقيمتها، بل رويت أنه ينقض البناء. ثبت حق البائع في الاسترداد من غير قضاء ولا رضاء المشتري، ويورث هذا الحق، ولا يورث حق الشفعة، وكذا حق البائع انضم إليه حق الشرع حتى يجب عليهما نقض هذا البيع، ولا يجب على الشفيع الأخذ بالشفعة ثم ينقض بناء المشتري لحق الشفيع، فلحق البائع أولى. كذا في «الكفاية». ورجح في «الفتح» مذهبهما، حيث قال: «قولهما أوجه، وكون البناء يقصد به الدوام يمنع للاتفاق في الإجارة على إيجاب القلع، فظهر أنه قد يراد للبقاء، وقد لا. فإن قيل: إن المستأجر يعلم أنه يكلّف القلع ففعله مع ذلك دليل على أنه لم يرد البقاء. قلنا: المشترى فاسداً أيضاً يكلّف القلع عندنا». انتهى. وتعقبه في «النهر» حيث قال: «أقول: البناء الحاصل بتسليط البائع إنما يقصد به الدوام بخلاف الإجارة، وبهذا عرف أن محط الاستدلال إنما هو التسليط من البائع، وكل ما هو كذلك ينقطع به حق الاسترداد». انتهى. قلت: وفيه أن المؤجر أيضاً سلط المستأجر على الانتفاع بأرضه، والمستأجر يملك البناء، فالأحسن الجواب بالفرق بين التسليطين بأن البائع سلطه على المبيع على وجه قد ينقطع به حق الاسترداد بأن يخرجه عن ملكه ببيع ونحوه، أو بأن يفعل فيه ما يقصد به الدوام؛ لجواز أن لا يطلب البائع الفسخ قبله، بخلاف المؤجر فإنه إنما سلطه في وقت خاص. وأما كون الفسخ حقاً للشرع فلا يبطل بتسليط البائع فينقض بأنه قد بطل بإخراجه عن ملكه ببيع ونحوه، وهو بتسليط البائع، فكذا هاهنا تقديماً لحق العبد لفقره، وكون البيع ونحوه تعلق به حق الغير فيقدم وهاهنا تعلق به حق العاقد العاصي فلا يقدم، وقد يمنع بأن العاصي لم يبطل الشرع حقه، كمن غصب حجراً وجعله أس حائطه، يضمن قيمته، ولا يكلف بنقض الحائط. كذا في «رد المحتار». وقال محمد: بل رويت الأخذ بالقيمة لكن نسيت. فشك أبو يوسف في روايته عن أبي حنيفة، ومحمد لم يرجع عن ذلك، وحمله على نسيان أبي يوسف، فإنه ذكر في كتاب الشفعة: إن المشتري إن اشترى شراء فاسداً إذا بنى فيها فللشفيع الشفعة عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما لا شفعة له، فهذا يدل على انقطاع حق البائع ببناء المشتري عند أبي حنيفة خلافاً لهما. قوله: فشك؛ أي في أنه هل سمع هذه الرواية عن الإمام أم لا، ولم يشك في الاختلاف. قوله: فهذا يدل... الخ؛ إذ ثبوت حق الشفعة مبني على انقطاع حق البائع في الاسترداد، فيكون نصاً على الاختلاف في انقطاع حق البائع بالبناء؛ لأن التنصيص على الاختلاف في الفرع يكون تنصيصاً على الاختلاف في أصل ذلك الفرع.
Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, said to Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him: 'I did not narrate to you from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, that he takes it by its value; rather, I narrated that he demolishes the building. The seller's right of reclamation is established without a court order or the buyer's consent, and this right is inherited, while the right of pre-emption is not inherited. Similarly, the seller's right is joined by the right of the Law such that both (the seller and buyer) must demolish this sale, while the pre-emptor is not required to take by pre-emption and then demolish the buyer's building for the pre-emptor's right; so the seller's right is more prior.' Thus in al-Kifaya. And in al-Fath, he preferred the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), saying: 'Their statement is more appropriate, and that building is intended for permanence prevents (the seller's right being cut off), as agreed regarding lease that demolition is required. So it appears that building may be intended for permanence or not. If it is said: The lessee knows he will be required to demolish, yet he does it; that indicates he did not intend permanence. We say: The buyer in a voidable sale is also required to demolish according to us.' End. And al-Nahr criticized him, saying: 'I say: The building that occurs by the seller's authorization is intended for permanence, unlike lease. By this it is known that the core of inference is the authorization from the seller; and everything that is so, the right of reclamation is cut off by it.' End. I (Ibn Abidin) say: But the lessor also authorized the lessee to benefit from his land, and the lessee owns the building. So the best answer is to distinguish between the two authorizations: The seller authorized him over the sold property in a manner that may cut off the right of reclamation—by him transferring it out of his ownership through sale or the like, or by doing in it that which is intended for permanence; because it is possible that the seller does not demand rescission before that. Unlike the lessor, for he only authorized him for a specific time. As for rescission being a right of the Law, it is not invalidated by the seller's authorization. It is refuted that it has been invalidated by him taking it out of his ownership through sale and the like, and that is by the seller's authorization. So here too, giving precedence to the right of the slave due to his poverty, and that sale and the like have a third-party right attached so it takes precedence; while here it has attached the right of the contracting party who is a transgressor, so it does not take precedence. It may be prevented that the transgressor's right is not invalidated by the Law, like one who usurps a stone and makes it the base of his wall; he guarantees its value and is not required to demolish the wall. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar. And Muhammad said: 'Rather, I narrated the taking by value but I forget.' So Abu Yusuf doubted his narration from Abu Hanifa, and Muhammad did not retract that, and attributed it to Abu Yusuf's forgetfulness. He mentioned in the Book of Pre-emption: If the buyer buys by a voidable purchase and builds, the pre-emptor has pre-emption according to Abu Hanifa, while according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) he has no pre-emption. This indicates that the seller's right is cut off by the buyer's building according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two. His statement: 'so he doubted'; i.e., as to whether he heard this narration from the Imam or not, and he did not doubt the disagreement. His statement: 'this indicates...'; since the establishment of the right of pre-emption is based on the seller's right of reclamation being cut off, so it is a text on the disagreement regarding the cutting off of the seller's right by building; because specifying the disagreement in the branch is specifying the disagreement in the root of that branch.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of a buyer's construction (building) on a voidable sale (bay' fasid). The core issue is whether the seller's right to reclaim the property (istirdad) is cut off by the buyer's act of building. Abu Hanifa holds that the right is indeed cut off; the seller can no longer demand demolition but is entitled to the value of the property. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) maintain that the seller's right remains, and the building must be demolished. The dispute extends to related matters: inheritance of the right of reclamation vs. pre-emption (shuf'a), and the role of the seller's authorization (taslit). The text first reports Abu Yusuf's statement refuting a narration from Abu Hanifa that the seller takes the value; rather, Abu Yusuf insists the building must be demolished. The author then cites al-Kifaya, which establishes that the seller's right is joined by a right of the Law (haqq al-shar'), making the sale obligatory to dissolve. Al-Fath al-Qadir prefers the Sahibayn's view, arguing that building is intended for permanence, unlike in lease. However, al-Nahr refutes this by noting that the building was authorized by the seller, so it is intended for permanence, cutting off the seller's right. The commentator (Ibn Abidin) then distinguishes between the seller's authorization and a lessor's authorization in lease. He further argues that a transgressor's right (the buyer in a voidable sale) is not invalidated by the Law, analogizing to a usurper who uses a stone as a foundation. The passage then recounts Muhammad's statement that he did narrate the value-based ruling but forgot, causing Abu Yusuf to doubt his own narration. This is supported by a cross-reference to the Book of Pre-emption (Kitab al-Shuf'a), where Abu Hanifa's ruling on pre-emption in a voidable sale implies that the seller's right is cut off by building. The marginal commentary clarifies that the disagreement in the branch (shuf'a) confirms the disagreement in the root (istirdad). This lesson illustrates the nuanced reasoning within the Hanafi school, employing analogy (qiyas) and istihsan to determine the operative ruling.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay‘ al-fāsid): A sale that is fundamentally valid in contract formation but has a deficiency in its description or consideration, rendering it voidable rather than void (batil).
الشفعة (al-shuf‘a): The right of a co-owner or neighbor to pre-empt the sale of property by taking possession of the sold share from the buyer upon paying the price.
الاسترداد (al-istirdād): The right of the seller in a voidable sale to reclaim the sold property from the buyer, typically upon rescission of the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the effect of the buyer building on a voidable sale property?
A) The seller can still demand demolition of the building and reclaim the property.
B) The seller's right to reclaim is cut off; he can only claim the value of the property.
C) The building must be demolished regardless of the seller's claim.
D) The buyer automatically becomes the owner of the property by building.

Key Takeaway
In a voidable sale, the buyer's building can cut off the seller's right to reclaim only if the building is intended for permanence and authorized by the seller—but this is disputed within the Hanafi school between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Building in a Fasid Sale and Pre-emption: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on building and pre-emption in fasid sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether a buyer's building in a fasid sale cuts off the seller's right, and explain the practical consequence for pre-emption.

Core Idea
For Abu Hanifa, building cuts off seller's right; for Sahibayn, it does not.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute that hinges on a subtle point: does a buyer's act of construction on a defectively purchased property terminate the original seller's right to reclaim it? The answer determines whether a pre-emptor can step in. We will unpack the positions of Abu Hanifa and his two principal students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and examine the reasoning behind each view, including a notable objection using the case of a usurper.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Read the passage and determine the precise legal issue: whether the seller's right to reclaim a property sold through a fasid sale is cut off by the buyer's building. Note that this directly affects the pre-emptor's right.
The dispute is not about the validity of the building itself, but about its effect on existing rights.
2
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
From the text, Abu Hanifa holds that the building cuts off the seller's right, so the pre-emptor can take the property by paying its value. Confirm with the reference to Kitab al-Shuf'a.
The text calls this a 'text' (nass) indicating a clear ruling.
3
State the Sahibayn's opposing view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the seller's right is not cut off, therefore no pre-emption exists. Note that Muhammad's own recollection of a narration is doubted by Abu Yusuf.
The text clarifies that the doubt was about the authenticity of the narration, not the legal ruling.
4
Analyze the objection based on the usurper analogy
The passage raises an objection: even a wrongdoer (the builder in a fasid sale, who may be a usurper) does not have his right nullified by the law. The example of a usurper who builds with a stolen stone is given: he must pay compensation but is not forced to demolish.
This objection appears to challenge Abu Hanifa's position, but the text does not resolve it, leaving the dispute open.
5
Draw the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In a real case, the applicable view determines the outcome: under Abu Hanifa, the pre-emptor can claim the property; under the Sahibayn, he cannot. Understand that both views are legitimate within the Hanafi school.
Later Hanafi fatwa works may incline towards one view, but this passage only presents the difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال محمد : بل رويت الأخذ بالقيمة لكن نسيت. فشك أبو يوسف له في روايته عن أبي حنيفة له ، ومحمد الله لم يرجع عن ذلك، وحمله على نسيان أبي يوسف له ، فَإِنَّهُ ذَكَرَ في كتاب الشفعة : إن المشتري إن اشترى شراء فاسداً إذا بنى فيها فللشفيع الشفعة عند أبي حنيفة لله، وعندهما لا شفعة له، فهذا يدل على انقطاع حق البائع ببناء المشتري عند أبي حنيفة له خلافاً لهما . يقدم، وقد يمنع بأن العاصي لم يبطل الشَّرعُ حقه ، كمَن غصب حجراً وجعله أس حائطه، يضمن قيمته، ولا يكلف بنقض الحائط. كذا في رد المحتار). [١]قوله : فشك ؛ أي في أنه هل سمع هذه الرواية عن الإمام أم لا ، ولم يشك في الاختلاف. [٢] قوله : فهذا يدل ... الخ ؛ إذ ثبوت حق الشفعة مبني على انقطاع حق البائع في الاسترداد، فيكون نصاً على الاختلاف في انقطاع حق البائع بالبناء ؛ لأنَّ التنصيص على الاختلاف في الفرع يكون تنصيصاً على الاختلاف في أصل ذلك الفرع.
And Muhammad said: Rather, I reported taking the value but forgot. So Abu Yusuf doubted [this] in his narration from Abu Hanifa. And Muhammad did not retract from that, and interpreted Abu Yusuf's doubt as forgetfulness. Indeed, he mentioned in the Book of Pre-emption: If the buyer purchased with a fasid purchase and then built on it, the pre-emptor has pre-emption according to Abu Hanifa, and according to the two, no pre-emption for him. So this indicates that the seller's right is cut off by the buyer's building according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two. And it is mentioned, and it may be prevented by the fact that the wrongdoer's right is not nullified by the law, like one who usurps a stone and makes it the foundation of a wall: he guarantees its value and is not required to demolish the wall. As in Radd al-Muhtar. [1] His statement: 'so he doubted' means whether he heard this narration from the Imam or not, but he did not doubt the difference. [2] His statement: 'so this indicates...' because the establishment of pre-emption is based on the cutting off of the seller's right to reclaim, so it is a text on the difference regarding the cutting off of the seller's right with building; because specifying the difference in a branch is specifying the difference in the root.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of a buyer's construction on a property purchased through a fasid (defective) sale. The core issue is whether the buyer's act of building cuts off the original seller's right to reclaim the property. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani reports a narration stating that he himself related the opinion that the pre-emptor takes the property by paying the value, but then forgot. Abu Yusuf doubted this report, but Muhammad did not retract. The text then cites a rule from Kitab al-Shuf'a: if a buyer purchases through a fasid sale and builds, then according to Abu Hanifa the pre-emptor still has the right of pre-emption, whereas according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahabayn), there is no pre-emption. This indicates that Abu Hanifa views the seller's right as cut off by the building, while the Sahabayn do not. However, a potential objection is noted: the Shari'ah does not nullify the right of a wrongdoer (the builder), as in the case of a usurper who builds with usurped material—he pays compensation but is not forced to demolish. This is cited from Radd al-Muhtar. The commentary further clarifies that the doubt of Abu Yusuf was about the authenticity of the narration, not about the legal difference itself.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'a): The right of pre-emption: a co-owner or neighbor has the right to purchase a sold property at the agreed price.
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fasid): A defective sale: a sale that is voidable due to a missing condition or attribute, e.g., selling something not in possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the position of Abu Hanifa regarding the seller's right when a buyer builds on property purchased through a fasid sale?
A) The seller's right is cut off.
B) The seller's right remains intact.
C) The seller's right is transferred to the pre-emptor.
D) The building makes the sale valid.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on whether building in a fasid sale cuts off the seller's right to reclaim, affecting the pre-emptor's right.

Disliked Actions in Sales: Najsh and Bidding Over Another
Ruling on najsh and al-sawm with hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on najsh and al-sawm, apply the exception for below-market pricing, and distinguish between a disliked (makruh) and a prohibited (haram) action in sales.

Core Idea
Najsh and bidding over another's bid are disliked due to deception, but not haram; an exception exists when the commodity is sold below its value.

This lesson explores two closely related disliked actions in Islamic commercial law: najsh (artificially inflating prices by bidding without intention to buy) and al-sawm (offering a higher bid after another person has already made an offer). The source passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the ruling, the underlying hadith evidence, and an important exception when the item is sold below its fair market value. Understanding these rulings helps you navigate ethical sales and avoid practices that harm fellow Muslims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two actions
Locate the two actions explicitly stated as disliked: najsh and al-sawm (bidding over another's bid). Note that the text also mentions a similar rule for marriage proposals.
Al-sawm refers specifically to raising the price while the first bidder is still negotiating.
2
Analyze the definition of najsh
Read the definition: bidding on an item without intending to buy, solely to trick another into paying more. Understand the linguistic root (stirring up game).
The Persian gloss clarifies the meaning.
3
Examine the evidence (dalil)
The hadith 'لا تناجشوا' (Do not practice najsh) is cited from Bukhari and Muslim. In Hanafi usul, such a prohibition (nahy) indicates karahah (disliked), not tahrim (prohibition), because the evidence is zanni (ahad hadith).
This highlights the importance of epistemology in deriving rulings.
4
Apply the exception
The text notes that if the commodity is sold below its market value, najsh is not disliked; rather, it becomes praiseworthy because it removes deception (the seller is being cheated).
This exception is based on istihsan (juristic preference) to prevent harm.
5
Understand al-sawm
Al-sawm (bidding over another's bid) is also disliked. The same ruling applies in marriage (proposing over another's proposal). The reason is to avoid creating conflict and harming the first party.
The text simply states it is disliked without further detail, implying a general prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيما يكره (وكُرِهَ النَّجَسُ ، نَجْشُ الصَّيدِ : بسكون الجيم: إثارته، والنجش جاء بفتح الجيم وسكونه : وهو أن يساوم سلعة لا يريد شراءها بأكثر من قيمتها ليرى الآخر فيقع فيه. (والسوم [١] قوله : وكره النجش ؛ لما فرغ المصنف له عن البيع الفاسد، وكان المكروه أدنى درجة منه لكنّه شعبة من شعبه ؛ لأنَّ القبح إذا كان لأمر مجاور كان مكروهاً، وإذا كان لوصف متصل كان فاسداً، فألحق مباحث المكروه لمباحث الفاسد مؤخراً، فقال: وكره النجش ، وإنّما كره ذلك لنهيه لله عنه ، حيث قال: «لا تناجشوا») : أي لا تفعلوا النجش ، أخرجه البخاري ومسلم من حديث أبي هريرة له عنه. وسبب ذلك : إيقاعُ رجلٍ فيه بأزيد من ثمن ، وهو خداع، والخداع قبيح جاور البيع، وكان مكروها. كذا في «العناية» (۲) ، وهذه الكراهة إذا كانت السلعة بلغت قيمتها، أما إذا لم تبلغ لا يكره لانتفاء الخداع، بل ذكر القُهُسْتانِيُّ (۳) ، وابنُ الكمال (٤) عن «شرح الطحاوي»: إنّه في هذه الصورة محمود والنجش : برانگیختن صيد ، وناجش : انكرر وبرماند صيدي راو جيزي كدنه خري بزيادت باخواستن تادبكري درافتد. كذا في «الصراح» و«المنتخب». [٢] قوله : جاء...الخ ؛ وقال في «الكفاية»: «هو بفتحتين، ويروى بالسكون: هو أن تستام السلعة بأزيد من ثمنها، ولا تريد شراءها، بل ليراك الآخر فيقع فيها ، وكذا في النكاح وغيره». انتهى [٣] قوله : والسوم ؛ أي يكره السوم على سوم غيره، وكذا البيع على بيع غيره
Chapter on what is disliked. (And najsh is disliked, najsh of the hunt: with sukun on the jim: stirring it up, and najsh with fatha on the jim and sukun: it is that one bargains for an item without intending to buy it for more than its value so that another sees and falls into it. And al-sawm. [1] His statement: 'and najsh is disliked': when the author finished with defective sales, and the disliked is a lower degree than it but it is a branch of it; because ugliness if for a surrounding matter is disliked, and if for a connected attribute is defective, so he attached the topics of the disliked to the topics of the defective as a sequel, and said: 'and najsh is disliked'. Indeed, it is disliked because of the prohibition from Allah, where He said: 'Do not practice najsh' meaning do not do najsh. It was related by Bukhari and Muslim from the hadith of Abu Hurairah. The reason for that is causing a man to fall into it for more than the price, and that is deception, and deception is ugly and surrounds the sale, and it is disliked. As in al-Inayah. This dislike is when the commodity has reached its value, but if it has not reached [its value] it is not disliked due to absence of deception. Rather, al-Quhustani and Ibn al-Kamal mentioned from Sharh al-Tahawi that in this case it is praiseworthy. And najsh: stirring up the hunt, and najash: to... [Persian gloss]. [2] His statement: 'came' etc. And in al-Kifayah: 'It is with two fathas, and narrated with sukun: it is that you bargain for an item for more than its price, not intending to buy it, but so that another sees you and falls into it. And similarly in marriage and other matters.' End. [3] His statement: 'and al-sawm' meaning the bidding over another's bid is disliked, and likewise the sale over another's sale.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on disliked actions in sales. The author lists two: najsh and al-sawm. Najsh literarily means stirring up game, but legally it refers to a person bidding on an item without intending to buy, simply to inflate the price and trick another into buying at a higher price. It is disliked because the Prophet explicitly forbade it in a sahih hadith. The commentator explains that najsh is a form of deception (khida'), and since it surrounds the sale, it renders the action makruh, not fasid. However, an exception is noted: if the item is being sold below its fair market value, then bidding it up to its proper value is actually praiseworthy, as it prevents the seller from being cheated. The ruling applies similarly in marriage contracts where someone may propose after another's proposal. The second disliked action is al-sawm, meaning bidding over the bid of a fellow Muslim, i.e., to offer a higher price while the first bidder has not yet finalized his purchase. This is also prohibited and disliked.

Key Terms
النَّجَش (al-najash): Artificial price inflation by bidding without intention to buy, to trick another into paying more.
السَّوْم (al-sawm): The act of bargaining; here specifically bidding over another's bid.
الخداع (al-khida'): Deception; the characteristic that makes najsh disliked.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on najsh when the commodity is sold below its market value?
A) It is still makruh tanzih.
B) It is haram.
C) It is not disliked; rather, it is praiseworthy.
D) It becomes fasid (defective).

Key Takeaway
Najsh and al-sawm are disliked due to deception and harming others, but the ruling changes when the commodity is sold below its value, making najsh praiseworthy to restore fairness.

Dislike of Bidding Over Another's Bid and Meeting Caravans
Rulings on two related disliked transactions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions that make bidding over another's bid and meeting caravans disliked (makruh tahrimi) and identify the proof from hadith.

Core Idea
Bidding after agreement and intercepting caravans are disliked because they harm others and violate hadith.

This lesson examines two common market practices that the Hanafi school rules as disliked (makruh tahrimi). While the underlying sale remains valid, the acts themselves are discouraged due to the harm they cause to individuals or the public. The proof comes from a sahih hadith. Understanding the precise conditions under which these acts become disliked is essential for proper fiqh application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two actions
The passage covers two separate disliked actions: (1) bidding over the bid of another (al-sawm 'ala sawm al-ghayr) and (2) going out to meet trade caravans before they reach the city (talaqqi al-jalb).
Each has its own conditions.
2
Examine the proof
The proof is the hadith 'Let none of you sell over the sale of another' (Bukhari and Muslim). Note that the hadith explicitly mentions sale, but the Hanafi school extends it to bidding by analogy, since the harm is similar.
The hadith uses 'brother' but the ruling applies to all, including non-Muslims.
3
Determine the condition for the first action
Bidding over another's bid is disliked only when the seller and a buyer have already agreed on a price. If no agreement has been reached, it is permissible and considered a legitimate auction (bai' al-yazid).
The condition is explicitly stated by the commentator: 'if they have agreed on a price'.
4
Determine the condition for the second action
Meeting caravans is disliked when the caravan is close to the city such that the public's right to purchase the goods at market price attaches. Intercepting them deprives the public and causes harm, especially in times of scarcity.
The example given: a man buys the entire stock from a caravan during a drought then resells at inflated prices.
5
Apply the ruling
Both acts are valid (sahih) contracts but carry the legal classification of makruh tahrimi due to the prohibited harm. The dislike is not in the contract itself but in the conduct.
This distinction is important: the sale is not invalid, but the act of bidding after agreement or intercepting is sinful if done with knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣]قوله : والسوم ; أي يكره السوم على سوم غيره، وكذا البيع على بيع غيره؛ على سَوْمٍ غيره إذا رضيا بثمن وتلقي الجلب المضرّ بأهل البلد)، الجَلَبُ المجلوب، فإنَّ المجلوب إذا قَرُبَ من البلدِ تعلّق به حق العامة، فيكره أن يستقبل البعض ويشتريه، ويمنع العامة عن شرائه لما نهى رسول الله عنه كما أخرجه البخاري ومسلم من حديث ابن عمر له قال : «لا يبع بعضكم على بيع بعض) ، وفي لفظ : «لا يبع الرجلُ على بيع (بعض)، ولا يخطب على خطبة أخيه إلا أن يأذن له». وصورة السوم : أن يستامَ الرَّجلان على السلعة، والبائع والمشتري رضيا بذلك العقد، ولم يعقد عقد البيع حتى دخل آخر على سومه، فإنّه يجوز لكن يكره لاشتماله على الإيحاش والإضرار وهما قبيحان ينفكان عن البيع، وكان مكروها إذا جنح البائعُ إلى البيع بما طلب الأوَّلُ من الثمن، وكذلك في النكاح. [1] قوله : على سوم غيره ; ولو ذميَّاً أو مستأمناً، وذكر الأخ في الحديث ليس قيداً بل لزيادة التنصير؛ لأنَّ السوم على السوم يوجب إيحاشاً وإضراراً، وهو في حق الأخ أشدُّ منعاً ، كما في قوله ل في الغيبة : ذكرك أخاك (۳) ؛ إذ لا خفاء في منع غيبة الذمي، والتفصيل في «النهر». [٢] قوله : إذا رضيا بثمن ; قيَّدَهُ به ؛ لأنه إذا ساومه ولم يركن أحدهما إلى الآخر فلا بأس، فإن هذا بيع من يزيد كما سيأتي. [۳] قوله : الجلب المجلوب ; جلب الشيء جاء به من بلد إلى بلد للتجارة، والجلب هم الذين يجلبون النعم من موضع إلى موضع للبيع ، وقد يتوسع فيه فيطلق على من يجلب الأقوات إلى البلدان، ولعله مصدر لغت به كذا في «شرح البرْجَندِي». [٤]قوله : فيكره أن يستقبل البعض...الخ ; وصورته على ما ذكره الإسبيجابي : إِنَّ واحداً من أهل المصرِ أُخبر بمجيء قافلةٍ عظيمة ، وأهلُ المصر في قحط وجدب، فتلقى ذلك الواحد القافلة واشترى منهم جميع ما يمتازون، ودخل المصر وباعه على ما يريدُ
[3] He said: 'And al-sawm' – that is, it is disliked to bid over the bid of another, and similarly to sell over the sale of another; over the bid of another if they have agreed on a price, and meeting the caravans that harm the people of the city. The jalb (caravan) is the goods brought; indeed, when the brought goods are close to the city, the right of the public attaches to them, so it is disliked to meet them and buy from them, and to prevent the public from buying them, because the Messenger of Allah prohibited it, as reported by Bukhari and Muslim from the hadith of Ibn Umar, who said: 'Let none of you sell over the sale of another' and in another wording: 'Let not a man sell over the sale of his brother, nor propose over his proposal, unless he gives him permission.' The image of al-sawm: that two men are bidding on the commodity, and the seller and buyer have agreed on that contract, but have not concluded the contract of sale until another comes and bids over his bid; then it is permissible but disliked because it contains causing discomfort and harm, and they are two ugly things that are separate from the sale, and it is disliked when the seller inclines to sell at the price the first asked. Similarly in marriage. [1] He said: 'over the bid of another' – even if he is a dhimmi or a musta'min, and mentioning 'brother' in the hadith is not a restriction but rather for emphasizing notification, because bidding over a bid causes discomfort and harm, and it is more severely prohibited in the case of a brother, as in His saying in backbiting: 'Mentioning your brother' – there is no doubt in the prohibition of backbiting a dhimmi; the detail is in al-Nahr. [2] He said: 'if they have agreed on a price' – he restricted it with this, because if he bids and one of them does not incline to the other, then there is no harm, for this is a sale by bidding which will come later. [3] He said: 'al-jalb al-majlub' – jalb of a thing means bringing it from one country to another for trade, and al-jalb are those who bring livestock from one place to another for sale, and it may be extended to apply to those who bring provisions to the cities; perhaps it is a verbal noun used in this way, as in Sharh al-Barjandi. [4] He said: 'it is disliked to meet some...' – its image as mentioned by al-Isbijabi: a man from the city is informed of the arrival of a large caravan while the people of the city are in drought and barrenness, so he meets the caravan and buys all that they have, enters the city and sells it at whatever price he wishes.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage covers two related actions: bidding over another's bid (al-sawm 'ala sawm al-ghayr) and meeting trade caravans (talaqqi al-jalb). Both are ruled as disliked (makruh). The proof is the hadith 'Let none of you sell over the sale of another' reported by Bukhari and Muslim. For the first action, the dislike is conditional: it only applies when the seller and a buyer have already agreed on a price, even if the contract is not yet concluded. If no agreement has been reached, it is permissible and considered a legitimate auction (bai' al-yazid). The commentator further notes that the prohibition applies equally to Muslims and non-Muslims (dhimmi, musta'min), and the term 'brother' in the hadith is not restrictive. For the second action, meeting caravans is disliked when the caravan is close to the city because the public's right to purchase the goods at market price attaches. Intercepting them harms the public by depriving them of the goods and raising prices. The specific example given is of a person who buys the entire stock from a caravan during a drought and then sells it at whatever price he wishes, which is clearly harmful to the public.

Key Terms
السَّوْم على سَوْم غيره (al-sawm 'ala sawm al-ghayr): Bidding on an item after another person has already made an offer and the seller has accepted.
تَلَقِّي الجَلَب (talaqqi al-jalb): Going out to meet incoming trade caravans before they reach the city to buy their goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is it disliked to bid over another's bid?
A) Always, regardless of circumstances.
B) Only when the seller and buyer have agreed on a price.
C) Only if the first bidder is a Muslim.
D) Only if the commodity is food.

Key Takeaway
Bidding after agreement and meeting caravans near the city are disliked (makruh tahrimi) due to harm, but the contracts themselves remain valid.

Ruling on Intercepting Incoming Goods (Talqi al-Jalab)
Ruling on talqi al-jalab with proof and conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on talqi al-jalab and identify the conditions that make it makruh tahrimi versus permissible.

Core Idea
Intercepting incoming goods is not haram but makruh tahrimi when it harms the public or deceives on price.

This lesson addresses a common market practice: meeting arriving caravans to buy goods before they reach town. While the contract itself remains valid, the act may be disliked because of its potential harm to the community. We will study the textual evidence and the conditions that determine the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is talqi al-jalab? What is the hukm (permissible, disliked, forbidden) in the Hanafi school?
Focus on the distinction between the act and the contract.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the hadith cited by the author. What does it prohibit? Is the prohibition absolute or conditioned?
Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Analyse the Hanafi understanding of the nahy
How does Hanafi usul interpret the imperative prohibition (nahy)? Does it indicate haram or makruh tahrimi? Why?
Hanafi: nahy indicates karahah tahrimiyyah, not haram, due to the contract being valid.
4
Identify the conditions for karahah
The text states it is disliked only when it causes harm (mudarrah) or deception (talbis al-sir). Note these conditions and their opposites.
If no harm or deception, the act is permissible.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Compare Hanafi position (makruh tahrimi) with Shafi'i position (haram). What practical difference does this make?
Hanafi: sale valid; Shafi'i: sale invalid? (Check fiqh).

على سَوْمٍ غيره إذا رضيا بثمن وتلقي الجلب المضرّ بأهل البلد على سوم غيره إذا رضيا بثمن" ، وتلقي الجلب المضرّ بأهل البلد)، الجَلَبُ المجلوب"، فإنَّ المجلوب إذا قَرُبَ من البلدِ تعلّق به حق العامة، فيكره أن يستقبل البعض ويشتريه، ويمنع العامة عن شرائه لما نهى رسول الله عنه كما أخرجه البخاري ومسلم من حديث ابن عمر له قال : «لا يبع بعضكم على بيع بعض» ، وفي لفظ : «لا يبع الرجلُ على بيع أخيه، ولا يخطب على خطبة أخيه إلا أن يأذن له». وصورة السوم : أن يستامَ الرَّجلان على السلعة، والبائع والمشتري رضيا بذلك العقد، ولم يعقد عقد البيع حتى دخل آخر على سومه، فإنّه يجوز لكن يكره لاشتماله على الإيحاش والإضرار وهما قبيحان ينفكان عن البيع، وكان مكروها إذا جنح البائعُ إلى البيع بما طلب الأوَّلُ من الثمن، وكذلك في النكاح.

[1] قوله : على سوم غيره ; ولو ذميَّاً أو مستأمناً، وذكر الأخ في الحديث ليس قيداً بل لزيادة التنصير; لأنَّ السوم على السوم يوجب إيحاشاً وإضراراً، وهو في حق الأخ أشدُّ منعاً ، كما في قوله في الغيبة : ذكرك أخاك ; إذ لا خفاء في منع غيبة الذمي، والتفصيل في «النهر».

[٢] قوله : إذا رضيا بثمن ; قيَّدَهُ به ; لأنه إذا ساومه ولم يركن أحدهما إلى الآخر فلا بأس، فإن هذا بيع من يزيد كما سيأتي.

[۳] قوله : الجلب المجلوب ; جلب الشيء جاء به من بلد إلى بلد للتجارة، والجلب هم الذين يجلبون النعم من موضع إلى موضع للبيع ، وقد يتوسع فيه فيطلق على من يجلب الأقوات إلى البلدان، ولعله مصدر لغت به كذا في «شرح البرْجَندِي».

[٤] قوله : فيكره أن يستقبل البعض...الخ ; وصورته على ما ذكره الإسبيجابي : إِنَّ واحداً من أهل المصرِ أُخبر بمجيء قافلةٍ عظيمة ، وأهلُ المصر في قحط وجدب، فتلقى ذلك الواحد القافلة واشترى منهم جميع ما يمتازون، ودخل المصر وباعه على ما يريدُ وهذا إنما يُكْرَهُ إذا كان مضراً بأهل البلد ، وقد سمعت أبياتاً لطيفة لمولانا برهان الإسلام به فكتبتها أحماضاً، وهي :
أبو بكر الولد المنتخب أراد الخروج لأمرٍ عَجَب
فقال إني عزمت الخروج لكفتارة هي لي
فقلت : ألم تسمعن يا بني بنهي أَتَى عن تلقي الجلب
[1] قوله : إذا كان مضراً بأهل البلد ; أو يلبس السعر على الواردين; لعدم علمهم به، فيكره للضرر في الأوّل، وللغرر في الثاني، وإذا انعدم الغرر والتلبيس فلا يكره.
On bidding on top of another's bid when they have agreed on a price, and intercepting incoming goods that harm the townspeople... The jalab are the goods brought in. When the goods come near the town, the public right attaches to them. It is disliked for someone to go out to meet them and buy them, preventing the public from buying, because the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) prohibited it as recorded by Bukhari and Muslim from Ibn Umar: 'Let none of you bid against another's bid,' and in another wording: 'Let not a man bid against his brother's bid, nor propose marriage against his brother's proposal unless he permits.' The form of bidding is that two men negotiate on the same item, and the buyer and seller are satisfied with that contract, but have not yet concluded the sale when another enters on top of their bidding. In such a case, it is permissible but disliked because it involves causing aversion and harm, both of which are reprehensible and separate from the sale. It is disliked when the seller inclines to sell at the price the first agreed upon, and similarly in marriage.

[Commentary on the terms and conditions.]
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling on intercepting incoming goods (talqi al-jalab) and bidding on top of another's bid (al-sawm 'ala sawm al-ghayr). The author states that when goods approach a town, a public right attaches to them, making it disliked (makruh) for an individual to meet the goods outside the town and buy them all, thereby preventing townspeople from purchasing. The proof is the hadith prohibiting outbidding another's bid. The commentary clarifies that the prohibition applies even if the other party is a non-Muslim or a musta'min, and the word 'brother' in the hadith is not restrictive but emphasizes the stronger prohibition among Muslims. The ruling is conditioned on the parties having agreed on a price; if no agreement is reached, there is no problem. The form of talqi al-jalab is when someone from the town, knowing of a large caravan arriving during a drought, intercepts and buys all the goods, then sells them at a high price. This is disliked only if it harms the townspeople or involves deception (gharar) by concealing market prices. The poem by Burhan al-Islam reinforces the prohibition. The Hanafi position is that the act is unlawfully disliked (makruh tahrimi) when harmful, but becomes permissible when no harm or deception occurs.

Key Terms
السَّوْم (al-sawm): Negotiation or bidding on a price for a commodity
الجَلَب (al-jalab): Goods brought from one country to another for trade
تَلَقِّي الجَلَب (talqi al-jalab): Going out to meet incoming caravans to buy goods before they reach the market

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in the passage, when is intercepting incoming goods (talqi al-jalab) disliked (makruh)?
A) In all circumstances
B) Only when it causes harm to the townspeople or involves deception in pricing
C) Only if the goods are food items
D) Only if the interceptor is a town resident

Key Takeaway
Talqi al-jalab is not inherently forbidden; its ruling depends on whether it causes harm or deception in the marketplace.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
