Ruling on Townsman Selling for Bedouin
Explains ruling on townsman selling for bedouin with conditions and hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a townsman selling for a bedouin, explain the condition of harm, and apply the hadith evidence to distinguish when it is makruh tahrimi versus permissible.

Core Idea
Selling for a bedouin to inflate prices is makruh tahrimi only if it harms townspeople or involves deception.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah discusses the ruling on a townsman (hadir) selling goods on behalf of a bedouin (badi) to obtain a higher price, especially in times of scarcity. The Hanafi school does not consider it absolutely prohibited; rather, it is disliked (makruh tahrimi) when it causes harm to townspeople or involves deception (gharar). The primary evidence is the Prophet's prohibition recorded by Bukhari. This lesson will clarify the conditions that trigger the disliked ruling and when the act is permissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its two forms
Read the passage to recognize the two described scenarios: (1) the bedouin entrusts goods to a townsman to sell at inflated prices; (2) a townsman meets the bedouin at the town entrance and buys the goods cheaply while the bedouin is unaware of market prices.
The second scenario involves gharar (deception) because the bedouin is ignorant of the current price.
2
Determine the default ruling and key condition
The passage states that the act is disliked (yukrah) in times of hardship if the townspeople are harmed. If no harm occurs, it is not disliked. Note that the ruling is conditioned on harm or gharar.
Some scholars (mentioned in the commentary) hold that it is disliked even without harm due to the absolute prohibition, but the author conditions on harm.
3
Locate the dalil and classify it
The author cites the hadith: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited a townsman from selling for a bedouin.' This is a hadith ahad (zanni al-thubut) from Sahih al-Bukhari.
In Hanafi usul, a hadith ahad can establish a wajib or makruh tahrimi but not a fard.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof yields the ruling)
The Prophet's prohibition (nahy) indicates that the act is prohibited. According to Hanafi usul, nahy al-tahrimi makes the act haram but if there is a counter-indication (like the sale being valid), it is reduced to makruh tahrimi. Here, the sale itself is valid (sahih) but the act of intermediation is disliked.
The Hanafi school thus rules it makruh tahrimi (forbidden to the level of being sinful) rather than haram.
5
Note the ikhtilaf (differences within and across schools)
Intra-Hanafi: Some scholars (e.g., al-Isbijabi) consider it makruh tanzihi if no harm. The mufta bihi is makruh tahrimi when harmful. Cross-madhhab: The Shafi'i school generally holds it as haram due to the absolute prohibition.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that Shafi'is would forbid the act even without harm, while Hanafis permit it when no harm or deception exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيع الحاضرِ للبادي طمعاً في الثَّمن الغالي زمان القحط)، صورته : أن البادي يَجْلِبُ الطعام إلى البلدِ فيطرحه على رجل يسكن البلد ليبيع من أهل البلد بثمن غال، فهذا يُكْرَهُ" في أيام العسرة . من الثمن ، ولو تركهم فأدخلوا ميرتهم بأنفسهم ، وباعوها من أهل المصر متصرفة توسع أهل المصر بذلك، فإذا كان الأمر بذلك فهو مكروه، وإن كان أهل المصر لا يتضررون بذلك فلا يكره وقال بعضهم : صورته أن يتلقاها رجلٌ من أهل المصر فيشتري منهم بأرخص من سعر المصر، وهم لا يعلمون سعر المصر، فالشراء جائز في الحكم، ولكنه مكروه؛ لأنه غرر سواء استضر به أهل المصر أو لم يستضرُّوا به).

[ ١ ] قوله : إذا كان مضراً بأهل البلد ; أو يلبس السعر على الواردين; لعدم علمهم به، فيكره للضرر في الأوّل، وللغرر في الثاني، وإذا انعدم الغرر والتلبيس فلا يكره.

(٢) قوله : صورته...الخ ; وقال بعضُهم صورته: أنَّ الرَّجل إذا كان له طعام وأهل المصر في قحط وهو لا يبيعه من أهل المصر حتى يتوسعوا، ولكن يبيعه من أهل البادية بثمن غال، وأهل المصر يتضررون فلا يجوز ، وإذا كانوا لا يتضررون بذلك فلا بأس ببيعه منهم ، وإلى هذه الصورة ذهب صاحب «الهداية»(۲).

[۳] قوله : فهذا يكره ; لما أخرجه البخاري عن ابن عمر له أنه قال : «نهى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أن يبيع حاضر لباد (۳) ; ولأن فيه إضراراً بأهل البلد وهو قبيح جدا، أما إذا
And the sale of a townsman for a bedouin out of greed for a high price during a time of drought: its form is that the bedouin brings food to the town and entrusts it to a resident to sell to townspeople at a high price. This is disliked in hard times. If they leave the bedouins to bring in their own goods and sell them, expanding the townspeople, then it is disliked. If the townspeople are not harmed, it is not disliked. Some said its form is that a townsman meets them and buys cheaper than the market price while they are unaware, making the purchase permissible in ruling but disliked due to gharar, whether harming townspeople or not.

[Commentary on conditions and hadith evidence.]
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a townsman selling goods on behalf of a bedouin (badawi) for the purpose of raising prices, especially in times of scarcity. The author describes two scenarios: (1) The bedouin brings food and hires a townsman to sell at inflated prices; this is disliked (makruh) if it harms the townspeople. (2) Some scholars describe it as a townsman buying the goods cheaply from the bedouin who are unaware of the market price; this involves gharar (deception) and is therefore makruh regardless of harm. The commentary emphasizes that the karahah is conditioned on harm or deception. The proof is the hadith from Bukhari: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited a townsman from selling for a bedouin.' The Hanafi position is that it is makruh tahrimi when harmful, but permissible if no harm or deception occurs. The passage ends abruptly but covers the essential ruling.

Key Terms
الحاضر (al-hadir): A townsman, a permanent resident of the town or city
البادي (al-badi): A bedouin or villager who comes to town to sell produce
العَسْرَة (al-‘usrah): Hard times, drought, scarcity of food

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The bedouin is from a distant village.
B) The sale occurs during a time of drought.
C) The act harms the townspeople or involves deception.
D) The townsman sells at a loss.

Key Takeaway
A townsman selling for a bedouin is makruh tahrimi when it causes harm to townspeople or involves deception; otherwise it is permissible.

Ruling on Town Dweller Selling for Bedouin During Famine
Expounding the ruling on a town dweller selling for a bedouin during famine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a town dweller selling for a bedouin during famine and apply the condition of harm to determine whether the sale is disliked.

Core Idea
A town dweller selling for a bedouin is only disliked if it harms townspeople or involves deception.

In times of scarcity, market transactions can easily veer into exploitation. This passage from al-Hidayah explores a specific scenario: a town dweller acting as an agent for a bedouin who brings food to town. The ruling hinges on whether the townspeople are harmed or deceived. Let's unpack the legal reasoning and see how the Hanafi school applies the hadith prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the disputed act
Recognize that the act is a town dweller selling goods on behalf of a bedouin, especially during famine or hardship. Note the two mentioned scenarios: (1) the bedouin entrusts goods to the town dweller to sell at a high price, (2) the town dweller buys from the bedouin at a cheaper price while the bedouin is ignorant of the market.
2
Extract the ruling from the text
The text states the sale is 'disliked' (makruh) under specific conditions. Determine the hukm level: it is makruh tanzihi, not haram, because the evidence is ahad and the harm is conditional.
The Hanafi principle: a prohibition from a hadith ahad indicates karahah (dislike) unless supported by qat'i evidence for haram.
3
Identify the condition that changes the ruling
The ruling depends on whether the townspeople are harmed or there is deception (gharar). If no harm and no deception, the sale is not disliked. 'توسعوا' (they become spacious/plenty) indicates when townspeople have other resources, the harm is absent.
The condition is harm or deception; both trigger karahah. The text also mentions a second opinion that the sale is always makruh due to gharar, but the relied-upon position is conditional.
4
Examine the dalil (hadith) and understand its application
The proof is the hadith narrated by Ibn Umar: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade a town dweller from selling for a bedouin.' The Hanafi school interprets this as indicating dislike, not absolute prohibition, because the illa (effective cause) is harm to townspeople. When the illa is absent, the ruling does not apply.
This is a clear example of takhsis al-illah — restricting the general ruling to its effective cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيع الحاضرِ للبادي طمعاً في الثَّمن الغالي زمان القحط)، صورته : أن البادي يَجْلِبُ الطعام إلى البلدِ فيطرحه على رجل يسكن البلد ليبيع من أهل البلد بثمن غال، فهذا يُكْرَهُ" في أيام العسرة . من الثمن ، ولو تركهم فأدخلوا ميرتهم بأنفسهم ، وباعوها من أهل المصر متصرفة توسع أهل المصر بذلك، فإذا كان الأمر بذلك فهو مكروه، وإن كان أهل المصر لا يتضررون بذلك فلا يكره وقال بعضهم : صورته أن يتلقاها رجلٌ من أهل المصر فيشتري منهم بأرخص من سعر المصر، وهم لا يعلمون سعر المصر، فالشراء جائز في الحكم، ولكنه مكروه؛ لأنه غرر سواء استضر به أهل المصر أو لم يستضرُّوا به).
[ ١ ] قوله : إذا كان مضراً بأهل البلد ؛ أو يلبس السعر على الواردين؛ لعدم علمهم به، فيكره للضرر في الأوّل، وللغرر في الثاني، وإذا انعدم الغرر والتلبيس فلا يكره.
(٢) قوله : صورته...الخ ؛ وقال بعضُهم صورته: أنَّ الرَّجل إذا كان له طعام وأهل المصر في قحط وهو لا يبيعه من أهل المصر حتى يتوسعوا، ولكن يبيعه من أهل البادية بثمن غال، وأهل المصر يتضررون فلا يجوز ، وإذا كانوا لا يتضررون بذلك فلا بأس ببيعه منهم ، وإلى هذه الصورة ذهب صاحب «الهداية»(۲).
[۳] قوله : فهذا يكره ؛ لما أخرجه البخاري عن ابن عمر له أنه قال : «نهى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أن يبيع حاضر لباد (۳) ؛ ولأن فيه إضراراً بأهل البلد وهو قبيح جدا، أما إذا كانوا في سعة فلا بأس به كما صرحوا به.
And the sale of the town dweller for the bedouin, seeking the high price during the time of famine. Its form: that the bedouin brings food to the town and entrusts it to a man residing in the town to sell it to the townspeople at a high price. This is disliked during times of hardship. If they leave them to bring their own provisions themselves and sell them to the townspeople, thereby easing the townspeople, then if the matter is such, it is disliked. And if the townspeople are not harmed by that, it is not disliked. And some said: its form is that a man from the townspeople meets them and buys from them cheaper than the town price, while they are unaware of the town price. The purchase is legally valid but disliked because it involves deception, whether the townspeople are harmed by it or not. [1] His saying: 'if it is harmful to the townspeople' or it confuses the price for the arrivals due to their ignorance of it, then it is disliked for harm in the first and for deception in the second. And if deception and confusion are absent, it is not disliked. (2) His saying: 'its form...' And some said its form: that a man has food and the townspeople are in famine, and he does not sell it to the townspeople until they become desperate, but sells it to the bedouins at a high price, and the townspeople are harmed, then it is not permissible. And if they are not harmed, there is no harm in selling to them. The author of al-Hidayah adopted this form. [3] His saying: 'this is disliked' because al-Bukhari narrated from Ibn Umar that he said: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade a town dweller from selling for a bedouin'; and because it involves harm to the townspeople, which is extremely reprehensible. However, if they are in plenty, there is no harm in it, as they explicitly stated.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, explaining the legal status of a town dweller selling goods on behalf of a bedouin during a famine. The author first describes the scenario: a bedouin brings food to town and entrusts it to a town dweller to sell at a high price. This is makruh (disliked) in times of hardship if it harms the townspeople. The key factor is harm: if the townspeople are not harmed, the practice is not disliked. Then a second opinion is presented: the practice is makruh due to deception (gharar) because the bedouin is unaware of the prevailing market price, making the transaction valid but disliked. The author clarifies that the ruling hinges on harm or deception. He then cites the hadith from Sahih al-Bukhari: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade a town dweller from selling for a bedouin.' The rationale is protecting the townspeople from price manipulation and ensuring fair trade. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that it is makruh tanzihan when harmful or deceptive; otherwise, it is permissible.

Key Terms
بيع الحاضر للبادي (bay' al-hadhir lil-badi): A town dweller selling goods on behalf of a bedouin (or to a bedouin) in a way that harms townspeople or involves deception.
غرر (gharar): Deception or uncertainty in a transaction, here referring to the bedouin's ignorance of the town price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the sale when the townspeople are not harmed and there is no deception?
A) Makruh
B) Haram
C) Permissible
D) Void

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of a town dweller selling for a bedouin is conditional on harm; without harm, the sale is not disliked.

Sale During Friday Adhan: The Role of Ta'lil
Explaining the ruling on sale during Friday adhan with Quranic proof and the principle of ta'lil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sale during the Friday adhan and explain how the principle of ta'lil affects the ruling.

Core Idea
A sale during Friday adhan is only disliked if it distracts; walking sales are exempt because the cause is absent.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the Friday adhan ruling from the Quranic verse 'leave trade'. The author clarifies that not all sales are prohibited; the key is whether the sale interferes with the obligation to hasten to prayer. This lesson will help you understand how the Hanafi school uses 'illa (effective cause) to derive nuanced rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and primary ruling
What is being ruled upon (sale during Friday adhan) and what is the basic hukm according to the text? The text says it is makruh due to adjacency to neglect of sa'y.
The initial ruling is established by the Quranic command to leave trade.
2
Locate the Quranic proof
Find the dalil: Surah al-Jumu'ah 62:9, the phrase 'fa-s'aw ila dhikr Allah wa dharu al-bay'. This is the basis.
Thubut is qat'i, but dalala is zanni due to interpretation.
3
Analyze the difference in understanding the proof
Some scholars take the verse literally, applying the prohibition to all sales. Others, like the Hanafis, consider the underlying cause ('illa).
The passage contrasts the two approaches.
4
Apply the principle of ta'lil
Identify the 'illa: neglect of the obligation to hasten to prayer. If the 'illa is absent (e.g., walking sales), the ruling changes.
This is a key Hanafi usul principle.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The preferred Hanafi position: no dislike when walking; dislike when causing delay. This is supported by texts like al-'Inayah, al-Nihayah, and al-Kifayah.
Some Hanafis hold a stricter view, but the ta'lil-based view is relied upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبيع عند أذان الجمعة، وتفريق صغير (والبيع عند أذان الجمعة، وتفريق صغير
[١] قوله : والبيع ... الخ ؛ للقبح المجاور، وهو الإخلال بالسعي الواجب الثابت بقوله : فَاسْعَوْا إِلَى ذِكْرِ اللَّهِ وَذَرُوا البَيْعَ ) ، فلا بأس به إذا تبايعوا وهما يمشيان، صرح به في «العناية»(۲) و«النهاية» و«الكفاية»(۳). قال بعضهم : إنه مكروه في هذه الصورة أيضاً، وهذا إنما يتأتى على القول بأن : النصوص غير معللة، أما على القول أنها معللة فلا كراهة، فإنَّ علةَ النَّهي الاشتغال عن السعي، فإذا لم يوجد بأن يتبايعا ماشيين انتفت العلة، فينتفي المعلول، وهاهنا مباحث شريفة تركناها ؛ لغرابة المقام، والمراد بالأذان هو الأذان الأول إذا كان بعد الزوال.
And the sale at the Friday adhan, and separating young [siblings]. [1] His saying: 'the sale...' etc.; because of the adjacent ugliness, which is the neglect of the obligatory sa'y (haste to prayer) established by His saying: 'So hasten to the remembrance of Allah and leave trade.' So there is no harm if they trade while walking, as stated in al-'Inayah, al-Nihayah, and al-Kifayah. Some said: it is disliked in this form as well, but this follows the view that texts are not based on effective causes (ta'lil). As for the view that they are based on effective causes, there is no dislike, because the cause of the prohibition is distraction from sa'y. When it is absent—as when they trade while walking—the cause is absent, so the effect is absent. There are noble investigations here which we left due to the rarity of the context. And what is meant by the adhan is the first adhan when it is after noon.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding sales conducted during the Friday prayer adhan. The author begins by noting that sale during the adhan is considered disliked due to its adjacency to the forbidden act of neglecting the obligatory Friday prayer. The Quranic verse (62:9) commands believers to 'hasten to the remembrance of Allah and leave trade.' However, if the transaction occurs while both parties are walking to the mosque (i.e., not delaying), it is not disliked, as clarified in major commentaries. The author then presents a scholastic debate: some scholars hold that even walking sales are disliked if they interpret the textual prohibition literally without considering the 'illa (effective cause). But the preferred view is that the prohibition is based on the cause of distraction from prayer; when the cause is absent, there is no dislike. This illustrates the Hanafi principle of ta'lil (rationalizing legal rulings). The adhan referred to is the first adhan after noon (zawal).

Key Terms
أذان الجمعة (adhan al-jumu'ah): The first call to Friday prayer made after noon (zawal).
تعليل (ta'lil): The principle that legal rulings are based on effective causes ('illal), allowing flexibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the sale is for necessities.
B) When the buyer and seller are walking to the mosque.
C) When the adhan is the second adhan.
D) When the sale is conducted after prayer.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on sales during Friday adhan hinges on the presence of distraction; walking sales are permissible because the 'illa is absent.

Separating Young Mahram Slaves: Ruling and Proof
Ruling with proof on separating young mahram slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on separating young mahram slaves, identify the two hadith proofs, and explain why the ruling is makruh rather than haram.

Core Idea
Separating a young child from a mahram relative is makruh, not haram, due to ahad hadiths that indicate dislike.

In Hanafi fiqh, the sale of young slaves is generally permissible. However, when two slaves are mahram relatives (e.g., siblings or mother and child), separating them by sale or gift raises a moral concern. This lesson examines the ruling derived from two authentic hadiths and the reasoning behind classifying it as makruh tanzih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Subject
The act is 'tafriq' (separating) two slaves who are young and mahram relatives. The scenario arises when a person comes to own two such slaves through gift, bequest, purchase, or inheritance.
Mahram here means a relative whom one cannot marry, such as mother, sister, daughter.
2
State the Ruling
The Hanafi ruling is that such separation is makruh tanzih (disliked but not prohibited). This applies even if one slave is young and the other is old, as long as they are mahram.
Makruh tanzih means the act is disliked but not sinful; avoiding it is rewarded but doing it is not punishable.
3
Locate the Proof (Dalil)
Two ahad hadiths are cited: (1) from Abu Ayyub al-Ansari: 'Whoever separates a mother from her child, Allah will separate him from his loved ones on the Day of Judgment'; (2) from Ali: the Prophet commanded him to retrieve a sold young brother slave.
These hadiths are found in Sunan al-Tirmidhi and Ibn Majah.
4
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The first hadith contains a clear threat (wa'id), normally indicating prohibition, but Hanafis interpret it as karahah because the threat is not necessarily for haram – it could be for makruh. The second hadith shows a command to undo the sale, implying the act is contrary to the Sunnah but not necessarily invalid.
Hanafi usul: a command to return does not automatically make the sale invalid; it only indicates dislike.
5
Note the Ikhtilaf
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may consider such separation haram based on the same hadiths. The Hanafi position is that the hadiths are ahad and fall short of establishing absolute prohibition.
The practical difference: in Hanafi, the sale is valid but disliked; in Shafi'i, the sale might be invalid (fasid).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتفريق صغير (٢) قوله : تفريق صغير ؛ صورتهُ أنَّ من ملك بقبول الهبة أو الوصية أو الشراء أو الإرث مملوكين صغيرين أحدهما ذو رحم محرم من الآخر، فالتفريق بينهما مكروه، وكذلك إن كان أحدهما صغيراً والآخرُ كبيراً، لما أخرجه الترمذي عن أبي أيوب الأنصاري له أنه : قال رسول الله : «مَن فرق بين والدة وولدها، فرَّقَ الله بينه وبين أحبته يوم القيامة). ولما أخرجه الترمذي وابن ماجة عن علي له من أنه وهبَ النَّبي الله العلي ـ غلامين أخوين صغيرين، ثم قال : ما فعل الغلامان ؟ فقال : بعت أحدهما، فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «أدرك أدرك» ، وفي رواية: «اردد اردد)، ففي الأول : وعيد صريح من فرق صغيراً عن ذي رحم محرم ، وفي الثاني : تكراراً لأمر بالادراك والرد.
And separating a young [sibling]. (2) His saying: 'separating a young [sibling]' — its form: that a person comes to own, by accepting a gift, bequest, purchase, or inheritance, two young slaves who are mahram relatives of each other. Separating them is disliked. Similarly, if one is young and the other is old. Because al-Tirmidhi narrated from Abu Ayyub al-Ansari that the Messenger of Allah said: 'Whoever separates a mother from her child, Allah will separate him from his loved ones on the Day of Judgment.' And because al-Tirmidhi and Ibn Majah narrated from Ali that he gave the Prophet two young brother slaves, then he asked: 'What happened to the two boys?' He replied: 'I sold one of them.' The Prophet said: 'Go and fetch him, go and fetch him' — in another narration: 'Return him, return him.' In the first hadith, there is an explicit threat for one who separates a young [child] from a mahram relative. In the second, a repeated command to fetch and return.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the legal category of separating young siblings or any young mahram relatives (including mother-offspring). The author clarifies that the scenario applies when a person acquires ownership of two young slaves who are mahram relatives. The ruling is that separating them (by sale or gift) is makruh (disliked). This applies even if one is young and the other is old. The proof is from two hadiths: first, from Abu Ayyub al-Ansari, in which the Prophet threatens divine separation for those who separate a mother from her child. Second, from Ali, where the Prophet commands him to retrieve a sold young brother slave. The first hadith contains an explicit threat (wa'id), indicating the gravity of the act. The second shows the Prophet's command to undo the separation, implying it is contrary to the Sunnah. The ruling is that such separation is makruh tanzihi, not haram, because the hadiths indicate dislike rather than absolute prohibition.

Key Terms
تفريق (tafriq): The act of separating two individuals, here specifically young mahram relatives by sale or gift.
ذو رحم محرم (dhu rahim mahram): A relative who is within the prohibited degrees of marriage (e.g., mother, sister, daughter).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is haram and the sale is invalid.
B) It is makruh (disliked).
C) It is permissible if both consent.
D) It is wajib to separate them.

Key Takeaway
Separating young mahram relatives by sale or gift is makruh tanzih, not haram, and the sale remains valid though morally discouraged due to ahad hadiths that indicate dislike.

Selling One of Young Mahram Siblings: Valid but Disliked
Intra-Hanafi ruling dispute on validity of separating young mahram siblings by sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling one of two mahram minors, identify the intra-madhhab dispute, and apply the principle that a prohibition for an external reason does not invalidate the contract.

Core Idea
Selling one mahram minor from the other is valid but makruh; the prohibition targets the separation, not the sale itself.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah discusses a specific scenario: selling one of two minor slaves who are blood relatives (mahram). The scholars agree it is disliked, but they differ on whether the sale is valid. Understanding why the sale remains valid despite the prohibition is crucial for distinguishing between intrinsic and extrinsic prohibitions in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act is selling one of two minor mahram slaves. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, it is makruh but the sale is valid. Note that Abu Yusuf holds it invalid.
2
Examine the Dalil
The passage cites two hadith: (1) a general threat against separating a mother and child, and (2) the specific incident of 'Ali selling one of two young brothers given to the Prophet, and the Prophet ordering retrieval.
Both hadith are from Sunan al-Tirmidhi and Ibn Majah.
3
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Abu Yusuf argues the Prophet's command to retrieve implies the sale was ineffective. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad respond that the sale's pillars are sound; the command was for moral correction, not nullification. The prohibition is for an external reason (the resulting separation), not an internal defect.
The key is the distinction between prohibition for an intrinsic defect vs. an external consequence.
4
Apply the Hanafi Principle
When a prohibition is for an external reason (ma'nā mujāwir), the contract remains valid. This is analogous to the prohibition of sale at Friday adhan, which does not invalidate the contract.
Compare with the principle: 'al-nahy 'ala al-sabab al-khariji la yufsid al-'aqd'.
5
Identify the Conditions and Exceptions
The two minors must be in the same ownership. The mahram relation must be through lineage (nasab), not breastfeeding. The dislike continues until puberty, but if the minor consents near puberty, it becomes permissible. Also, a rightful cause (like crime) allows separation.
If one slave belongs to the owner's minor son, selling is allowed due to separate ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : تفريق صغير ؛ صورتهُ أنَّ من ملك بقبول الهبة أو الوصية أو الشراء أو الإرث مملوكين صغيرين أحدهما ذو رحم محرم من الآخر، فالتفريق بينهما مكروه، وكذلك إن كان أحدهما صغيراً والآخرُ كبيراً، لما أخرجه الترمذي عن أبي أيوب الأنصاري رضي الله عنه أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : «مَن فرق بين والدة وولدها، فرَّقَ الله بينه وبين أحبته يوم القيامة). ولما أخرجه الترمذي وابن ماجة عن علي رضي الله عنه من أنه وهبَ النَّبي صلى الله عليه وسلم غلامين أخوين صغيرين، ثم قال : ما فعل الغلامان ؟ فقال : بعت أحدهما، فقال رسول الله عليه وسلم : «أدرك أدرك» ، وفي رواية: «اردد اردد)، ففي الأول : وعيد صريح من فرق صغيراً عن ذي رحم محرم ، وفي الثاني : تكراراً لأمر بالادراك والرد. عن ذي رحم محرم منه بلا حق مستحق) : هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمّدٍ ، وأمَّا عند أبي يوسف رحمه الله إذا كان القرابة قرابة ولادة لا يجوز بيع أحدهما بدون الآخر، فإنَّه قال لعلي رضي الله عنه : أدرك أدرك». ولو كان البيع نافذا لا يمكنه الاستدراك ، ولو كان بحق مستحق كدفع أحدهما عن الآخر بالجناية. ولما لم يتعرض للبيع فقلنا بكراهة البيع لإفضائه إلى التفريق ، وهو مجاور ينفك عنه بجواز أن يقع ذلك بالهبة. كذا في «العناية» وغيرها ؛ ولأنَّ الصَّغير يستأنس بالكبير، والكبير يتعاهده، فكان في بيع أحدهما قطع الاستئناس، والمنعُ من التَّعاهد وفيه ترك الرحمة على الصغار، وقد أوعد عليه. كذا في «الهداية». ثم لا بد من اجتماعهما في ملكه، حتى لو كان أحدهما له، والآخر لابنه الصغير، له أن يبيع أحدهما لتفريق الملك ، كما صرح به العلامة العيني، وهذه الكراهة تمتد إلى البلوغ، وقال بعض مشايخنا : إذا راهق الصغير، ورضي أن يفترق بينهما لا بأس بذلك. ذكره البرْجَنْدي وغيره. [11 ] قوله : ذي رحم محرم منه ؛ الضمير راجع إلى الرحم لا إلى الصغير، فلا بُدَّ أن تكون محرميّته من جهةِ الرَّحم لا من الرضاع احتراز عن ابن عم هو أخ رضاعاً، فإنّه رحم محرم ، لكنَّ محرميّته من الرضاع لا من الرحم. [٣]قوله : بالجناية ؛ سواء كانت جناية مال أو نفس، فإنه إذا جنى أحدهما جناية أو استهلك مال أحد فللمولى أن يدفعه كما صرحوا به.
He said: 'Separation of a small child' – its form is that someone owns, by acceptance of a gift, bequest, purchase, or inheritance, two minor slaves, one of whom is a close blood relative (dhi rahim mahram) of the other. Then separating them is disliked (makruh). Likewise if one is minor and the other is adult. Because al-Tirmidhi narrated from Abu Ayyub al-Ansari that the Messenger of Allah said: 'Whoever separates a mother and her child, Allah will separate him from his loved ones on the Day of Judgment.' And because al-Tirmidhi and Ibn Majah narrated from 'Ali that he gave the Prophet two young brothers as a gift, then said: 'What did the two boys do?' He replied: 'I sold one of them.' The Messenger of Allah said: 'Catch up, catch up' and in another version: 'Return, return.' The first hadith contains an explicit warning against separating a minor from a blood relative, and the second contains a repeated command to retrieve and return. (The condition: from a blood relative without a rightful cause) – this is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. As for Abu Yusuf, if the relationship is one of lineage, it is not permissible to sell one without the other. Because he said to 'Ali: 'Catch up, catch up' – and if the sale were effective, he could not have retrieved it. And if it is for a rightful cause, like handing over one of them for the other's crime. And since he did not address the sale, we said it is disliked (makruh) because it leads to separation, which is a contiguous evil, and it can be avoided by making it a gift. Thus it is in al-'Inayah and others. And because the minor finds comfort with the adult, and the adult takes care of him. So selling one cuts off comfort and prevents care, which is abandoning mercy to the young, and it has been warned against. Thus in al-Hidayah. Then it is necessary that both are in his ownership, so if one is his and the other is his minor son's, he may sell one due to separation of ownership, as stated by al-'Ayni. And this dislike extends until puberty. Some of our scholars said: When the minor approaches puberty and consents to separation, there is no harm. Mentioned by al-Barjandi and others. [11] He said: 'dhi rahim mahram minhu' – the pronoun refers to the womb, not the minor. So the mahram relation must be through lineage, not through breastfeeding, as a precaution against the case of a paternal cousin who is also a foster brother: he is a blood relative, but his being mahram is due to breastfeeding, not lineage. [2] He said: 'for he said: ...etc.' – this is the evidence for the impermissibility of selling them. And the response on behalf of Imam and Muhammad is that the pillar of the sale, i.e., offer and acceptance, issued from its competent person (the sane adult) in its place (wealth), and dislike only arises for a contiguous meaning, which is the loneliness resulting from separation. And whenever a prohibition is for a meaning outside the prohibited thing, it does not invalidate the contract, like the prohibition of contracting at the Friday call to prayer. [3] He said: 'by crime' – whether it is a crime against property or life. For if one of them commits a crime or consumes someone's property, the master may hand him over, as they have stated.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the ruling of selling one of two young slaves who are mahram to each other through lineage. The author first describes the scenario: someone owns two minor slaves who are blood relatives (e.g., siblings). It is makruh (disliked) to separate them by selling one, even if one is an adult. The proof is from two hadith: (1) a general threat against separating a mother and child, and (2) the specific incident of 'Ali giving the Prophet two young brothers, then selling one, and the Prophet ordering him to retrieve the sold one. A dispute arises among the Hanafi imams: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the sale is valid but makruh because the prohibition is for an external reason (contiguous evil). Abu Yusuf holds the sale invalid if the relationship is through lineage, reasoning that the Prophet's command to 'catch up' implies the sale was not binding. The commentary supports the former view, arguing that the sale's pillars are sound and the dislike is for the resulting separation, not an inherent defect in the contract — similar to the prohibition of sale at Friday adhan. Conditions: the two must be in the same ownership; if one belongs to the owner's minor son, selling one is allowed. The dislike continues until the minor reaches puberty. If the minor approaches puberty and consents, separation becomes permissible. Further, the mahram relation must be through lineage (nasab), not through breastfeeding (rada'). Finally, if one slave commits a crime, the master may hand him over to the victim, which is a permissible separation due to necessity.

Key Terms
ذو رحم محرم (dhu rahim mahram): A blood relative whom one cannot marry (mahram) due to lineage, not due to breastfeeding.
تفريق صغير (tafriq saghir): Separating a minor slave from his mahram relative by selling one of them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) regarding the validity of selling one of two young mahram slaves?
A) The sale is invalid and the seller must retrieve the slave.
B) The sale is valid but disliked (makruh).
C) The sale is valid and recommended.
D) The sale is haram and the contract is void.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between prohibitions that affect the contract's essence (invalidating) and those that only render the act disliked (makruh), keeping the sale valid.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

8 min
Selling One of Two Mahram Relatives: Valid but Disliked
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mahram sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling one of two mahram relatives, explain the intra-madhhab disagreement, and apply the distinction between validity and dislikedness.

Core Idea
Sale of one mahram sibling is valid but disliked because the harm is external, not contractual.

The classical Hanafi masters debate a sensitive case: a person owns two mahram relatives (e.g., two young siblings). Can he sell one without the other? The answer reveals how Hanafi fiqh separates the validity of a contract from its moral desirability. This lesson unpacks the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad against the objection of Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
The passage states that according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, selling one of two mahram relatives without a legitimate right is makruh (disliked) but valid. Understand that the sale itself is legally sound; the dislike is due to the external consequence of separation.
Makruh here is tanzihi, not tahrimi.
2
Understand Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf holds that such a sale is not permissible. He cites a report from Ali (ra): 'A dirham, a dirham' (interpreted as a command to prevent the sale). He argues that if the sale is executed, separation cannot be undone.
Abu Yusuf's position is based on preventing harm, not on a defect in the contract.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to Abu Yusuf
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad respond that the dislike arises from the external factor (tafriq), not from any invalidity in the contract. The separation can alternatively be achieved through gift (hiba), which avoids the disliked consequence.
This is similar to the ruling on selling at Friday adhan: forbidden but valid.
4
Note the conditions and exceptions
The dislike applies only when both relatives are in the same ownership and there is no legitimate right (e.g., paying a crime victim). Also, the dislike ends when the minor reaches puberty and consents to separation.
If one relative belongs to the seller and the other to his son, selling one is not disliked.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a person owning two young siblings, decide the ruling: sale is valid but makruh. If the buyer is a relative who will not separate them, some scholars say the dislike may be reduced.
The mufta bihi is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع/ البيع الفاسد والمكروه
عن ذي رحم محرم منه بلا حق مستحق) : هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمّدٍ ، وأمَّا عند أبي يوسف الله إذا كان القرابة قرابة ولادة لا يجوز بيع أحدهما بدون الآخر، فإنَّه قال لعلي ه : أدرك أدرك». ولو كان البيع نافذا لا يمكنه الاستدراك ، ولو كان بحق مستحق كدفع أحدهما عن الآخر بالجناية
ولما لم يتعرض للبيع فقلنا بكراهة البيع لإفضائه إلى التفريق ، وهو مجاور ينفك عنه بجواز أن يقع ذلك بالهبة. كذا في «العناية»(۱) وغيرها ؛ ولأنَّ الصَّغير يستأنس بالكبير، والكبير يتعاهده، فكان في بيع أحدهما قطع الاستئناس، والمنعُ من التَّعاهد وفيه ترك الرحمة على الصغار، وقد أوعد عليه. كذا في «الهداية»(٢). ثم لا بد من اجتماعهما في ملكه، حتى لو كان أحدهما له، والآخر لابنه الصغير، له أن يبيع أحدهما لتفريق الملك ، كما صرح به العلامة العيني(٣) ، وهذه الكراهة تمتد إلى البلوغ، وقال بعض مشايخنا : إذا راهق الصغير، ورضي أن يفترق بينهما لا بأس بذلك. ذكره البرْجَنْدي وغيره.
Book of Sale / Invalid and Disliked Sale. Regarding 'a mahram relative without a legitimate right': This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. As for Abu Yusuf, if the kinship is by blood, it is not permissible to sell one of them without the other, for he said 'perhaps? catch up catch up'. And if the sale is executed, he cannot catch up. And if it is due to a legitimate right, like repelling one of them from the other due to a crime. Since the contract [of sale] is not invalidated, we say it is disliked [karahah] because it leads to separation, and this is an external [reason] that can be avoided by the possibility of that separation occurring through gift. Thus in al-'Inayah and others. And because the young one is comforted by the older one, and the older one takes care of him, so selling one of them cuts off that comfort and prevents care, and there is abandonment of mercy towards the young, and it has been threatened against. Thus in al-Hidayah. Then it is necessary that both be in his ownership, such that if one is his and the other is for his young son, he may sell one of them to separate the ownership, as stated by al-'Ayni. This dislike extends until puberty. And some of our scholars said: when the young one approaches puberty and consents to separation, there is no harm in it. Mentioned by al-Birjandi and others.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the ruling on selling one of two mahram relatives (e.g., two young siblings or a mother and child) when there is no legitimate right forcing separation. The matn states 'عن ذي رحم محرم منه بلا حق مستحق' – regarding a mahram relative without a legitimate right [to separate]. The commentary clarifies that according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the sale is valid but makruh (disliked) because it leads to separation. Abu Yusuf, however, holds that such a sale is not permissible at all, based on a statement attributed to him: 'أدرك أدرك' interpreted as an instruction to prevent the sale. The reasoning for karahah is that the sale causes separation (tafriq) which results in psychological harm: the young one is comforted by the elder and the elder cares for the young; selling one cuts this. This is an external factor (ma'na mujawir) that does not invalidate the contract itself. The dislike can be avoided if the separation happens through gift (hiba). A condition is that both must be in the same ownership; if one belongs to the seller and the other to his son, he may sell his share. The dislike extends until puberty; when the child approaches puberty and consents, it is no longer makruh.

Key Terms
ذي رحم محرم (dhi rahim muharram): A relative who is a mahram (permanently prohibited in marriage) due to blood kinship, such as siblings or parent-child.
كراهة (karāha): Dislike/disapproval that does not affect the validity of the act, i.e., makruh tanzihi.
تفريق (tafrīq): Separation of the two relatives through sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, selling one of two mahram siblings without a legitimate right is:
A) Valid and recommended
B) Invalid (batil)
C) Valid but disliked (makruh)
D) Prohibited (haram)

Key Takeaway
Selling one of two mahram relatives is valid but disliked because the harm is external and can be avoided through alternative means like gift.

Returning a Defective Item from a Pair: Why It's Not Disliked
Ruling with talil: returning defective item from a pair is not disliked.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on returning a defective item from a pair of mahram relatives and explain why it is not disliked.

Core Idea
Returning a defective item from a pair is a right; incidental harm to the other is overlooked.

You know that separating two small siblings in a sale is normally disliked because it harms them. But what if one is defective? The Hanafi school rules that the buyer may return the defective one without being blamed. This lesson unpacks the reasoning: a legitimate right (khiyar al-‘ayb) overrides the prohibition because preventing the right would harm the buyer. The incidental harm to the sibling is overlooked in the law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the explicit ruling: returning a defective item from a pair is not disliked (la yukrah).
The phrase 'والرد بالعيب لا يكره' is clear.
2
Understand the scenario
The case involves buying two small siblings (mahram) for oneself, then discovering a hidden defect in one.
The defect must be latent (not apparent at sale).
3
Grasp the reason
The return is based on a legitimate right (bi-haqq mustaḥaqq) – the right of khiyar al-‘ayb.
This right is established by the defect.
4
Analyze the reasoning
The general prohibition of separating siblings is to prevent harm. But here, enforcing that prohibition would harm the buyer by depriving him of his right. The harm to the sibling from separation is incidental to fulfilling a legitimate right, so it is ignored.
Principle: incidental harm (ḍamnan) is not considered when a right is established.
5
Apply the principle
Recognize that khiyar al-‘ayb allows the buyer to rescind part of the sale. This ruling shows that rights based on defect prevail over general prohibitions when harm is incidental.
Compare with the previous lesson where separation without right is disliked.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرَّد بالعيب لا يُكْرَه".
(1) قوله : والردّ ; صورته : اشترى أخوين صغيرين لنفسه فوجد بأحدهما عيباً لم يظهر عند الشراء، فله أن يرده ويمسك الثاني ؛ لأنَّ هذا التفريق بحق مستحق. [٢]قوله : لا يكره ; لأنَّ التفريق إنّما نهى عنه لدفع الضرر عن الصغير، وليس من شرط دفع الضرر عن شخص إلحاق الضرر بغيره ، فإذا تعلق بأحدهما حق فالمنع من إيفاء الحق إضرار بصاحب الحق ، وإنّما حصل الإضرار بالصغير ضمناً لحق مستحق فلا يلتفت إليه ؛ لأنه كم من شيء يثبت ضمناً ولا يثبت قصداً. كذا في «البناية).
"And the return due to a defect is not disliked." (1) His statement: 'And the return' – its scenario: He bought two small brothers for himself and found in one of them a defect that was not apparent at the time of purchase. So he may return that one and keep the other, because this separation is due to a legitimate right. (2) His statement: 'is not disliked' – because the prohibition of separation was only to prevent harm to the small child, and it is not a condition of preventing harm to a person to cause harm to another. So when a right attaches to one of them, preventing the fulfillment of that right harms the owner of the right. The harm to the small child occurs only incidentally due to a legitimate right, so it is not considered; for many things are established incidentally and not intended. Thus in al-Binayah.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling that returning a defective item from a pair is not disliked. The scenario: a person purchases two small siblings (who are mahram to each other) and later discovers a defect in one of them that existed at the time of sale but was not apparent. The buyer has the right to return only the defective one and keep the other, even though this separates them. The reason given for not disliking this return is that the earlier prohibition of separation (which made selling one of them disliked) was to prevent harm to the small child. However, when a legitimate right (the right of return due to defect) is involved, preventing that right would harm the buyer. The harm to the small child from separation is incidental (bil-ḍamān) to fulfilling a legitimate right, and thus it is overlooked, much like how some things are established as incidental but not as intended. This reasoning is from al-Binayah.

Key Terms
الرد بالعيب (al-radd bil-‘ayb): The right to return a defective item due to a latent defect (khiyar al-‘ayb).
بحق مستحق (biḥaqq mustaḥaqq): Based on a legitimate right that is established (e.g., the right of return due to defect).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone buys two small siblings and finds a defect in one, what is the ruling on returning only the defective one?
A) Disliked (makruh)
B) Valid but disliked
C) Not disliked (la yukrah)
D) Invalid

Key Takeaway
A legitimate right (such as khiyar al-‘ayb) overrides the general prohibition of separating siblings; incidental harm is legally overlooked.

Sale by Bidding (Bai' al-Muzayada) is Permissible
Ruling on the permissibility of auction-style sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on auction sales and identify the evidence from the Prophet's practice.

Core Idea
Auction sales are permissible in Hanafi fiqh, supported by the Prophet's own transaction.

The text addresses a common question: can a seller accept the highest bid? The phrase 'لا بيع من يزيد الثمن' might appear prohibitive, but the intended meaning is permissive. The passage provides three proofs: prophetic practice, absence of harm, and public need.

Guided Path
1
Interpret the title phrase
Determine whether the phrase 'لا بيع من يزيد الثمن' is a prohibition or a denial of prohibition. In context, it means 'there is no [issue with] the sale of the one who increases the price', i.e., it is permissible.
2
Identify the primary proof
The hadith records that the Prophet himself sold a drinking cup and a saddle blanket, which indicates that bidding or negotiation in a sale is permitted by his action.
3
Recognize the secondary proofs
The text adds that there is no harm in this type of sale and that it is beneficial for the poor, fulfilling a pressing need. These strengthen the permissibility.
4
Note the synonym
This sale is also called bai' al-dalalah (sale by broker). Recognising the terminology helps in understanding related fiqh discussions.
5
Conclude the Hanafi ruling
Based on the evidence, the Hanafi ruling is that auction sales are mubah (permissible). There is no prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(لا بيعُ مَن يَزِيدُ الثَّمن) .
(3) قوله : لا بيع من يزيدُ الثمن ; لما ثبت أنَّ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم «باغ قدحاً وحلساً (۳) ; ولأنه لا ضرر فيه، ولأنه بيع الفقراء، والحاجة ماسة إليه. كذا في «البحر»(۳)، ويسمّى هذا البيع بيع الدلالة.
("There is no sale of the one who increases the price"). (3) His statement: 'There is no sale of the one who increases the price' – because it is established that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) 'sold a drinking cup and a saddle blanket' (3); and because there is no harm in it; and because it is the sale of the poor, and there is a pressing need for it. Thus in al-Bahr. And this sale is called 'sale by brokerage' (bai' al-dalalah).
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage affirms the permissibility of selling to the highest bidder (bai' al-muzayada or bai' man yazid). The text presents three proofs: (1) A hadith that the Prophet himself sold a drinking cup and a saddle blanket (implying bidding or negotiation); (2) There is no harm in it; (3) It is a transaction that benefits the poor and is often needed. The commentary notes that this type of sale is also called 'bai' al-dalalah' (sale by broker). The phrasing 'لا بيع من يزيد الثمن' is interpreted as 'there is no [problem with] the sale of the one who increases the price', meaning it is permissible.

Key Terms
بيع من يزيد الثمن (bai' man yazīd ath-thaman): A sale where the price is increased through bidding; auction sale.
بيع الدلالة (bai' ad-dalāla): Sale by broker or agent; often synonymous with auction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on selling to the highest bidder (bai' man yazid)?
A) Disliked (makruh)
B) Impermissible (haram)
C) Permissible (mubah)
D) Recommended (sunnah)

Key Takeaway
Bai' al-muzayada is permissible in the Hanafi school based on prophetic practice, public need, and absence of harm.

Ruling on Returning a Defective Item (Radd bil-‘Ayb)
Ruling on returning defective goods with commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on returning a defective item and explain why separation of siblings is permissible in that context.

Core Idea
Returning a defective item is a buyer’s right that overrides the prohibition on separating siblings.

This lesson focuses on the first part of the passage: the ruling that returning a defective item (الرد بالعيب) is not disliked. The commentary illustrates a scenario where a buyer purchases two young siblings and later discovers a defect in one. Despite the general prohibition on separating siblings, the buyer may return the defective one and keep the other. This is because the separation is based on a rightful claim (حق مستحق), and the resulting harm to the sibling is incidental and therefore overlooked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the explicit ruling: 'Returning due to defect is not disliked'. Determine its hukm level (mubah).
2
Understand the scenario
Visualize: a buyer purchases two young siblings (e.g., slave children) for himself, later finds a pre-existing defect in one.
The defect must have been hidden at the time of sale.
3
Analyze the reasoning
The commentary explains that the prohibition on separating siblings was to prevent harm. Here, preventing the return would harm the buyer's right. The harm to the sibling is incidental (ضمناً) and not intended, so it is disregarded.
Principle: 'Things established incidentally are not established intentionally'.
4
Apply the principle
Generalize: any rightful claim (such as khiyar al-‘ayb) takes precedence over general prohibitions aimed at preventing harm, as long as the harm to others is incidental and unavoidable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا بيعُ مَن يَزِيدُ الثَّمن [۱ والرَّد بالعيب لا يُكْرَه".
(لا بيعُ مَن يَزِيدُ الثَّمن) .
(1) قوله : والردّ ؛ صورته : اشترى أخوين صغيرين لنفسه فوجد بأحدهما عيباً لم يظهر عند الشراء، فله أن يرده ويمسك الثاني ؛ لأنَّ هذا التفريق بحق مستحق. [٢]قوله : لا يكره ؛ لأنَّ التفريق إنّما نهى عنه لدفع الضرر عن الصغير، وليس من شرط دفع الضرر عن شخص إلحاق الضرر بغيره ، فإذا تعلق بأحدهما حق فالمنع من إيفاء الحق إضرار بصاحب الحق ، وإنّما حصل الإضرار بالصغير ضمناً لحق مستحق فلا يلتفت إليه ؛ لأنه كم من شيء يثبت ضمناً ولا يثبت قصداً. كذا في «البناية).
(۳) قوله : لا بيع من يزيدُ الثمن ؛ لما ثبت أنَّ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم «باغ قدحاً وحلساً (۳) ؛ ولأنه لا ضرر فيه، ولأنه بيع الفقراء، والحاجة ماسة إليه. كذا في «البحر»(۳)، ويسمّى هذا البيع بيع الدلالة.

No sale of one who increases the price. And returning due to defect is not disliked. Commentary: (1) He said: 'And returning' – its scenario: He bought two young brothers for himself, and found in one a defect that did not appear at the time of purchase. He may return that one and keep the other because this separation is by a rightful claim. (2) He said: 'It is not disliked' because separation was prohibited only to prevent harm to the young. It is not a condition of preventing harm to a person to cause harm to another. So when a right is attached to one of them, preventing the fulfillment of the right harms the owner of the right. The harm to the young occurred incidentally due to a rightful claim, so it is not regarded because many things are established incidentally and not established intentionally. Thus in al-Binayah. (3) He said: 'No sale of one who increases the price' because it is established that the Messenger of Allah sold a cup and a rug; and because there is no harm in it; and because it is the sale of the poor and there is dire need for it. Thus in al-Bahr. And this sale is called brokerage sale.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct rulings from the matn, each with its own commentary. The first ruling is that returning a defective item (الرد بالعيب) is not disliked (لا يكره). The commentary clarifies that if one purchases two young siblings and finds a defect in one that was latent at purchase, he may return that one and keep the other. This is permissible because the prohibition on separating siblings was to prevent harm to them, but here the buyer has a right to return the defective item. Preventing that right would harm the buyer, and any incidental harm to the young is overlooked as it is a necessary consequence of fulfilling a rightful claim. This principle is cited from al-Binayah: many things are established indirectly and not directly. The second ruling is that bidding up (بيع من يزيد الثمن) is prohibited. The commentary provides a hadith that the Prophet (ﷺ) sold a cup and a rug, which is taken as evidence of permissibility? Actually, the phrasing 'لا بيع' indicates a prohibition, but the commentary mentions the hadith and argues that there is no harm in it and it is the sale of the poor, implying it is allowed. This point requires further clarification; the text from al-Bahr is cited. This type of sale is known as brokerage sale (بيع الدلالة).

Key Terms
الرد بالعيب (al-radd bil-‘ayb): The right to return a purchased item due to a defect that existed at the time of sale and was unknown to the buyer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases two cats (brothers) and finds a defect in one. According to the Hanafi ruling, can he return the defective cat and keep the other?
A) No, because separating siblings is absolutely prohibited.
B) Yes, because returning a defective item is a right that justifies separation.
C) No, because a defect in one implies both are defective.
D) Yes, but he must also compensate the seller for the separation.

Key Takeaway
Returning a defective item is a buyer's right that can be exercised even if it results in separation of grouped items, as long as the harm to the other is incidental.

Ruling on Bidding Up (Bay‘ Man Yazīd al-Thaman)
Ruling on bidding up with commentary and proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bidding up and distinguish between prohibited bidding (najash) and permissible brokerage sales (bay‘ al-dalālah).

Core Idea
Bidding up is prohibited, but brokerage sales that serve the poor are permitted.

This lesson covers the second part of the passage: the ruling that 'لا بيع من يزيد الثمن' – no sale of one who increases the price. While this seems to prohibit any bidding, the commentary cites a hadith where the Prophet sold a cup and a rug, and argues that certain types of price-increasing sales (such as brokerage) are allowed because they serve the poor and involve no harm. This reveals a nuanced Hanafi position: the category of bidding up (najash) is prohibited, but the practice known as 'بيع الدلالة' (brokerage sale) is permissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the explicit ruling
The matn states 'لا بيع من يزيد الثمن'. Determine its apparent meaning: a sale where the price is increased through bidding is not valid or prohibited.
The phrase 'لا بيع' can indicate prohibition (nahy).
2
Examine the commentary's proof
The commentary mentions that the Prophet (ﷺ) sold a cup (قدح) and a rug (حلس). This hadith is used as evidence that some forms of bidding are permissible.
The exact hadith: 'باغ قدحاً وحلساً' – likely 'He sold a cup and a rug'.
3
Understand the reasoning
The commentary gives three reasons: (1) there is no harm in it; (2) it is the sale of the poor; (3) there is dire need for it. This suggests that the prohibition is removed when the practice benefits the needy.
4
Distinguish the types
Distinguish between prohibited bidding (najash) – where someone artificially inflates price to deceive – and permissible brokerage (bay‘ al-dalālah) – where a broker facilitates a sale for a fee, often increasing the price for the seller's benefit without deception.
The commentary explicitly calls this type 'بيع الدلالة'.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position according to Zahir al-Riwayah is that bidding up (najash) is makruh tahrimi (prohibited close to haram). However, brokerage sales that are customary and involve no deceit are permissible.
The Hanafi position is nuanced: not all price increases are prohibited.

Source Text

This passage presents two distinct rulings from the matn, each with its own commentary. The first ruling is that returning a defective item (الرد بالعيب) is not disliked (لا يكره). The commentary clarifies that if one purchases two young siblings and finds a defect in one that was latent at purchase, he may return that one and keep the other. This is permissible because the prohibition on separating siblings was to prevent harm to them, but here the buyer has a right to return the defective item. Preventing that right would harm the buyer, and any incidental harm to the young is overlooked as it is a necessary consequence of fulfilling a rightful claim. This principle is cited from al-Binayah: many things are established indirectly and not directly. The second ruling is that bidding up (بيع من يزيد الثمن) is prohibited. The commentary provides a hadith that the Prophet (ﷺ) sold a cup and a rug, which is taken as evidence of permissibility? Actually, the phrasing 'لا بيع' indicates a prohibition, but the commentary mentions the hadith and argues that there is no harm in it and it is the sale of the poor, implying it is allowed. This point requires further clarification; the text from al-Bahr is cited. This type of sale is known as brokerage sale (بيع الدلالة).

Key Terms
بيع من يزيد الثمن (bay‘ man yazīd al-thaman): A sale where the price is increased through bidding, often associated with auction-like bidding up.
بيع الدلالة (bay‘ al-dalālah): A brokerage sale where a middleman facilitates the transaction, often by increasing the price for the seller's benefit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the Hanafi ruling on bidding up?
A) All forms of bidding up are strictly prohibited (haram).
B) Bidding up is prohibited only if it involves deception; brokerage sales are allowed.
C) Bidding up is permissible for luxury items but not for necessities.
D) Bidding up is permitted because the Prophet himself engaged in it.

Key Takeaway
Not all price-increasing sales are prohibited; the Hanafi school permits brokerage (bay‘ al-dalālah) when it is transparent and serves a genuine need.

Definition and Pillars of Rescission
Linguistic and legal definition of rescission, its pillars, and an intra-Hanafi dispute on acceptable wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal definition of rescission (iqala), identify its pillars (offer and acceptance), and explain the Hanafi dispute on the form of offer and acceptance.

Core Idea
Rescission is a mutual annulment contract; its wording can be flexible per the two elders or explicit per Muhammad.

Rescission (الإقالة) is a vital concept in Islamic sales law, allowing parties to mutually undo a contract. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya lays out the linguistic and legal definition, then delves into the essential pillars and a key internal Hanafi dispute about how the offer and acceptance must be phrased. Understanding this will help you apply the correct form for a valid rescission.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish linguistic vs. legal definition
Note that linguistically iqala means 'lifting' (annulment), derived from 'qīl' (saying), not 'qawl'. Legally, it is the lifting of a sale or contract.
The author refutes a false etymology to clarify the root.
2
Identify the rukn (pillar)
The rukn is the offer (ījāb) and acceptance (qabūl) that indicate the rescission.
3
Analyze the condition on wording
The text states a condition: the offer and acceptance must be in two past-tense words, or one future and one past. This is the view of the two elders.
Focus on the difference between the two elders and Imam Muhammad.
4
Understand the session requirement
Acceptance must occur in the same session (majlis), and can be by action (e.g., taking back goods), analogous to a sale.
5
Apply the dispute to a scenario
Consider: buyer says 'Aqilnī' and seller replies 'Aqaltuka'. According to the two elders it is valid; Muhammad would require explicit mention of rescission.
Identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

فإن عنده يشترط أن يعبر بهما عن المعنى كالبيع، وتتوقف على القبول في المجلس، ولو فعلاً كالبيع، حتى لو قبل الآخر بعد زوال المجلس أو بعدما صدر عنه ما

Chapter of Rescission. (1) He said: Chapter of Rescission: It is deliverance from the filth of corrupt and disliked sales. Since it is by annulment, rescission has a special connection to them, so it was placed after them. Rescission linguistically means lifting absolutely. It is said it is derived from 'qawl' (speech), with the hamza for removal, so 'aqala' means to remove the speech, i.e., the first speech, which is the sale, as it is said 'ashkāhu' meaning he removed his complaint. This is refuted on several grounds: First, it is pronounced 'qiltuhu' with kasra, indicating its root letter is ya not waw, so it is not from qawl. Al-Qamus said: 'qalta al-bay‘a' (with kasra) and 'aqlatahu' meaning annulled it; 'istaqalatahu' means asked him to annul; 'taqābala al-bay‘ān' and 'aqāla Allāhu ‘ushrataka' and 'aqālakahā'. End. He mentioned it under qaf with ya. Similar is in al-Misbah al-Munir. Second, as-Sihah mentions it under qaf with ya not waw. Third, Majmu‘ al-Lugha says: 'qāla al-bay‘a qīlan wa iqālatan' meaning annulled it. Thus ash-Shami mentioned it, so it is established that it is derived from 'qīl'. Legally, it is the lifting of the sale. Al-Jawharah said: it is the lifting of the contract, which is a definition covering rescission of sale, lease, etc. Its pillar is offer and acceptance that indicate it. The condition is that they be in two past-tense words, or one future and the other past, like 'Aqilnī faqad aqlatuka' according to the two elders, like marriage, contrary to Muhammad. He requires that they express the meaning like a sale, and acceptance must be in the session, even by an act, like a sale. So if the other accepts after the session ends or after an action indicating rejection, [it is invalid].
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage inaugurates the chapter on rescission (الإقالة). The commentator begins by explaining the placement of the chapter: since rescission is an annulment, it is particularly connected to corrupt and disliked sales, so it is discussed after them. The linguistic analysis delves into the root of the word 'iqalah'. Some claim it is from 'qawl' (speech) with the hamza for negation, meaning removal of the original statement (the sale). The commentator refutes this with three arguments: (1) the verb is pronounced 'qaltu' with kasra (ya), not with fatha (wa), so it is not from qawl, corroborated by al-Qamus; (2) as-Sihah lists it under qaf-ya; (3) Majmu‘ al-Lugha explicitly derives it from 'qīl' (saying). Thus linguistically, iqalah means annulment. Legally, it is the lifting of the sale (or contract, as per al-Jawharah, covering also leases). The pillar (rukn) is offer and acceptance that indicate the rescission. The condition is that the offer and acceptance be expressed in past tense, or one future and one past. This is the view of the two elders (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), analogous to marriage. Imam Muhammad disagrees, insisting that the words must explicitly convey the meaning, like in a sale: 'I rescind' or the like. Additionally, acceptance must occur in the same session, and may be by an act (like taking back the goods), as in a sale. If acceptance is delayed until after the session or after a rejecting statement, it is invalid. The authority for this is al-Jawharah, and the dispute is classic Hanafi: the two elders allow general expressions like 'forgive me' while Muhammad requires specific terms.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-iqālah): Legally, the lifting (annulment) of a sale or other contract by mutual agreement.
الركن (al-rukn): The essential component of an act; here, the offer and acceptance that constitute rescission.
الشيخين (al-shaykhayn): The two elders, i.e., Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two elders (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), what is the condition for the wording of offer and acceptance in rescission?
A) Must be in past tense only.
B) Must be explicit like 'I rescind the sale'.
C) Can be in two past tense words or one future and one past.
D) Must involve the word 'iqalah' specifically.

Key Takeaway
Rescission (iqala) is a mutual annulment contract; its pillars are offer and acceptance, and the Hanafi school primarily follows the two elders in allowing flexible wording.

Iqalah: Definition, Conditions, and Hanafi Ruling
Definition and ruling of iqalah with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of iqalah, identify its pillar and the condition of wording according to the Hanafi school, and explain when it is mandub vs. wajib.

Core Idea
Iqalah is rescission, not a new sale: the two shaykhs allow flexible wording like marriage, while Muhammad requires sale-like past tense.

Iqalah (rescission of a sale) is a contract that lifts an existing sale by mutual agreement. The Hanafi school defines it as 'raf' al-bay' (lifting the sale) and classifies its pillar as offer and acceptance. A key intra-madhhab dispute concerns the permissible verb tense for the offer and acceptance. The ruling varies: generally recommended, but obligatory when the original contract is invalid or involves slight deception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic root
Trace the debate on whether 'iqalah' derives from 'qawl' (speech) or 'qayl' (rescinding). The commentator refutes the former with three lexical sources.
The correct root is q-y-l, not q-w-l.
2
Distinguish legal definition from linguistic
Legal iqalah is 'lifting a sale' or more broadly 'lifting a contract' (al-Jawharah). Note the scope: includes sale, lease, etc.
3
Analyze the pillar and condition on wording
Pillar: offer and acceptance. Condition: according to the two shaykhs, one verb may be future (imperative) and the other past; Muhammad requires both past. Understand the analogy to marriage vs. sale.
The mufta bihi position allows mixed tenses.
4
Determine the ruling and its exceptions
Iqalah is mandub for valid sales due to the hadith. It becomes wajib if the contract is fasid or makruh, or if the seller has slightly deceived (ghabn yasir).
The ruling changes based on the nature of the original contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter of Iqalah. The deliverance from the impurity of invalid and disliked sales. Since it involves rescission, iqalah has a special connection to them, so its mention follows them. Linguistically, iqalah means lifting absolutely. It is said to be derived from 'qawl' (speech), with the hamza for negation, so 'aqala' means to remove the speech: that is, the first speech, which is the sale, just as it is said 'ashkahu' meaning he removed his complaint. This is refutable in several ways: First: it is said 'qiltuhu' with kasra, indicating the root letter is ya not waw, so it is not from 'qawl'. Al-Qamus says: 'qalta al-bay'a' with kasra, and 'aqaltuhu' meaning I rescinded it, 'istaqalahu' meaning he asked him to rescind, 'taqabala al-bayi'ani', and 'aqala Allahu 'ushrataka' and 'aqalakahuma'. Third: it is mentioned in Majmu' al-Lughah: 'qala al-bay'a' qaylan and iqalahan meaning rescinded it, as al-Shami mentioned. So it is established that it is derived from 'qayl'. Legally: it is the lifting of a sale. Al-Jawharah says: it is the lifting of a contract, which is a definition covering iqalah of sale, lease, and the like. Its pillars are offer and acceptance indicating it, and the condition that they be in two past tense verbs or one future and one past, like 'aqilni faqad aqaltuka' according to the two shaykhs, like marriage, contrary to Muhammad. For according to him, they must express the meaning like a sale, and it is contingent upon acceptance in the session, even if by action like a sale, such that if the other accepts after the session ends or after he has done something indicating disinterest, the iqalah is not completed. It is recommended due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever rescinds a Muslim's sale, Allah will rescind his stumble on the Day of Resurrection,' reported by Abu Dawud and Ibn Majah, and narrated by Ibn Hibban in his Sahih and al-Hakim who said it meets the conditions of the two shaykhs, and according to al-Bayhaqi: 'Whoever rescinds for a regretful person,' as in al-Fath. And it is obligatory in every disliked and invalid contract, as in al-Bahr, and when the seller has slightly deceived him, as in al-Nahr. And there are lengthy discussions deposited in al-Bahr, al-Nahr, and other detailed works.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling of iqalah (rescission of a sale) from the Hanafi perspective. The commentator begins by explaining the linguistic meaning: 'raf' (lifting) and the debate whether it derives from 'qawl' (speech) or from a different root. He refutes the 'qawl' derivation with three lexical authorities (al-Qamus, al-Sihah, Majmu' al-Lughah) showing the root is q-y-l, not q-w-l. Legally, iqalah is defined as lifting a sale (or contract according to al-Jawharah, making it more general). Its pillar is offer and acceptance. A key condition, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two shaykhs), is that the wording can include a future tense verb in the offer, e.g., 'aqilni' (implying future) answered by 'faqad aqaltuka' (past). Muhammad al-Shaybani, however, requires both past-tense verbs as in a regular sale. Also, acceptance must occur in the same session, even by action; if delayed or after an indication of disinterest, the iqalah fails. The ruling is mandub (recommended) based on several hadiths promising divine reward. It becomes wajib (obligatory) when the contract is makruh or fasid, or when the seller has slightly deceived the buyer, to undo the harm.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqalah): Legally, the lifting/termination of a sale (or contract) by mutual agreement, returning the goods and price to their original owners.
ركن (rukn): Pillar: the essential components of iqalah, which are offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) 'Aqaltuka' and 'qabiltu' (both past but different verbs)
D) 'Sa'uqiluka' and 'qabiltu' (future + past)

Key Takeaway
Iqalah is a rescission contract with flexible wording; its general ruling is mandub but becomes wajib when the original contract is unlawful or involves slight deception.
