The Dual Nature of Iqalah: Rescission for Parties, Sale for Third Parties
Explaining why iqalah is rescission for parties but sale for third parties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the dual nature of iqalah and apply it to situations involving preemption and subsequent sales.

Core Idea
Iqalah is a rescission between the parties but a new sale for third parties – a hybrid legal nature.

The Hanafi school treats iqalah (mutual rescission of a sale) with a nuanced dual nature: it is a rescission (faskh) between the two contracting parties, but a new sale (bay') from the perspective of third parties. This distinction, drawn from the literal meaning of 'iqalah' and its practical implications, affects rights such as preemption (shuf'ah) and the validity of subsequent sales. In this lesson, we will explore the linguistic basis for this ruling and its concrete applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic argument
Note that the word 'iqalah' literally means 'lifting' or 'removal', which is the opposite of 'establishing' (as in sale). The principle that speech is taken in its real (haqiqi) meaning requires iqalah to be treated as rescission between the parties.
This is a dalil aqli based on language, not a revealed text.
2
Distinguish the two perspectives
Understand that for the original parties, iqalah cancels the sale and reverts ownership. For third parties (e.g., a preemptor), it is considered a new sale because it transfers ownership again, triggering rights like preemption.
This dual treatment is unique to the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the practical consequences
Apply the ruling: any increase or decrease from the original price in iqalah is void (batil). The seller can resell the item to the buyer before taking possession, because ownership reverts immediately upon iqalah.
If iqalah were a sale, reselling before possession (qabd) would be invalid.
4
Apply to preemption (shuf'ah)
Consider the case: A sells a house, a preemptor (C) waives his right, then A and B rescind. Because iqalah is a new sale for third parties, C cannot claim preemption again (he already waived). If iqalah were pure rescission, C could claim on the return.
The Hanafi ruling protects the finality of waivers and prevents reopening claims.
5
Compare with other schools
Note that other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) treat iqalah entirely as rescission, so the preemptor could claim again after rescission. Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) in shuf'ah cases.
The ikhtilaf is legitimate and based on different usul principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : فسخ في حق ...الخ ؛ لأنها تنبئ عن الفسخ والرفع، والأصل في الكلام أن يحمل على حقيقته فلا يحمل على البيع ؛ لأنه ضدُّها، إذ هي عبارة عن الرفع والإزالة، والبيع عبارة عن الإثبات، فتعين البطلان في الحمل على البيع. وأما كونها بيعاً في حق غيرهما ، فمعنوي إذ يثبت به حكم البيع، وهو الملك ، ولما كان فسخاً في حق المتعاقدين فبطل نطقاً به من الزيادة على الثَّمن الأول، والنقصان. ولو باع البائع المبيع من المشتري قبل أن يستردّه منه جاز، ولو كان بيعاً لم يكن جائزاً ؛ لكونه قبل القبض. ولما كان بيعاً جديداً في حق غيرهما فوجبت الشفعة للشفيع فيما إذا باع داراً فسلَّمَ الشفيع شفعته ثم تقايلا ، وعاد المبيعُ إلى ملك البائع ، ولو كانت الإقالة فسخاً في غيرهما لما كان له ذلك.
His statement: 'It is rescission in respect of the two contracting parties... etc.' because it indicates rescission and lifting, and the principle in speech is that it is taken according to its reality, so it is not taken as a sale because it is its opposite, since it (iqalah) expresses lifting and removal, while sale expresses establishment. So it is determined that it is invalid to treat it as a sale. As for its being a sale in respect of others, it is figurative because the ruling of a sale is established by it, which is ownership. And since it is a rescission in respect of the two contracting parties, any increase or decrease from the first price is void. And if the seller sells the sold item to the buyer before taking it back from him, it is permissible; if it were a sale, it would not be permissible due to being before taking possession. And since it is a new sale in respect of others, preemption becomes obligatory for the preemptor in the case where he sold a house, and the preemptor waived his preemption, then they rescinded, and the sold item returns to the seller's ownership; if iqalah were a rescission in respect of others, he would not have that (preemption).
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage explains the dual nature of iqalah: it is a rescission (faskh) between the original parties, but a new sale (bay') from the perspective of third parties. The commentator argues linguistically: the word 'iqalah' literally means lifting, which is the opposite of establishing (as in sale). Therefore, between the parties, it must be treated as rescission, not a sale. This has practical consequences: any increase or decrease from the original price is invalid (bati), because the contract is canceled entirely, not replaced. Also, the seller can resell the item to the buyer before taking it back from him, because ownership reverts immediately upon iqalah; if it were a sale, reselling before taking possession (qabd) would be invalid. As for third parties, such as a preemptor (shafi'), iqalah is considered a new sale. Example: A sells a house to B. The preemptor (C) waives his right of preemption. Then A and B rescind the sale (iqalah), and the house returns to A. Later, C cannot claim preemption again because the iqalah is treated as a new sale between A and B, and C already waived his right. If iqalah were a rescission regarding third parties, C could claim preemption on the return of property, but he cannot. Thus, the Hanafi ruling is nuanced: for the parties, it is faskh (no new warranty period, etc.), but for third parties, it is treated as a bay' with its own consequences.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqalah): Mutual rescission of a sale contract by the parties, canceling the exchange and reverting ownership.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of a contract, returning to original state. In iqalah, it is the primary nature between the parties.
شفعة (shuf'ah): Preemption right of a co-owner or neighbor to buy property sold to an outsider. Here, it becomes relevant because iqalah is treated as a new sale for third parties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a seller, after iqalah with the buyer, sells the same item to the buyer again before taking it back?
A) Invalid because the seller does not possess the item before actually taking it back.
B) Valid because iqalah immediately returns ownership to the seller.
C) Valid only if the buyer still has the item in his possession.
D) Invalid because it requires a new contract of sale with proper offer and acceptance.

Key Takeaway
Iqalah has a hybrid nature: rescission for the parties (canceling the contract) but a new sale for third parties (triggering preemption and other rights).

The Legal Nature of Iqalah: Faskh or New Sale?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on iqalah classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on iqalah and apply the practical differences to cases involving separated increase or preemption.

Core Idea
Iqalah is faskh for the parties but a new sale for third parties—a dual nature with real legal effects.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: is the mutual rescission of a sale (iqalah) a rescission (faskh) or a new sale? The answer determines crucial practical consequences such as preemption rights and validity in the face of separated increases. The commentator presents three positions within the Hanafi school, with Abu Hanifa's view as the most relied-upon.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling of Abu Hanifa
State that according to Abu Hanifa, iqalah is a rescission (faskh) with respect to the two contracting parties, but a new sale with respect to a third party. This dual nature is the foundation for the entire discussion.
The third party can be a preemptor (shafi') or Allah (in the case of istibra').
2
Understand the linguistic reasoning
Examine why iqalah cannot be a sale for the parties: the word 'iqalah' literally means annulment and removal (raf'), while sale means establishment (ithbat). Since the literal meaning is clear and oppositional, it must be taken as faskh.
The principle: al-asl fi al-kalam an yuhmala 'ala haqiqatih (the default in speech is literal meaning).
3
Recognize the practical reason for treating as sale for third parties
For third parties, the effect of iqalah is a transfer of ownership, which is the essence of sale. Thus, it is treated as a new sale wherever third-party rights or duties are involved, such as preemption (shuf'ah) and istibra'.
This is a figurative (ma'nawi) sale, not a literal one.
4
Compare the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Abu Yusuf holds that iqalah is primarily a sale; only if sale is impossible (e.g., before possession in movables) does it become faskh. Muhammad reverses the order: first faskh, then sale. Memorize the hierarchy for each imam.
Imam al-Shafi'i in his new view agrees with Muhammad.
5
Apply the differences to a concrete case
Consider a sold slave who gives birth after possession. Per Abu Hanifa, the separated increase prevents faskh, so iqalah is void. Per Abu Yusuf, if iqalah can be a sale, it is valid. Work through the reasoning step by step.
Also consider preemption: if the preemptor waived his right before iqalah, Abu Hanifa's ruling revives it because iqalah is a new sale for third parties.

It is a rescission with respect to the two contracting parties and a sale with respect to a third party. (It is a rescission with respect to the two contracting parties and a sale with respect to a third party) It indicates reluctance; the iqalah is not completed, and it is recommended due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever rescinds a sale for a Muslim, Allah will rescind his slip' - reported by Abu Dawud, and Ibn Majah added 'on the Day of Resurrection', and it is narrated by Ibn Hibban in his Sahih and by al-Hakim who said it meets the conditions of the two shaykhs, and according to al-Bayhaqi: 'Whoever rescinds for a regretful person'. Thus in al-Fath. And it becomes obligatory in every disliked and invalid contract, as in al-Bahr, and in the case where the seller has slightly deceived [the buyer]. As in al-Nahr. And there are lengthy discussions here deposited in al-Bahr, al-Nahr, and other extensive works.

His saying: 'rescission with respect to...' because it indicates annulment and removal, and the principle in speech is to take it in its literal meaning, so it is not taken as a sale because it is its opposite, since it [iqalah] expresses removal and elimination, while sale expresses affirmation; thus carrying it as a sale is invalid.

As for it being a sale with respect to others, it is figurative, because by it the ruling of sale is established, which is ownership. Since it is a rescission with respect to the two contracting parties, any increase or decrease from the first price is void in wording. If the seller sells the sold item to the buyer before taking it back from him, it is permissible; if it were a sale, it would not be permissible because it would be before possession.

Since it is a new sale with respect to others, preemption becomes obligatory for the preemptor in the case where he sold a house, the preemptor surrendered his preemption, then they rescind, and the sold item returns to the seller's ownership. If iqalah were a rescission with respect to others, he would not have that.

Book of Sales / Chapter of Iqalah and Murabahah and Tawliyah

And if it becomes void after the birth of the sold female slave

Iqalah is a rescission with respect to the two contracting parties, a new sale with respect to others according to Abu Hanifa. If it cannot be made a rescission with respect to them, it becomes void. The benefit of it being a sale with respect to a third party is that it necessitates preemption by iqalah, for the preemptor is a third party to them, and it necessitates istibra', for it is a right of Allah, so Allah is a third party to them. According to Abu Yusuf, it is a sale; if it cannot be made a sale, it is made a rescission; if it cannot be made, it becomes void. According to Muhammad, the opposite.

(And it becomes void after the birth of the sold female slave)

[1] His saying: 'if it cannot be made a rescission' - such as if the sold female slave gives birth after possession, because the separated increase prevents the rescission of the contract as a right of the Shari'ah, as its verification has passed. [2] His saying: 'it becomes void' - due to the impossibility of rescission due to the separated increase after possession as a right of the Shari'ah, not before at all. Thus in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and others. [3] His saying: 'it is a sale' - with respect to the seller and the buyer. So if the sold item increases after possession with a separate increase, iqalah is permissible according to him, because it is a transfer of ownership from both sides with financial compensation which is the sold item, and consideration is for meanings not bare words, except that in movable property before possession, if it is considered a sale it would be invalid, so it is considered a rescission to carry their words on validity. Whatever is a sale becomes void by the destruction of the goods with the buyer, and the sold item is returned to the buyer due to defect, and preemption is established through it, because these are rulings of sale. If it cannot be made a sale - such as before possession in movables, or after the destruction of one of the two considerations in barter - it is made a rescission because it is positioned for that or possible. If it cannot be made either a rescission or a sale - such as before possession in movables with more than the first price or less, or with another type, or after the destruction of the goods in non-barter transactions - the iqalah becomes void according to him, and the first sale remains as it was, because the sale of movables before possession is not permissible, and rescission is with the first price, while they named something else. [4] His saying: 'according to Muhammad is the opposite of this' - this is also the saying of al-Shafi'i in his new view, and
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage expands on the legal nature of iqalah (rescission/retraction of a sale), a key issue in Hanafi contract law. The commentator begins by stating the central ruling: iqalah is a faskh (rescission) with respect to the two contracting parties but a new sale with respect to a third party. This dual nature has practical consequences. The passage then provides the linguistic and legal reasoning: the word 'iqalah' literally means annulment and removal, which is opposite to the meaning of sale (ithbat/establishment), so it must be taken as faskh when referring to the parties. However, with respect to third parties (like the preemptor or the right of Allah in istibra'), its effect is like a sale because it transfers ownership. The text supports the recommendation of iqalah with a hadith: 'Whoever rescinds a sale for a Muslim, Allah will rescind his slip' and variants, cited from Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, Ibn Hibban, etc. Iqalah becomes obligatory (wajib) in cases of disliked or void contracts or when the seller has slightly deceived. The core of the passage is the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Abu Hanifa rules that iqalah is primarily faskh; if faskh is impossible (e.g., due to a separated increase like birth after possession), it becomes void. Abu Yusuf considers it a sale; if that fails, then faskh; otherwise void. Muhammad al-Shaybani reverses this order. The practical difference appears in cases like a separated increase or preemption rights. The text also notes that Imam al-Shafi'i (in his new view) agrees with Muhammad's position. The commentator then discusses examples: if the sold item gives birth after possession, the separate increase prevents faskh, so the iqalah fails per Abu Hanifa, but per Abu Yusuf, if it can be considered a sale it is valid. The discussion shows the Hanafi method of prioritizing meanings over bare words and the effort to uphold the validity of parties' intentions.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqālah): The cancellation or rescission of a sale by mutual agreement of the parties, returning the goods and price.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment; termination of a contract retroactively, as if it never existed.
بيع (bayʿ): Sale; a contract transferring ownership of a thing for a price.
شفعة (shufʿah): Preemption; the right of a co-owner or neighbor to buy sold property under certain conditions.
استبراء (istibra'): Waiting period to ensure a female slave or animal is not pregnant before transfer; here it is a right of Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Iqalah is primarily a rescission (faskh) between the original parties but effects a new sale for third parties, a nuanced distinction with practical consequences.

The Nature of Iqalah: Faskh or Bay'? Effect of Separate Increase
Explaining the nature of iqalah as faskh or bay' and effect of offspring

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on whether iqalah is a rescission or a sale, and apply the ruling to a case where a separate increase (e.g., offspring) occurs after delivery.

Core Idea
Iqalah is faskh between parties but bay' for third parties—a fictional sale to protect third-party rights.

This lesson examines a critical intra-Hanafi dispute: is iqalah (mutual rescission of a sale) a rescission (faskh) or a new sale (bay')? The answer determines the validity of iqalah when a separate increase, such as the birth of an offspring, occurs after the buyer takes delivery. You will learn the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and the practical consequences for pre-emption (shuf'ah) and istibra'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The two main questions: (1) What is the legal nature of iqalah for the original parties vs. third parties? (2) How does a separate increase (e.g., a child born to a sold slave) affect the possibility of iqalah?
A separate increase is one that is physically distinct, like offspring or picked fruit.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that iqalah is a rescission (faskh) between the contracting parties but a new sale (bay') with respect to third parties. If faskh is impossible due to a separate increase after delivery, the iqalah becomes void.
The benefit of treating it as a sale vis-à-vis third parties is to establish pre-emption and istibra'.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf considers iqalah a sale in all respects. If that is not possible (e.g., before delivery in movables), it becomes a rescission; if that too is impossible, it is void.
He prioritizes the meaning (sale) over the literal term (iqalah).
4
State Muhammad al-Shaybani's position
Muhammad reverses Abu Hanifa: iqalah is primarily a rescission, and only becomes a sale when rescission is impossible.
This is also the view of al-Shafi'i in his new opinion.
5
Apply to the case of a separate increase after delivery
According to Abu Hanifa, if a sold slave gives birth after delivery, iqalah is void because the separate increase prevents faskh. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, iqalah is valid as a sale because the increase does not prevent a sale.
The practical difference is whether the rescission is allowed or the original sale continues.

[٤] قوله: وعند محمد رحمه الله عكس هذا؛ وهذا قول الشافعي رحمه الله في الجديد، وقولُ
و صحت بمثل الثمن الأول، وإن شرط غير جنسه أو أكثر منه هذا تفريع كونها فسخاً إذ بعد الولادة لا يمكن الفسخ فتبطل عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما لا تبطل؛ لأنها تكون بيعاً.
وصحت بمثل الثمن الأول، وإن شرط غير جنسه أو أكثر منه، إذا تقايلا على غير جنس الثمن الأول، أو على أكثر منه، فعند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله يجب الثمن الأول زفر، فهي فسخ إن كانت بالثمن الأول؛ لأنَّ الإقالة موضوع للفسخ والرفع، يقال في الدعاء: اللهم أ قلني عثرتي، فيعمل بمقتضاه. فإن لم يمكن جعلها فسخاً بأن كانت بعد القبض بالثمن الأول بعد الزيادة المنفصلة، أو بعد القبض بخلافِ جنس الثمن الأول، أو بعد القبض بأكثر من الثمن الأول فتجعل بيعاً حملاً على محتمله؛ صيانة لكلام العاقل عن الإلغاء، ولهذا صار بيعاً في حق غيرهما لعدم ولايتهما عليه. فإن لم يمكن جعلها بيعاً وفسخاً بأن كانت قبل القبض خلاف جنسِ الثَّمَنِ الأَوَّلِ تبطل الإقالة، ويبقى البيعُ الأوَّلُ على حاله؛ لأنَّ الفسخ لا يكونُ على خلافِ الثَّمَنِ الأول، والبيع لا يجوز قبل القبض، وبالأقل من الثمن يكون فسخاً عنده بالثمن الأول؛ لأنه سكوت عن بعض الثَّمن، ولو سكت عن الكل كان فسخاً فكذا إذا سكت عن البعض.
[١] قوله: فتبطل؛ لامتناع الفسخ بسبب الزيادة، ولو كانت بيعاً محضاً لجاز، قالوا: هذا إذا ولدت بعد القبض، وأما إذا ولدت قبله فالإقالة صحيحة عنده، وأما المتصلة كالسمن والجمال وغيرهما، فيصح قبل القبض أيضاً، كما صرحوا به. [٢] قوله: وصحت بمثل...الخ؛ إلا إذا باع المتولي والوصي للوقف، أو الصغير شيئاً بأكثر من قيمته، أو اشتريا شيئاً بأقل منها للوقف أو للصغير، حيث لا تجوز الإقالة إن كان بمثلِ الثَّمَنِ الأَوَّلِ رعاية لجانب الوقف وحق الصغير. كذا في «المنح»، وينبغي أن تجوز على نفسه في مسألة البيع. كما في «رد المحتار».
Book of Sale / Chapter of Rescission (Iqalah), Selling at Profit (Murabahah), and Delegation (Tawliyah)
Iqalah is a rescission (faskh) with respect to the two contracting parties, and a new sale (bay') with respect to others according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him). If it is not possible to make it a rescission with respect to the two, it becomes void. The benefit of it being a sale with respect to a third party is that the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) becomes obligatory through iqalah, because the pre-emptor is a third party to them, and istibra' (waiting for a period to ensure the womb is clean) becomes obligatory because it is a right of Allah Most High, so Allah is a third party to them. According to Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him), it is a sale. If it is not possible to make it a sale, it is made a rescission; if not possible, it becomes void. According to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), the opposite of this.
(It becomes void after the birth of the sold item's offspring)
Because
[1] His saying: If it is not possible to make it a rescission, such as when the sold item gives birth to an offspring after delivery, because the separate increase prevents rescission of the contract as a right of the Shari'ah, as its verification has passed earlier. [2] His saying: It becomes void; due to the impossibility of rescission due to the separate increase after delivery as a right of the Shari'ah, not before it absolutely. Thus in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and others. [3] His saying: It is a sale; with respect to the seller and buyer. So if the sold item increases after delivery with a separate increase, iqalah is valid according to him, because it is a transfer of ownership (tamlik) from both sides in exchange for valuable consideration ('iwad mali) which is the sold item, and consideration is for meanings not bare words. Except that in movable property before delivery, if it were construed as a sale, it would be void, so it is construed as a rescission to construe their words as valid. And what is a sale is void by the perishing of the commodity with the buyer, and the sold item is returned due to defect, and pre-emption is established by both because these are rulings of sale. If it is not possible to make it a sale, such as before delivery in movables, or after the perishing of one of the two countervalues in exchange (muqayadah), it is made a rescission because it is placed for it or it can bear it.
If it is not possible to make it either a rescission or a sale, such as before delivery in movables for more than the first price or less than it, or for a different type, or after the perishing of the commodity in other than exchange, iqalah becomes void according to him, and the first sale remains as it was; because a sale of movable property before delivery is not permissible, and rescission is with the first price, and they have specified other than that.
[4] His saying: And according to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) the opposite of this; and this is the saying of al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) in his new opinion, and the saying of
And it is valid with the like of the first price, even if they stipulate other than its type or more than it. This is a branching of it being a rescission, because after birth rescission is not possible, so it becomes void according to Abu Hanifa, and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it does not become void because it becomes a sale.
And it is valid with the like of the first price, even if they stipulate other than its type or more than it. If they make iqalah on other than the type of the first price, or on more than it, then according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) the first price is obligatory as a surplus (zafar), so it is a rescission if it is with the first price; because iqalah is placed for rescission and removal, it is said in supplication: O Allah, rescind (aqilni) my slip, so it is acted upon accordingly. If it is not possible to make it a rescission, such as after delivery with the first price after a separate increase, or after delivery with other than the type of the first price, or after delivery with more than the first price, then it is made a sale, construing it upon its possibility; to safeguard the speech of a sane person from being nullified, and for this reason it becomes a sale with respect to others because they have no authority over them. If it is not possible to make it either a sale or a rescission, such as before delivery with other than the type of the first price, the iqalah becomes void and the first sale remains as it was; because rescission cannot be upon other than the first price, and a sale before delivery is not permissible. And with less than the price, it is a rescission according to him with the first price; because it is silence about part of the price, and if he were silent about all of it, it would be a rescission, so likewise if he is silent about part.
[1] His saying: So it becomes void; due to the prevention of rescission because of the increase, and if it were a pure sale it would be permissible. They said: This is if it gives birth after delivery. But if it gives birth before delivery, then iqalah is valid according to him. As for connected increases like fatness, beauty, etc., it is valid before delivery as well, as they have stated. [2] His saying: And it is valid with the like...etc.; except if the trustee (mutawalli) and executor (wasi) for the endowment (waqf), or for a minor, sell something for more than its value, or buy something for less than its value for the endowment or the minor, then iqalah is not permissible if it is with the like of the first price, out of consideration for the endowment and the right of the minor. Thus in al-Manh, and it should be permissible by himself in the matter of sale. As in Radd al-Muhtar.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi commentary on the nature of iqalah (rescission of sale) and its validity when a separate increase, such as the birth of an offspring, occurs after delivery of the sold item. The author explains that iqalah is fundamentally a rescission (faskh) between the original parties but is treated as a new sale (bay') with respect to third parties, according to Abu Hanifa. This distinction has practical consequences: if iqalah is considered a sale, it gives rise to the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) for a neighbour or partner (since the pre-emptor is a third party), and also necessitates istibra' (waiting one menstrual cycle) for a female slave sold, because that is a right of Allah. The author then presents the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Abu Yusuf considers iqalah a sale in all respects, but if that is not possible (e.g., before delivery in movables), it becomes a rescission; if that too is impossible, it is void. Muhammad reverses this: iqalah is primarily a rescission, and only becomes a sale when necessary. The core issue is the effect of a separate increase (like a child born after the buyer took possession). According to Abu Hanifa, such an increase prevents rescission because it is a new right created by the Shari'ah, so iqalah becomes void. However, his disciples hold that iqalah can still be valid as a sale because the increase does not prevent a sale. The commentary also discusses scenarios involving different prices or types of consideration, emphasizing that the legal nature of iqalah is determined by the possibility of construing it as rescission or sale, always aiming to give effect to the parties' words. Finally, an exception is noted for fiduciaries selling or buying for a waqf or minor to prevent exploitation.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-iqālah): Rescission of a sale contract by mutual agreement, returning the sold item to the seller and the price to the buyer.
الزيادة المنفصلة (al-ziyādah al-munfaṣilah): A separate increase, such as a child born or fruit picked, that is physically distinct from the original sold item.
الشفعة (al-shufʿah): The right of pre-emption allowing a co-owner or neighbour to purchase a sold property on the same terms.
الاستبراء (al-istibrāʾ): The waiting period to ensure a female slave's womb is empty before she is sold, to avoid mixing lineage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a sold slave gives birth after delivery, what is the ruling on iqalah?
A) Valid as a rescission.
B) Valid as a new sale.
C) Void because rescission is prevented by the separate increase.
D) Void only if the offspring is a male.

Key Takeaway
Iqalah is a dual-nature contract: rescission between the parties but sale for third parties, and a separate increase after delivery blocks rescission but not sale.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
The Nature of Iqala: When the Price Changes – An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on iqala with varying price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani on iqala when the second price differs in type or amount, and apply the ruling to determine validity in scenarios involving timing and defects.

Core Idea
Iqala is a rescission by nature, but when the price changes, the Hanafi masters diverge: is it void, a rescission, or a new sale?

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute: what happens when a buyer and seller mutually agree to cancel a sale, but the second price is not the same as the original? The answer depends on whether the change is in type, amount, or timing, and on which imam's view is followed. We will walk through the scenarios and understand the legal reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core nature of iqala according to Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa holds that iqala is a rescission (fasakh), not a new sale. Therefore, the condition for a valid iqala is that it must be with the exact first price. Any deviation challenges the rescission.
The linguistic root of iqala means 'to lift' or 'remove', supporting the idea of undoing the contract.
2
Analyze the Sahibayn's starting point
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani generally treat iqala as a sale (bay') when the price differs. Abu Yusuf holds that iqala with a different price is always a new sale. Muhammad makes a distinction: if rescission is still possible (e.g., price is less, as silence about part), it remains a rescission; if not (more or different type), it becomes a sale.
This difference stems from their view on the primary nature of transactions: sale is the default.
3
Apply the rulings to scenarios of different price type or more
If the parties stipulate a price of a different type (e.g., same amount but different currency) or a larger amount: Abu Hanifa says iqala is void if before receipt (because rescission is impossible and sale cannot be before receipt), but after receipt it becomes a valid sale (since rescission is impossible and sale is possible). The Sahibayn say it is always a valid new sale, regardless of timing.
The commentary explains that Abu Hanifa's conversion to sale protects the speech of the contracting parties from being nullified.
4
Examine the special case of a lesser price
When the second price is less than the first: Abu Hanifa considers this a rescission with the first price, because silence about part of the price is like silence about all (like a complete forgiveness). Abu Yusuf treats it as a new sale for the lesser amount. Muhammad agrees with Abu Hanifa that it is a rescission, as silence about part is analogous to silence about all.
The exception is when the goods have become defective – then reduction is allowed.
5
Consider the defect exception and guardian's situation
If the goods have a defect after receipt, iqala for less than the original price is permissible by all, because the reduction compensates for the loss due to the defect. Additionally, if the parties are guardians (for waqf or minor), iqala with the same price may be prohibited to protect their interests, as explained in the margin.
These exceptions show that the ruling is not absolute but considers preservation of rights and justice.

Likewise in the case of less than it, unless it has become defective, then that (the less) is obligatory. Because according to him (Abu Hanifa), iqala is a rescission, and rescission is only upon the first price, so that condition is a null condition, and iqala is not invalidated by a null condition. So the iqala is valid and the condition becomes void. According to the two (Sahibayn), it becomes a sale with that named price. And likewise in the case of less than it, unless it has become defective, then that (the first price) is obligatory: meaning the first price becomes obligatory if they rescind for less than it, unless it has become defective, then the less becomes obligatory, and this is according to Abu Hanifa. According to Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him), it becomes a sale for the less, because according to him the principle is that it is a sale. According to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), it becomes a rescission with the first price, because it is silence about part of the first price, and if he were silent about all and rescinded, it would be a rescission, so this is more appropriate. (1) His statement: 'because iqala...' means because iqala is a rescission according to the Imam, and it only occurs upon the first price, so that condition becomes superfluous and null, and iqala is not invalidated by a null condition, because the invalidation of a sale by a null condition is due to the necessity of riba, and there is no riba in rescission. (2) His statement: 'According to the two, it becomes a new sale with that named price' because the principle according to Abu Yusuf is that it is a sale; according to Muhammad, if rescission becomes impossible, then making it a sale is possible, so when he increases or stipulates a different type, he intends a sale. [3] His statement: 'unless it has become defective' is an exception from his statement 'likewise in the case of less'; because then iqala is permissible for less than the first price, because the reduction in price is in exchange for the loss due to the defect, and the reduction must be equal to the share of what is lost, and it is not permissible to reduce more than that. This is in al-Fath and others. And in al-Bahr, quoting from some commentaries of al-Hidayah: It is narrated from Taj al-Shari'ah that this is when the share of the defect is equal to the reduction, or more or less within the range of minor discrepancy. [4] His statement: 'so this is more appropriate'; and it was objected that his being a rescission when silent about all the price is either specifically according to his madhhab or by agreement. The first is qiyas of a differing view upon a differing view, and the first is not valid because Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) only makes it a rescission due to impossibility of making it a sale.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah discusses the legal ruling on iqala (mutual rescission of a sale) when the parties agree on a price different from the original sale price. It is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The passage opens by stating that iqala is valid if it is for the same price. If a different type of price or a larger amount is stipulated, this branches off from the nature of iqala as a rescission. Abu Hanifa holds that iqala is fundamentally a rescission (fasakh), so it can only occur with the original price. After delivery (especially in the case of a pregnant animal giving birth), rescission is impossible, so the iqala becomes void. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, saying it does not void because it becomes a new sale. The passage then elaborates: if the parties rescind with a different type of price or more, according to Abu Hanifa, the first price is obligatory (i.e., the iqala is considered with the first price) if possible; it is a rescission if with the first price. If not possible (e.g., after receipt with a separate increase), it becomes a sale. If neither rescission nor sale is possible (e.g., before receipt with a different type), the iqala voids and the original sale remains. For a lesser price, it is a rescission with the first price because silence about part is like silence about all. The commentary then adds exceptions: if the parties are guardians dealing with waqf or minor's property, iqala with the same price is not allowed to protect their interests. Then the passage discusses when the goods have become defective: then iqala for less than the first price is permissible, and the reduction must correspond to the defect. The passage ends with an objection and discussion about the basis of Muhammad's view. Throughout, the commentary explains the reasoning behind each imam's position, using principles like the protection of speech from nullification and the impossibility of rescission after certain changes.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqālah): Mutual cancellation/rescission of a sale contract by the consent of both parties, returning the goods and price to their original owners.
فسخ (fasakh): Annulment or rescission of a contract, restoring the pre-contract status.
بيع (bay'): Sale contract involving exchange of property or rights for compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling of Abu Hanifa on iqala when the parties agree on a lesser price than the original (without a defect)?
A) It is void.
B) It is a rescission with the first price.
C) It is a new sale for the lesser price.
D) It is valid only if the difference is minor.

Key Takeaway
The ruling on iqala with a changed price divides the Hanafi school: rescission is possible only with the original price; otherwise, it becomes a new sale or void depending on timing and the imam's view.

Rescission for Less Than Original Price: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on rescission for less than original price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three Hanafi positions on rescission for less than the original price and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of each.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: rescission is revocation, invalid condition disregarded; Sahibayn split on whether it's a new sale or revocation.

When two parties agree to cancel a sale but at a lower price than originally paid, is that a valid rescission or a new sale? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples differ. This lesson walks through each position, the reasoning behind it, and the practical outcome in cases with or without defects in the goods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed point
The transaction is an iqālah (rescission) where the parties agree on a price less than the original. The dispute: is this a valid faskh (revocation) on the original price, or does it create a new sale at the lower price?
Focus on the nature of iqālah: faskh or bay'.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and dalīl
Abu Hanifa: iqālah is faskh; faskh can only be on the original price. The condition for less is a void condition (shart fāsid), but faskh is not invalidated by a void condition (unlike sale where riba may arise). Thus iqālah is valid, condition void, original price must be paid. Exception: if a defect (ayb) occurs, reduced price is allowed as compensation.
Key: void condition in faskh does not spoil the contract.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and dalīl
Abu Yusuf: iqālah is originally a sale (bay') in his view. Therefore, the iqālah for less is a new sale at the named lower price. He does not recognize it as faskh unless faskh is impossible (e.g., if sale is not possible for some reason).
Abu Yusuf's principle: al-aṣl huwa al-bay'.
4
State Muhammad al-Shaybani's position and dalīl
Muhammad: iqālah is faskh on the original price. He argues that agreeing to less is like silence about part of the original price; if silence about all would be faskh, then silence about part is even more so (awlā). So it remains faskh with original price due, unless defect changes it.
Muhammad uses a qiyās awlawī (a fortiori analogy).
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequences: Under Abu Hanifa, parties must transact at original price unless defect; under Abu Yusuf, new sale at lower price; under Muhammad, same as Abu Hanifa but different reasoning. The exception for defect is agreed by all: reduced price is allowed to compensate for the defect.
The text mentions an objection to Muhammad's analogy but defends it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنَّ الإقالة فسخ عنده ، والفسخ لا يكون إلا على الثَّمَنِ الأَوَّلِ، فذلك الشَّرِطُ شرط فاسد، والإقالة لا تَفْسُدُ بالشَّرط الفاسد، فصحت الإقالة وبَطَلَ الشَّرط، وعندهما تكونُ بَيْعاً بذلك المُسْمَّى . وكذا في الأَقل منه إلا إذا تَعَيَّبَ فيجب ذلك : أي يَحِبُّ الثَّمنُ الأَوَّل إذا تقايلا على أقل منه ، إلا إذا تعيب فحينئذ يَحِبُ الأقل وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ، وعند أبي يوسف له يكونُ بَيْعاً بالأقل، فإِنَّ الأصل عنده أنَّه بَيْعٌ ، وعند محمد الله تكون فسخاً بالثَّمن الأول ؛ لأنَّه سكوت عن بعض الثَّمن الأوَّل، ولو سكت عن الكلِّ وأقال كان فسخاً ، فهذا أولى) قوله : لأنَّ الإقالة...الخ ؛ أي لأن الإقالة فسخ عند الإمام، وهو لا يكون إلا على الثّمن الأول، فيصير ذلك الشرط لغواً وفاسداً ، والإقالة لا تفسد بالشرط الفاسد ؛ لأنَّ فساد البيع بالشرط الفاسد يكون للزوم الربا، ولا رباً في الفسخ. قوله : وعندهما تكون بيعاً جديداً بذلك المسمى ؛ لأنَّ الأصل هو البيع عند أبي يوسف ، وعند محمد الله إن تعدَّرَ الفسخ فجعلُها بيعاً ممكن ، فإذا زادَ أو شرط خلاف الجنس كان قاصداً للبيع. قوله : إلا إذا تعيب ؛ استثناء من قوله : وكذا في الأقل ؛ فإنَّ الإقالة حينئذ تجوزُ بأقل من الثمن الأوّل ؛ لأنَّ نقصان الثمن يكون بمقابلة الفائت بالعيب، ولا بُدَّ أن يكون النقصان بقدر حصة الفائت، ولا يجوز أن ينقص من الثمن أكثر منه. كذا في «الفتح»، وغيره. وفي «البحر» نقلاً عن بعض شروح الهداية : إنه نقل عن تاج الشريعة هذا إذا كانت حصة العيب مقدار المحطوط أو زائداً أو ناقصاً بقدر ما يتغابن الناس فيه. قوله : فهذا أولى ؛ واعترض عليه بأنّ كونه فسخاً إذا سكت عن كل الثمن، إما أن يكون على مذهبه خاصة أو على الاتفاق ، والأول قياس المختلف على المختلف، والأوَّلُ غيرُ ناهض ؛ لأنَّ أبا يوسف له إنما يجعله فسخاً لامتناع جعله بيعاً. إلا إذا دَخَلَ عَيْبٌ، فإنَّه فسخ بالأقل.
Because rescission is a revocation according to him, and revocation is only upon the original price, so that condition is a void condition, and rescission is not invalidated by a void condition; thus the rescission is valid and the condition is void. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is a sale at that named price. Likewise in the less than it, except if a defect occurs, then that is necessary: meaning the original price is necessary if they rescind on less than it, except if a defect occurs, then the less is necessary—this according to Abu Hanifa. According to Abu Yusuf, it is a sale for the less, for the principle with him is that it is a sale. According to Muhammad, it is a revocation at the original price, because it is silence about part of the original price, and if he were silent about all and rescinded, it would be a revocation, so this is more appropriate. He said: 'Because rescission...' etc.; i.e., because rescission is revocation according to the Imam, and it is only upon the original price, so that condition becomes void and corrupt, and rescission is not invalidated by a corrupt condition because the corruption of a sale by a corrupt condition occurs due to the necessity of riba, and there is no riba in revocation. He said: 'And according to them it is a new sale at that named price' because the principle is sale according to Abu Yusuf, and according to Muhammad, if revocation is impossible, then making it a sale is possible, so if he increases or stipulates something contrary to the genus, he intends a sale. He said: 'Except if a defect occurs' is an exception from his saying 'likewise in the less'; for in that case rescission is permitted for less than the original price because the reduction in price is in exchange for what is lost due to the defect, and the reduction must be equal to the portion lost, and it is not permissible to reduce the price more than that. Thus in al-Fath and others. And in al-Bahr, quoting some commentaries of al-Hidayah: it is transmitted from Taj al-Shari'ah that this is when the portion of the defect is equal to the amount deducted, or more or less by the amount people usually overlook in bargaining. He said: 'So this is more appropriate'; and it is objected to it that his being a revocation when he is silent about the entire price is either according to his madhhab specifically or by agreement, and the first is analogizing a difference upon a difference, and the first is not valid because Abu Yusuf only makes it a revocation due to the impossibility of making it a sale. Except if a defect enters, then it is a revocation for the less.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding إقالة (rescission of sale) when the parties agree to rescind for less than the original price. The core issue is whether such an إقالة is valid as a فسخ (revocation) of the original sale or whether it constitutes a new بيع (sale). The text begins by explaining Imam Abu Hanifa's position: for him, الإقالة is a فسخ, and a فسخ can only be upon the original price. Therefore, if the parties stipulate a lower price, that condition is void (شرط فاسد), but the rescission itself remains valid; the condition is simply struck down. The ruling is that the original price must be paid. The text then states the exception: if the commodity has become defective (تعيب), then the rescission is valid at the reduced price, because the reduction compensates for the defect. Abu Yusuf, on the other hand, considers the entire transaction a new sale at the agreed lower price, since for him the original nature of إقالة is sale. Muhammad al-Shaybani takes a middle position: he holds it is a فسخ by the original price, because by agreeing to less, the parties are effectively silent on part of the price; and silence on all would be a فسخ, so silence on part is even more so. The commentary then elaborates: the reason an invalid condition does not spoil the rescission is that in sales, corrupt conditions lead to riba, but in فسخ there is no riba. For the exception of defect, the reduction must be proportional to the loss; exceeding it is not allowed. Later commenters add that if the defect portion is within the range people normally tolerate (يتغابن الناس فيه), it is acceptable. Finally, a objection is raised regarding Muhammad's analogy, but it is defended. The passage ends with a summary: if a defect appears, rescission is for the reduced price.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-iqālah): In this text: rescission of a sale contract by mutual agreement of the parties, releasing each other from the obligations.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Revocation or annulment of a contract, returning it to its pre-contract state.
الشرط الفاسد (al-sharṭ al-fāsid): A condition that is legally invalid and renders the contract corrupt in other schools, but here it does not invalidate the rescission because rescission is not a sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the exception to the rule that iqālah must be on the original price?
A) When the buyer is poor
B) When a defect occurs in the goods
C) When the seller agrees to a lower price
D) When the rescission is done after delivery

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, iqālah for less than the original price is valid, but the imams differ on whether it is a faskh (with original price due) or a new sale (at lower price), with the defect exception agreed by all.

Destruction of Price vs Commodity in Rescission
Ruling on whether destruction of the price or the commodity prevents the validity of rescission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the effect of destruction of the price versus the commodity on the validity of rescission and explain the legal reasoning behind the distinction.

Core Idea
Destruction of the commodity prevents rescission; destruction of the price does not — because the commodity is the core subject of the contract.

This lesson examines the impact of the destruction of either the price or the commodity on the validity of rescission (إقالة) in a sale contract. The Hanafi school makes a decisive distinction: while the loss of the price does not bar rescission, the loss of the commodity does. Understanding this distinction requires grasping the nature of the contract's subject matter and the logic of rescission as an annulment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (rescission) and the two scenarios: destruction of the price vs destruction of the commodity. State the ruling for each.
Rescission of a sale after destruction of the price is valid; after destruction of the commodity, it is invalid.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The passage provides a talil (reasoning): rescission is the lifting of the contract, which requires the contract to still exist. The contract's existence hinges on the commodity, not the price.
The price is treated as a mere description (waṣf), while the commodity is the primary subject (maḥall) of the contract.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the proof leads to the distinction: the commodity is essential for the contract's existence, so its destruction voids the sale and makes rescission impossible. The price is non-essential, so its destruction does not affect the contract's existence.
The analogy: the price is like a description; its loss does not destroy the substance being described.
4
Note the exception for partial destruction
If only part of the commodity is destroyed, rescission is invalid for that portion but valid for the remaining part. This treats the part like the whole.
This is consistent: rescission requires existence of the subject matter for the portion being rescinded.
5
Evaluate the practical consequence
Apply the ruling: if the commodity is destroyed, the buyer cannot return it; rescission is only possible if the commodity is still intact. If the price is destroyed, rescission can still proceed because the seller can receive a substitute for the price.
The practical difference: rescission returns both parties to pre-contract status; the commodity must be returnable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يمنعها هلاك الثَّمن بل المبيع ، وهلاك بعضهِ يَمْنَعُ بقدره). لانتفاء ذكر الثمن بخلاف صورةِ النقصان فإنّ فيها ما يصلح ثمناً، كذا في «العناية». قوله : ولم يمنعها ... الخ ؛ يعني إنَّ هلاك الثمن لا يمنع صحة الإقالة بخلاف هلاك المبيع، فإنّه يمنع عنها ؛ لأنَّ شرط صحة العقدِ قيام العقد، والإقالة رفع العقد، فتقتضي قيام البيع، وقيامه لا يكون إلا بالمبيع ؛ لكونه محل إضافة العقد دون الثمن ، فلو هلك المبيع قبل القبض يبطل البيع، بخلاف هلاكِ الثمن فإنّه بمنزلة الوصف فلم يشترط وجوده عند البيع. قوله : وهلاك بعضه ... الخ ؛ معنى إن هلك بعض المبيع لا تجوز الإقالة بقدره ، وتصح في الباقي اعتباراً للبعض بالكل.
And destruction of the price does not prevent it, but destruction of the commodity does; and destruction of part of it prevents to the extent of that part. Because the price is not mentioned, unlike the case of reduction where there is something suitable as a price. Thus in al-‘Inayah. He said: 'And destruction of the price does not prevent it... etc.'; meaning that destruction of the price does not prevent the validity of rescission, unlike destruction of the commodity, for it prevents it; because the condition for validity of the contract is the existence of the contract, and rescission is the lifting of the contract, so it requires the existence of the sale, and its existence is only through the commodity, since it is the subject of the contract's addition, not the price. So if the commodity is destroyed before delivery, the sale is invalidated, unlike destruction of the price, for it is like a description, so its existence is not required at the time of sale. He said: 'And destruction of part of it prevents to the extent of it'; meaning if part of the commodity is destroyed, rescission is not permissible to that extent, but is valid for the remainder, treating the part like the whole.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage explains the effect of destruction (هلاك) of either the price (ثمن) or the commodity (مبيع) on the validity of إقالة (rescission). The ruling is clear: destruction of the commodity prevents rescission entirely, because rescission is the annulment of a contract, and a contract can only be annulled if it still exists. The existence of the sale depends on the commodity being extant; the price is merely a description. Therefore, if the commodity is destroyed before delivery, the sale itself is void, and rescission is meaningless. However, if the price is destroyed, rescission is still valid because the price is not the core subject of the contract. If only part of the commodity is destroyed, rescission cannot take place for that portion, but it is valid for the remaining part. The commentary supports this by quoting al-‘Inayah: the destruction of the price does not prevent rescission because the price is not mentioned in the rescission in the same way as the commodity. The analogy is drawn with the case of a reduction in price (due to defect), where something remains suitable as a price. The reasoning is that rescission requires the continuation of the original sale; that continuation hinges on the existence of the commodity, not the price.

Key Terms
الهلاك (al-halāk): Complete destruction or loss of an item, making it impossible to return.
المبيع (al-mabīʿ): The commodity sold; the subject matter of the sale.
الثمن (al-thaman): The price paid for the commodity.
الإقالة (al-iqālah): Mutual rescission of a contract; cancellation by agreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Rescission is invalid entirely.
C) Rescission is not permissible for the destroyed part but is valid for the rest.
D) Rescission is valid only if the price is also destroyed.

Key Takeaway
Rescission is only valid if the commodity still exists; destruction of the price does not affect it.

Effect of Destruction of Price and Goods on Rescission
Ruling with proof on rescission conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on rescission (iqalah) when the price or goods are destroyed and apply the underlying qiyas reasoning.

Core Idea
Destruction of goods voids the sale, so rescission fails. Destruction of price does not—price is merely a description.

When a sale is concluded but the goods or price are destroyed before delivery, can the parties mutually agree to unwind the transaction? In this passage, the commentator explains the Hanafi position: the key is whether the essential object of the sale (the goods) still exists. The price is secondary and its destruction does not block rescission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage contrasts destruction of the price (thaman) versus destruction of the goods (mabi‘). Determine which prevents rescission and which does not.
Destruction of goods prevents; destruction of price does not.
2
Locate the underlying reasoning (ta‘lil)
Find the logical basis: rescission (iqalah) lifts the sale contract, so the contract must still exist. Existence of the sale depends on the goods, not the price.
The goods are the object of the contract; the price is a description.
3
Analyze the partial destruction case
If part of the goods is destroyed, rescission is invalid for that part but valid for the remainder, analogizing part to whole.
Partial destruction blocks rescission only for the destroyed portion.
4
Apply the qiyas structure
The reasoning is a qiyas: the original sale (asl) requires existence of goods (illa); rescission removes the contract (hukm). Therefore, if goods are destroyed, the sale no longer exists and rescission is impossible.
The price is treated as a mere description, not an essential element.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يمنعها هلاك الثمن بل المبيع، وهلاك بعضه يمنع بقدره. إلا إذا دخل عيب، فإنه فسخ بالأقل. (ولم يمنعها هلاك الثمن بل المبيع، وهلاك بعضه يمنع بقدره). لانتفاء ذكر الثمن بخلاف صورة النقصان فإن فيها ما يصلح ثمناً، كذا في «العناية». قوله: ولم يمنعها... الخ؛ يعني إن هلاك الثمن لا يمنع صحة الإقالة بخلاف هلاك المبيع، فإنه يمنع عنها؛ لأن شرط صحة العقد قيام العقد، والإقالة رفع العقد، فتقتضي قيام البيع، وقيامه لا يكون إلا بالمبيع؛ لكونه محل إضافة العقد دون الثمن، فلو هلك المبيع قبل القبض يبطل البيع، بخلاف هلاك الثمن فإنه بمنزلة الوصف فلم يشترط وجوده عند البيع. قوله: وهلاك بعضه... الخ؛ معنى إن هلك بعض المبيع لا تجوز الإقالة بقدره، وتصح في الباقي اعتباراً للبعض بالكل.
And the destruction of the price does not prevent it, but rather the destruction of the goods, and the destruction of part of it prevents to the extent of it. Except if a defect arises, then it is a rescission for the lesser. (And the destruction of the price does not prevent it, but rather the goods, and the destruction of part of it prevents to the extent of it). Because the mention of the price is not considered [in the contract], unlike the case of deficiency where there is something that suffices as a price, as in al-‘Inayah. He said: His statement: 'And it does not prevent it...' etc.; meaning that the destruction of the price does not prevent the validity of the rescission, unlike the destruction of the goods, for it prevents it, because the condition for the validity of the contract is the existence of the contract, and rescission is a lifting of the contract, thus it necessitates the existence of the sale, and its existence is only through the goods, since it is the object of the contract's attachment, not the price. So if the goods are destroyed before delivery, the sale becomes void, unlike the destruction of the price, for it is like a description, so its existence is not required at the time of the sale. He said: His statement: 'And the destruction of part of it...' etc.; meaning if part of the goods is destroyed, rescission is not valid for that portion, and it is valid for the remainder, analogizing the part to the whole.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah provides a ruling with proof regarding the effect of destruction (halak) of the price (thaman) versus the goods (mabi‘) on the validity of rescission (iqalah) of a sale. The text clarifies two points: 1) Destruction of the price does not prevent rescission because the price is merely a description of the sale, not its essential object. 2) Destruction of the goods prevents rescission entirely because the sale contract depends on the existence of the goods. If part of the goods is destroyed, rescission is invalid for that part but valid for the remainder, by analogy to the whole. The reasoning is based on the nature of the contract: rescission lifts the contract, so the contract must still exist at the time of rescission. The goods are the primary object of the contract, while the price is secondary.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqalah): Rescission of a sale by mutual agreement of buyer and seller.
هلاك (halak): Destruction or perishing of the subject matter before delivery.
مبيع (mabi‘): The goods sold, the object of sale.
ثمن (thaman): The price paid for the goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Rescission is invalid for the entire sale.
B) Rescission is valid only for the undestroyed portion.
C) Rescission is valid for the entire sale with compensation.
D) Rescission is invalid for the destroyed portion and valid for the rest.

Key Takeaway
Destruction of goods (mabi‘) prevents rescission because it voids the sale, while destruction of price (thaman) does not affect rescission.

Murabaha and Tawliya: Definitions and Legal Framework
Defines Murabaha and Tawliya with reasoning for chapter organization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definitions of Murabaha and Tawliya and identify the key condition that distinguishes them from each other and from Musawama and Wadi'a.

Core Idea
Murabaha sells with profit; Tawliya sells at cost — both require cost disclosure.

In this chapter, the commentator Al-Marghinani opens the discussion of Murabaha (sale with declared profit) and Tawliya (sale at cost). These two types of sale are placed after rescission because they are more general — they can occur with any owner, not only the original seller. The text also explains why Musawama (ordinary bargaining) and Wadi'a (sale at a loss) are omitted: one is too obvious, the other too rare. Let's walk through the precise definitions and the legal reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defined terms
Read the two definitions: Murabaha is selling at cost plus profit; Tawliya is selling at cost without profit. Note the Arabic phrases.
2
Understand the chapter placement
The author places these after rescission because rescission is exclusive to the seller, while Murabaha and Tawliya apply to any owner, including those who acquired goods by other means.
This shows the scope of these sales.
3
Analyze Murabaha's definition
The key phrase 'at its price' (bithamanihi) means the cost basis includes what the seller paid, plus permissible expenses like tailoring fees. The profit (fadl) can be of a different kind.
The text explicitly allows adding expenses even if not of the same type.
4
Analyze Tawliya's definition
Tawliya literally means making the buyer a guardian over the goods, transferring ownership at cost without any profit.
It is like a partnership in the purchase.
5
Contrast with omitted types
Musawama (bargaining) is the norm and omitted for being obvious; Wadi'a (sale at a loss) is rare because trade aims for profit.
This emphasizes the practical purpose of these definitions.

باب المرابحة والتولية المرابحة : بيع المشترى بثمنه وفضل، والتوليةُ : بيعه به بلا فضل. قوله : باب المرابحة والتولية ؛ لما فرغ المصنف له ما يتعلق بالأصل وهو المبيع من البيوع اللازمة وغير اللازمة وما يرفعهما، شرع في بيان الأنواع التي تتعلق بالثمن من المرابحة والتولية وغيرهما ، وأخَّرهما عن الإقالة ؛ لأنَّ الإقالة بمنزلة المفرد من المركب. فإن الإقالة إنما تكون مع البائع بخلاف التولية والمرابحة، فإنهما أعم من كونهما مع البائع وغيره، ولم يذكر المساومة : وهي البيع بأي ثمن اتفق ، وهو المعتاد والوضيعة : وهو تمليك المبيع بمثل الثمن مع نقصان ثمن يسير؛ لظهورهما ولندرة وقوع بيع الوضيعة، فإنَّ الغرض من البيعات الاسترباح لا النقصان. قوله : المرابحة؛ مصدرُ رابح : أي باغ المتاعَ أو اشترى مرابحة، إذا سمى لكل قدر من الثمن ربحاً. قوله : بيع المشترى ؛ بصيغة اسم المفعول ؛ أي ما شراه ، وهذا بناء على الغالب فإنّ من غصب عبداً أو أبق فقضى القاضي بالقيمة وأداها ثم عاد إلى الغاصب، فللغاصب أن يبيعه مرابحة على القيمة التي أدّاها ، كذا في فتاوى قاضي خان، فالمراد من المشترى ما يملكه من العروض ؛ لعدم جواز بيع الدراهم مرابحة. قوله : بثمنه : أي بما قام عليه، فإنّه سيجيء أنه له أن يضم أجرة القصار وغيره، ولم يقل بثمنه الأول ؛ لأنَّ ما يأخذه من المشتري ليس ثمنه الأول بل مثله. قوله : وفضل ؛ أي مع فضل على ما قام عليه، وإن لم يكن من جنسه كأجر قصار ونحوه. قوله : والتولية ؛ مصدر ولى غيره : إذا جعله والياً، فكأنَّ البائع جعل المشتري والياً فيما اشتراه. قوله : بيعه به بلا فضل : أي بيعُ المشتري بلا فضل ما شراء بثمنه ولو حكماً.
Chapter on Murabaha and Tawliya. Murabaha: sale of what has been purchased at its price with a profit. Tawliya: sale of it at its price without profit. He said: The statement: 'Chapter on Murabaha and Tawliya'; when the author finished what pertains to the principal, i.e., the goods, from binding and non-binding sales and what lifts them, he began to explain the types that relate to the price, such as Murabaha and Tawliya and others, and he placed them after rescission because rescission is like a single element from a compound. Rescission only occurs with the seller, unlike Tawliya and Murabaha, for they are more general, occurring with the seller or another. He did not mention Musawama: which is sale at any price agreed upon, and it is the usual practice, and Wadiah: which is transfer of ownership of the goods at the same price with a small reduction, because they are obvious and because sale with a loss is rare, for the purpose of sales is profit, not loss. He said: 'Murabaha' is a verbal noun of rabaha: meaning he sold goods or bought in a Murabaha transaction, if he named a profit for each portion of the price. He said: 'Sale of what has been purchased' is in the form of the passive participle; i.e., what he bought, and this is based on the usual case, for if someone usurps a slave or a runaway and the judge awards its value and he pays it, then the property returns to the usurper, he may sell it as Murabaha on the value he paid, as in the Fatawa of Qadi Khan. Thus what is meant by 'what has been purchased' is what he owns of goods, because it is not permissible to sell dirhams as Murabaha. He said: 'at its price': i.e., at what it cost him, for it will come that he may include the tailor's fee and other expenses, and he did not say 'at its first price' because what he takes from the buyer is not its first price but its equivalent. He said: 'with a profit': i.e., with a profit over what it cost him, even if it is not of the same kind, like the tailor's fee and similar. He said: 'Tawliya' is a verbal noun of walla ghayrahu: if he makes him a guardian, as if the seller makes the buyer a guardian over what he bought. He said: 'sale of it at its price without profit': i.e., selling what he bought without profit, at its price even if it is considered.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Murabaha (sale with profit) and Tawliya (sale at cost). The author explains why these topics are placed after rescission: rescission is a specific contract with the seller, while Murabaha and Tawliya are broader, applicable with the seller or any owner. The definitions are given: Murabaha is selling goods at a price that includes the cost plus a declared profit; Tawliya is selling at cost without profit. The commentary provides important details: the goods must have been purchased (or acquired by similar means, such as court-ordered payment), and the seller may include expenses like tailoring fees in the cost base. The profit need not be in the same form as the cost. The text also notes that Musawama (ordinary bargaining) and Wadiah (sale at a loss) are omitted due to being common practice or rare. Key terms are defined: 'Murabaha' denotes a transaction where profit is specified per unit of price; 'Tawliya' implies making the buyer a partner or guardian over the sale.

Key Terms
مرابحة (murabaha): A sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a specified profit.
تولية (tawliya): A sale where the seller transfers the goods at cost price, without profit.
مساومة (musawama): Ordinary sale by bargaining without reference to cost.
وضيعة (wadi'a): Sale at a loss, where the goods are sold for less than cost.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what distinguishes Murabaha from Tawliya?
A) Murabaha is with a profit, Tawliya is without profit.
B) Murabaha is with a loss, Tawliya is at cost.
C) Murabaha requires cash payment, Tawliya allows credit.
D) Murabaha is only for movable goods, Tawliya for real estate.

Key Takeaway
Murabaha and Tawliya are defined by cost disclosure: Murabaha adds profit, Tawliya does not.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner
6 min
Definition and Condition of Murabahah and Tawliyah
Defines Murabahah and Tawliyah and specifies the mithli condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define Murabahah and Tawliyah, state their key condition (homogeneous good/mithli), and apply the condition to determine validity.

Core Idea
Murabahah and Tawliyah are valid only for homogeneous goods (mithli); uncertainty in value invalidates the sale.

This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah defines two special types of sale—Murabahah (sale at cost plus profit) and Tawliyah (sale at cost without profit). The author explains that their validity depends on the item being a homogeneous good (mithli) because the buyer must be able to trust the seller's disclosure of cost. The passage also clarifies that the seller may include expenses like tailoring, dyeing, and transport in the cost price.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Read the definitions of Murabahah (cost plus profit) and Tawliyah (cost without profit). Note that both are trust-based sales where the buyer relies on the seller's disclosed cost.
Murabahah includes a known profit; Tawliyah has no profit.
2
Understand the condition of mithli
The passage states that the item must be homogeneous (mithli), such as dirhams, dinars, weighed, measured, or counted goods. If the item is heterogeneous (qimi), its value is uncertain, contradicting the trust basis.
Examples of mithli: grain, standard commodities. Examples of qimi: animals, jewels.
3
Recognize allowable additional costs
The seller can include costs like tailoring, dyeing, embroidery, and transport in the cost price. These are added to the original purchase price even if not of the same type.
This is because the cost price is what the seller actually incurred.
4
Analyze the rationale of trust and certainty
These sales rely on trust (amanah). If the item is mithli, the buyer can compare prices with known market equivalents. For qimi goods, value is only guesswork, leading to suspicion of dishonesty.
The text warns against betting on ribawi wealth; uncertainty (jahalah) is prohibited.

Chapter of Murabahah and Tawliyah
Murabahah: selling the purchased item at its price plus profit. Tawliyah: selling it at its price without profit.
He said: Chapter of Murabahah and Tawliyah; when the author finished what pertains to the original, that is the sold item, from binding and non-binding sales and what nullifies them, he began explaining the types that pertain to the price, from Murabahah, Tawliyah, and others. He delayed them after Iqalah because Iqalah is like a single component of a composite. For Iqalah occurs only with the seller, unlike Tawliyah and Murabahah, as they are more general, occurring with the seller or others. He did not mention Musawamah: selling at any agreed price, which is customary; and Wadi'ah: transferring ownership of the sold item at the like of the price with a slight decrease, because of their commonness and the rarity of Wadi'ah sales, since the purpose of sales is profit, not loss.
He said: Murabahah is a verbal noun from rabah: i.e., he sold merchandise or bought as Murabahah when he specifies for each portion of the price a profit.
He said: 'selling the purchased item' in the passive participle form: i.e., what he bought. This is based on the usual case, for if someone usurped a slave or a runaway (abiq) and the judge decreed the value and he paid it, then the slave returns to the usurper, the usurper may sell it as Murabahah on the value he paid, as in Fatawa Qadikhan. So the meaning of 'purchased' is what he owns of goods, because it is not permissible to sell dirhams as Murabahah.
He said: 'at its price': i.e., what it cost him, for it will come that he may include the tailor's wage and the like. He did not say 'at its first price' because what he takes from the buyer is not its first price but its like.
He said: 'and profit': i.e., with profit on what it cost him, even if not of its type, like the tailor's wage and the like.
He said: 'and Tawliyah' is a verbal noun from walla ghayrahu: when he makes him a wali (trustee). So it is as if the seller made the buyer a trustee over what he bought.
He said: 'selling it at it without profit': i.e., selling the purchased item without profit, at its purchase price, even if by way of legal fiction.
And their condition: that he bought it as a homogeneous good (mithli), and he may include the wage of the tailor, the dyer, the embroiderer, the twister, and the transport to its price.
Murabahah is that he stipulates that the sold item is at the first price at which he bought it with a known profit. Tawliyah is that he stipulates it at that price without profit.
And their condition: buying it as a homogeneous good. Because the benefit of these two sales is that the intelligent (buyer) trusts the actions of the clever (seller), so he binds himself to the like of what (the seller) bought it for, or the like with profit. This meaning appears only in homogeneous goods, not heterogeneous ones, because heterogeneous goods may be sought by their form without considering their monetary value. Also, the value is unknown, and these two sales are based on trust. And he may include the wage of the tailor, the dyer, the embroiderer, the twister, and the transport to its price.
He said: 'mithli' like dirhams and dinars, and what is measured, weighed, and counted in similar units; because if it were not homogeneous, like diverse things such as animals and jewels, it would be Murabahah at the value, which is unknown; because its knowledge is not possible in reality, so it is not permissible to sell it as Murabahah, unless the one buying as Murabahah is from the one who owns that substitute from the seller due to some cause.
The consideration in Murabahah is what the first contract was upon, not what was paid in exchange for it. So if it were for ten dirhams, and he paid a dinar or a garment worth ten or less or more, the capital is the ten (dirhams), not what was paid.
He said: The foundation of these two sales, Tawliyah and Murabahah, is trust and avoidance of betrayal and its suspicion. Equality and similarity in values are not known except by conjecture, so it resembles betrayal due to suspicion of similarity. For this reason, gambling in ribawi wealth is prohibited, as they have clarified.
He said: 'and he may include...' i.e., it is permissible for him to include the wage of the tailor and the dyer, whether the dye is black or otherwise.
'At-tiraz': it is with kasr of the ta', and ra' with 'tahqiq, and zay with 'i'jam: the embroidery of the garment.
'AL-fatil': it is with fatha of the fa': what is made on the edges of garments with silk or linen.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a definition and condition statement from the commentary on the chapter of Murabahah and Tawliyah. It begins by defining both types of sale: Murabahah is selling the purchased item at its cost price plus a stated profit, while Tawliyah is selling it at its cost price without any profit. The commentator explains the author's arrangement, noting that these chapters are placed after Iqaalah because the latter is limited to the original seller, whereas Murabahah and Tawliyah can be with the seller or another party. The commentary then clarifies the definition of 'purchased item'—it applies to goods, not cash like dirhams. The cost price (thaman) can include additional expenses like tailor's wages, even if not of the same type. The key condition for both sales is that the item must be a homogeneous good (mithli), such as dirhams, dinars, measured, weighed, or counted items that are similar. If the item is heterogeneous (like animals or jewels), its value is unknown, making the sale impermissible unless the buyer already owns the substitute. The passage also confirms that the seller may include manufacturing costs (tailor, dyer, embroiderer, twister, transport) in the cost price. The foundation of these sales is trust, which is undermined by the uncertainty of value in heterogeneous goods.

Key Terms
المرابحة (al-murabahah): A sale where the seller states the cost price and adds a known profit.
التولية (al-tawliyah): A sale where the seller transfers the item at the same cost price without profit.
المثلى (al-mithli): Homogeneous goods that can be replaced by similar units, e.g., dirhams, dinars, measured, weighed, or counted items.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key condition for the validity of a Murabahah or Tawliyah sale according to this passage?
A) The item must be a commodity that can be weighed.
B) The item must be a homogeneous good (mithli).
C) The seller must have purchased the item from the original manufacturer.
D) The profit must be less than 5% of the cost.

Key Takeaway
Murabahah and Tawliyah are trust-based sales valid only for homogeneous goods (mithli), with costs including manufacturing expenses.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
