Definition and Conditions of Murabahah and Tawliyah
Defines murabahah and tawliyah, their condition of mithli, and rules on additional costs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define murabahah and tawliyah, explain the condition of mithli, and identify which costs may be added to the capital.

Core Idea
Murabahah and tawliyah require the original purchase to be of a fungible good; only value-increasing costs may be added.

This lesson unpacks the definitions and key conditions of two trust-based sales: murabahah (cost-plus) and tawliyah (assignment at cost). You will learn why the Hanafi school insists on fungibility and how to distinguish permissible from impermissible added costs. Understanding these rules ensures valid transactions free from riba and deception.

Guided Path
1
Define the two contracts
State the definitions of murabahah and tawliyah as given in the text. Murabahah is selling at the original price plus known profit; tawliyah is selling at cost without profit.
Both rely on the seller's honesty in disclosing the original cost.
2
Identify the condition of mithli
The passage states the condition that the original purchase must be for a fungible good (mithli). Explain why: the naive buyer trusts the smart seller's cost, which only makes sense for items that are identical in kind.
Non-fungibles (qimiyyat) have subjective value and cannot be compared by cost alone.
3
List permissible added costs
Identify the costs that may be added to the capital: wages for laundering, dyeing, embroidering, thread-twisting, and carriage. These increase the item's substance or value.
The text emphasizes that the wage must be paid to a third party; doing it oneself or receiving it as a gift cannot be added.
4
Distinguish permissible from impermissible costs
The text lists costs that may NOT be added: shepherd's wage and storage rent. Explain why: they do not increase the substance or value of the item.
Other examples like plastering a house, lining a well, etc. are permissible because they increase value.
5
Recognize remedies for fraud
If fraud appears, in murabahah the buyer may keep the item at the stated price or return it; in tawliyah the buyer deducts the fraud amount according to Abu Hanifa.
The buyer's option depends on the type of trust-based sale.

Source Text

المرابحة هي أن يشترط أن المبيع بالثمن الأول الذي اشترى به مع فضل معلوم. والتولية : أن يشترط أنه بذلك الثمن بلا فضل. وشرطهما شراؤه بمثلى ؛ لأَنَّ فائدة هذين البيعين أن الغبي يعتمد على فعل الذكي، فيطلب نفسه بمثل ما اشترى به هو، أو بمثله مع فضل، وهذا المعنى إنما يظهر في ذوات الأمثال دون ذوات القيم ؛ لأنَّ ذوات القيم قد تطلب بصورتها من غير اعتبار ماليتها، وأيضاً القيمة مجهولة، ومبنى البيعين على الأمانة.
وله ضم أجر القصار، والصباغ، والطراز، والفتل، والحمل إلى ثمنه.
المراد بالقصار: غسل الثوب وتنظيفه. والصباغ: صبغ الثوب. والطراز: علم الثوب. والفتل: ما يصنع بأطراف الثياب بحرير أو كتان. والحمل: حمل المبيع من مكان إلى مكان براً كان أو بحراً إلى رأس المال؛ لأنَّ الأصل فيه أنَّ كل ما يزيد في المبيع أو قيمته كالصبغ والحمل يلحق به، وما لا فلا. والظاهر أنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ من الصبغ والفتل وغيرهما يزيد في العين باعتبار الاتصال، والحمل يزيد في القيمة؛ لأنها تكون مختلفة باختلاف الأمكنة.
وإنما قيد بالأجر؛ لأنه لو فعل شيئاً من ذلك بيده أو بإعارة لا يجوز أن يضمه إلى رأس المال. وكذا إذا تطوع متطوع بهذه الأشياء.
وكذا يضم أجر تجصيص الدار، وطي البئر، وكبرى الأنهار والقناة، والمسنضاة، والكراب، وكشح الكروم وسقيها والزرع، والغرس وسوق الغنم بخلاف أجرة الراعي، وكراء بيت الحفظ؛ لأنهما لا يزيدان في العين ولا في القيمة.
لكن يقول قام علي بكذا لا اشتريته بكذا؛ لأنه كذب، ولو ملكه بهبة أو إرث أو وصية، وقومه ثم باعه مرابحة على تلك القيمة يجوز.
قوله: فإن ظهر؛ بالبينة أو بإقرار البائع أو بنكوله عن اليمين، وهو المختار.
قوله: في المرابحة أخذه بثمنه أو رده؛ يعني أن المشتري في المرابحة بالخيار إن شاء أخذه بثمنه وهو المسمى، وإن شاء ردَّه إن أمكن الرد، وإن ظهر الخيانة في التولية حط المشتري قدر الخيانة، وهذا عند الإمام.
Murabahah is that he stipulates the sold item is at the first price he bought it for plus a known profit. Tawliyah is that he stipulates it is at that price without profit. Their condition is that he bought it for a fungible (mithli); because the benefit of these two sales is that the naive person relies on the action of the smart one, so he ties himself to the like of what he bought it for, or its like with profit, and this meaning appears only in fungibles, not in valuables; because valuables may be sought for their form without consideration of their monetary value, and also the value is unknown, and the basis of the two sales is trust. And he may add the wage of the launderer, the dyer, the embroiderer, the thread-twister, and the carriage to its price. By launderer is meant washing and cleaning the garment. Dyer: dyeing the garment. Embroiderer: the mark on the garment. Thread-twister: what is made at the edges of garments with silk or linen. Carriage: carrying the sold item from place to place, by land or sea, to the capital; because the principle in that is that everything that increases the sold item or its value, like dye and carriage, is attached to it, and what does not is not. The apparent is that each of dye, thread-twisting, etc. increases the substance by reason of attachment, and carriage increases the value because values differ with different locations. And it is restricted to the wage because if he did any of that with his own hand or by loan, it is not permissible to add it to the capital. Likewise if a volunteer volunteers these things. And likewise he adds the wage of plastering the house, lining the well, the beds of rivers and canals, the reservoirs, the cultivation, the trimming of vines and their irrigation, planting, and herding sheep, unlike the wage of the shepherd and the rent of the storage house because they do not increase the substance nor the value. But he says, 'It cost me such-and-such,' not 'I bought it for such-and-such,' because that is a lie. And if he owned it by gift or inheritance or bequest, and valued it, then sold it on murabahah at that value, it is permissible. His statement: 'If fraud appears' — by evidence or the seller's confession or his refusal to take an oath, and that is the chosen view. His statement: 'In murabahah, he takes it at its price or returns it' means the buyer in murabahah has an option: if he wishes, he takes it at its price (the named price), and if he wishes, he returns it if return is possible. If fraud appears in tawliyah, the buyer deducts the amount of the fraud, and this is according to Imam Abu Hanifa.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah defines two types of sales based on trust: murabahah (cost-plus sale) and tawliyah (assignment sale). Murabahah is when the seller states the original purchase price and adds a known profit margin. Tawliyah is when the seller states the original purchase price without any profit, essentially selling at cost. A key condition for both types is that the original purchase must have been for a fungible good (mithli), such as gold, silver, or standardized weights and measures. The reason is that these sales rely on the buyer's trust in the seller's honesty regarding the cost. With non-fungible goods (qimiyyat), the value is uncertain and varies, so stating the original price would not give the buyer reliable information. The commentator then explains that the seller may add certain costs to the capital price, such as wages for laundering, dyeing, embroidering, and carriage, because these increase the value or the physical substance of the item. However, costs that do not increase value, like shepherding or storage rent, cannot be added. The seller must also use truthful language: he should say 'this cost me' rather than 'I bought it for' to avoid deception. If fraud is discovered, the buyer in murabahah has the option to keep the item at the stated price or return it, while in tawliyah the buyer deducts the amount of fraud according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
المرابحة (al-murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the original cost and adds a known profit.
التولية (al-tawliyah): A sale where the seller transfers the item at the original cost without profit.
مثلى (mithli): A fungible good that can be measured by weight, volume, or count of similar units.
ذوات القيم (dhawat al-qiyam): Non-fungible goods that differ in individual value and cannot be replaced by identical units.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary condition for the validity of murabahah and tawliyah according to the Hanafi school?
A) The seller must have owned the item for at least one year.
B) The original purchase must be for a fungible (mithli).
C) The profit must be less than the original price.
D) The buyer must be a Muslim.

Key Takeaway
Murabahah and tawliyah are valid only when the original purchase was for a fungible good; costs that increase value may be added, but those that do not cannot, and fraud gives the buyer specific remedies.

Khiyanah in Murabaha and Tawliya: Three Hanafi Positions
Intra-Hanafi dispute over khiyanah remedy in murabaha and tawliya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on the remedy for khiyanah (deception) in murabaha and tawliya sales and explain the reasoning behind Abu Hanifa's differentiation.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa differentiates remedies by contract type: option in murabaha, deduction in tawliya.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the buyer's remedy when the seller deceives about the cost in murabaha (cost-plus sale) and tawliya (sale at cost). The three leading imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad—offer distinct solutions based on their understanding of contract structure and the preservation of contractual integrity. We will walk through each position and the underlying reasoning that links the legal consequence to the type of contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contract types
Murabaha is a sale where the seller discloses the original cost and adds a stated profit. Tawliya is a sale at the original cost with no profit. The remedy for deception (khiyanah) depends on which contract is used.
Both contracts require disclosure of the original cost; deception occurs when the seller lies about that cost.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
For murabaha, if deception is proven, the buyer has the option (khiyar) to either take the item at the named price or return it. For tawliya, the buyer simply deducts the amount of deception from the price and pays the reduced amount. The key difference is that in tawliya, taking the named price would convert the contract to murabaha, which is contrary to the parties' intent.
This preserves the essential nature of each contract: tawliya cannot exceed cost, murabaha allows profit.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
In both murabaha and tawliya, the buyer deducts the amount of deception plus a proportionate share of the profit (if any). For example, if the claimed cost was 10 with profit 5, but actual cost 8, the buyer pays 12 (8 cost + 4 profit, since profit is proportionally reduced).
Abu Yusuf treats both contracts uniformly based on the actual cost as the foundation.
4
State Muhammad's position
In both contracts, the buyer has an option to either take the item at the named price or return it, similar to the option of defect. The names 'murabaha' and 'tawliya' are mere descriptions; the underlying transaction is a new sale at a specified price.
Muhammad equates both to a direct bargain (musawamah) with the description serving as an inducement.
5
Trace the reasoning for the difference
Abu Hanifa's differentiation arises from the structural purpose: tawliya is a transfer at cost, so allowing the buyer to take at a higher price would change its nature. Murabaha, by contrast, inherently involves a profit margin, so the named price can be upheld while granting an option due to the loss of consent. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad each adopt a uniform rule for consistency, but with different starting points (cost vs. sale price).
The divergence illustrates how different usul principles (preserving contract type vs. focusing on disclosure) lead to different rulings.

(۳) قوله : لأن...الخ ؛ حاصله أنه شراء جديد، وأحكامه منقطعة عن العقدِ الأَوَّل
But he says: 'It cost me such-and-such', does not say 'I bought it for such-and-such'. Then if deception appears to the buyer in the murabaha, he takes it at its price or returns it. And 'al-haml' meaning transport of the goods from place to place, by land or sea, is added to the capital; because the principle is that everything that increases the goods or its value, like dye and transport, is attached to it, and what does not, is not. And it is apparent that each of dye, twisting, and the like increases the substance (ayn) by virtue of attachment, and transport increases the value because value differs by location. It is restricted to 'by hire' because if he did any of that himself or by lending, it is not allowed to add it to the capital, and likewise if a volunteer volunteers for these mentioned items. Likewise is added the hire of plastering the house, digging the well, cleaning the rivers, the canal, the channels, the earthwork, pruning vines and watering them, sowing, planting, and driving the sheep, unlike the hire of the shepherd and the rent of the storage room because they neither increase the substance nor the value. [1] His saying: 'I did not buy it for such-and-such' is said because it is a lie. And if he owned it by gift, inheritance, or bequest, and evaluated it then sold it by murabaha based on that value, it is valid. Its form is that he says 'Its value is such-and-such' or 'I will do murabaha with you on the value' or 'its mark (raqm).' The meaning of 'raqm' is that he writes on the garment an amount, whether equal to the price or more, then does murabaha on it. If he says 'its raqm is such-and-such' and he is truthful, he is not a deceiver; the buyer's ignorance is due to his own ignorance. So it is in al-Fath. [2] His saying: 'then appears' means by evidence or by admission of the seller or by his refusing the oath; this is the chosen view. It is said that it is only established by his admission because he contradicts himself in the claim of deception, but the correct view is that it is heard like the claim of a defect. So it is in al-Fath. (3) His saying: 'in the murabaha he takes it...' etc. means that the buyer in murabaha has an option: if he wishes he takes it at its price which is named, and if he wishes he returns it if return is possible. And if deception appears in tawliya, the buyer deducts the amount of deception. This is with Imam Abu Hanifa. (1) Fath al-Qadir (6:122). (2) Fath al-Qadir (6:126). And in tawliya he deducts from its price. And with Abu Yusuf, Allah have mercy on him, he deducts in both. And with Muhammad, he has an option in both. Then if he purchases a second time after selling at a profit, if he does murabaha, what he profited is deducted. If the profit covers the price, he does not do murabaha. That is, if he bought a cloth for ten and sold it for fifteen, then bought it for ten, then if he sells it by murabaha, he deducts what he profited and says: 'It cost me five.' And if he bought for ten and sold for twenty, then bought for ten, he does not sell it by murabaha at all. And with them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he says: 'It cost me ten' in both cases, because the second purchase is a new transaction, disconnected in rulings from the first. Its reasoning: if he were to consider the price he named, no tawliya would remain because it would exceed the first price, so it would turn into murabaha. Unlike murabaha, because if the named price were considered, it would not be overcome; rather it is murabaha as it was. So the named price is considered with the option in khiyanah in murabaha due to loss of consent, and it is not considered in khiyanah in tawliya so that it does not turn into murabaha. So deduction is determined in tawliya's khiyanah. [1] His saying: 'he deducts in both' means in murabaha and tawliya he deducts the amount of deception along with its share of profit in murabaha. For example, if he said 'I bought this cloth for ten' and sold it by murabaha for fifteen, then it appears that the seller had bought it for eight, he deducts the amount of deception: i.e., two dirhams, and deducts from the profit the equivalent of the deception amount, which is one dirham. So he takes the cloth for twelve dirhams, because the basis in the terms tawliya and murabaha is that the contract is tawliya and murabaha, not just the naming; so it is built on the first contract to realize the origin which is tawliya and murabaha. [2] His saying: 'he has an option in both' means in both murabaha and tawliya: if he wishes he takes it at the full price, and if he wishes he returns it, because they both entered into a contract by their choice at a price they named, so it is concluded at that full price, as if they had done a direct bargain. Mentioning tawliya and murabaha is like a description for facilitation and encouragement; so the buyer has an option when it is lost, as if he found the goods defective. (3) His saying: 'because...' etc.; its summary is that it is a new purchase, and its rulings are disconnected from the first contract.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary on the Hidayah discussing the rulings of khiyanah (deception) in murabaha (cost-plus sale) and tawliya (transfer sale at cost). The text opens with a rule about what the seller should say: he should state what the item cost him, not that he bought it for a certain price, to avoid lying. If deception is proven, the buyer in murabaha has an option to take the item at the named price or return it. Then the text discusses additions to capital: transport costs (haml) and similar expenses that increase the item or its value can be added to the capital, but only if paid by hire; if done by the seller himself or for free, they cannot be added. Examples include dyeing, twisting, plastering, digging wells, etc. The text then moves to the core dispute: if deception is found in murabaha, the buyer has an option; in tawliya, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the buyer simply deducts the amount of deception from the price. Abu Yusuf holds that in both murabaha and tawliya, the buyer deducts the deception amount along with its share of profit. Muhammad holds that the buyer has an option in both, either to take at the named price or return. The text further discusses a scenario where the seller buys back the same item after selling it at a profit, and then resells it by murabaha: the previous profit must be deducted. The reasoning provided is that the second purchase is a new transaction, independent of the first. The reason for the difference in khiyanah between murabaha and tawliya is that in tawliya, if the named price were taken, it would turn into murabaha because the price exceeds the first cost, so deduction is necessary to preserve the tawliya contract. In murabaha, the named price can be retained with the option because it remains murabaha. The commentary illustrates Abu Yusuf's view with an example: if the seller claimed cost was ten, sold at fifteen (profit five), then it appears actual cost was eight, the buyer deducts two (deception) and also one from the profit (proportionate share), paying twelve. Muhammad's view equates both contracts to a direct sale, giving the buyer a full option. The text concludes with the reasoning that the terms 'murabaha' and 'tawliya' are descriptive, so when the described benefit (truthfulness) is lost, the buyer has an option like the option of defect.

Key Terms
مرابحة (murabaha): A sale where the seller discloses the original cost and adds a specified profit.
تولية (tawliya): A sale where the seller transfers the goods at the original cost without profit.
خيانة (khiyanah): Deception in disclosing the true cost; e.g., claiming a higher cost than actually paid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the buyer's remedy when deception (khiyanah) is proven in a tawliya sale?
A) He may either take the item at the named price or return it.
B) He deducts the amount of deception from the price and pays the reduced amount.
C) He deducts the deception amount plus a proportionate share of the profit.
D) He has no remedy; the sale is binding.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's distinction between murabaha and tawliya protects the structural integrity of each contract type in the face of deception.

Repurchase After Profit Sale: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Cost Basis
Intra-Hanafi dispute on repurchase after profit sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on the cost basis after repurchase and apply the rule to a numerical example.

Core Idea
A second purchase after profit sale confirms the first profit, creating a shubha that blocks cost-basis reset according to Abu Hanifa.

This passage presents a subtle intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf about what happens when someone sells an item at a profit (murabahah) or at cost (tawliyah) and later repurchases it. The key question: does the second purchase reset the cost basis to the new price, or does the earlier profit taint the transaction? The three leading Hanafi imams give different answers, rooted in whether the second contract is considered completely disconnected from the first.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Read the opening: the buyer sells cloth bought for 10 at a profit of 5 (murabahah price 15), then repurchases it for 10. Now he wants to resell it on murabahah. What cost does he disclose?
2
State each Imam's ruling
Abu Hanifa: deduct the first profit (cost = 5). Abu Yusuf: cost = 10 (new purchase price) with mandatory reduction if misrepresentation. Muhammad: cost = 10 but buyer has the option to take at 10 or return.
The passage also discusses tawliyah, but focus on murabahah.
3
Trace the reasoning behind the ikhtilaf
The Sahibayn view the second purchase as a new contract (shira' jadid) disconnected from the first. Abu Hanifa argues that before the second purchase, the first profit was at risk of being lost (if the buyer returned a defective item); the second purchase confirms the profit, creating a shubha that the profit was obtained through the second contract.
This shubha prevents treating the second purchase as a clean slate.
4
Examine the qiyas analogy
Abu Hanifa compares to divorce witnesses: if witnesses testify to a divorce before consummation, they confirm the wife's right to half the dower (which was at risk of falling by apostasy). Similarly, the second purchase confirms the first profit.
The illa is 'confirmation after risk of loss'.
5
Apply the rule to a numerical example
Original cost 10, first profit 5, second purchase price 10. For Abu Hanifa, the new cost basis is 5 (10 - 5). For the Sahibayn, it remains 10.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf – the practical difference.

وفي التَّولية حطه من ثمنه، وعند أبي يوسف له يحط فيهما، وعند محمد له خير فيهما. فإن اشترى ثانياً بعد بيع بربح، فإن رابح طرح عنه ما ربح، وإن استغرق الرِّبْحُ الثَّمَنَ لم يرابح: أي إذا اشترى ثوباً بعشرة وباعه بخمسة عشر، ثُمَّ اشتراه بعشرة، فإنَّه إن باعَهُ مرابحة طرح عنه ما ربح، ويقول: قام علي بخمسة. وإن اشترى بعشرة وباعَهُ بعشرين ثم اشتراه بعشرة لا يبيعه مرابحة أصلاً، وعندهما يقول: قام علي بعشرة في الفصلين؛ لأن البيع الثاني بيع متجدد، ومنقطع الأحكام عن الأول. ووجهه: أنه لو اعتبر ما سمّاه من الثمن لما بَقِيَ تولية؛ لأنه زائد على الثمن الأول فينقلب مرابحة؛ بخلاف المرابحة؛ لأنه لو اعتبر فيها المسمى من الثمن لم يتغلَّب، بل هي مرابحة كما كانت، فاعتبر المسمى مع الخيار في خيانة المرابحة؛ لفوات الرضاء، ولم يعتبر في خيانةِ التَّولية لئلا تنقلب مرابحة؛ فتعين الحط في خيانة التولية. [١] قوله: يحط فيهما؛ أي في المرابحة والتولية يحط قدر الخيانة مع حصتها من الربح في المرابحة، مثلاً: إذا قال: اشتريت هذا الثوب بعشرة فباعه مرابحة بخمسة عشر، ثم ظهر أنَّ البائع كان اشتراه بثمانية، يحط قدر الخيانة: أي درهمان، ويحط من الربح ما يقابل قدر الخيانة، وهو درهم واحد. فيأخذ الثوب باثني عشر درهماً؛ لأنَّ الأصل في لفظ التولية والمرابحة كون العقدِ تولية ومرابحة؛ لا التسمية فقط، فيبتني على العقد الأوّلِ لتحققِ الأصل الذي هو التولية والمرابحة. [٢] قوله: خير فيهما؛ أي في المرابحة والتولية كليهما، فإن شاء أخذه بكل الثمن، وإن شاء ردّه؛ لأنهما باشرا عقداً باختيارهما بثمن سمّياه، فينعقد بجميع ذلك الثمن، كما لو باشرا مساومة، وذكر التولية والمرابحة جارٍ مجرى الوصف؛ للترويح والترغيب، فيتخيرُ المشتري عند فواته كما لو وجد المبيع معيباً. (٣) قوله: لأن...الخ؛ حاصله أنه شراء جديد، وأحكامه منقطعة عن العقدِ الأَوَّل. ولأبي حنيفة له أن قبل الشراءَ الثَّانِي يُحْتَمَلُ أَن يَطْلِعَ على عيب فَيَرُدَّهُ عليه، فيسقط الرِّبْحُ الذي ربحه، فإذا اشتراه ثانياً تأكد ذلك الربح، فصار للمشتري الثاني شبهة أن الربح قد حصل به، فلا يكون منقطع الأحكام عن الأول. [١] قوله: ولأبي حنيفة...الخ؛ حاصلهُ أنَّ ما ربح المشتري أوَّل مرَّة له شبهة الحصول بالعقد الثاني؛ لأنَّ الربح في البيع الأول كان على شرف السقوط بأن يرد المشتري المبيع لظهور عيب فيه ويستردَّ منه كلَّ الثمن فيبطل حقه في الربح، وبالشراء ثانياً وقع الأمن من ذلك، وتأكد الربح الأول. وللتأكيد شبهة بالإيجاب حتى غرمَ شهودُ الطَّلاقِ قبل الدخول نصف الصداق إذا رجعوا؛ لأنَّ شهادتهم أكدت نصف المهر الذي كان على شرف السقوط بالردّة، وتمكّن ابن الزوج، وبيع المرابحة يمتنع بالشبهة، كما يمتنع بالحقيقة.
And in tawliyah, he reduces from its price. And according to Abu Yusuf, he reduces in both (murabahah and tawliyah). And according to Muhammad, he has a choice in both. If he buys a second time after selling at a profit, then if the profit is less than the price, he deducts the profit and says: it cost me five. And if the profit consumes the price, he does not sell at profit at all. According to them (Sahibayn), he says: it cost me ten in both cases, because the second sale is a new transaction, disconnected from the first. Its reasoning: if the named price were considered, tawliyah would not remain because it exceeds the first price and becomes murabahah. Unlike murabahah, because if the named price were considered, it would not change; it remains murabahah as before. So the named price is considered with the option in the breach of murabahah due to loss of consent, and not considered in the breach of tawliyah to avoid converting to murabahah. Thus reduction is determined in the breach of tawliyah. [1] His saying: 'he reduces in both' means in murabahah and tawliyah he reduces the amount of the breach along with its share of profit in murabahah. For example, if he said: I bought this cloth for ten and sold it on murabahah for fifteen, then it appears that the seller had bought it for eight, he reduces the breach: two dirhams, and reduces from the profit the equivalent of the breach: one dirham. So he takes the cloth for twelve dirhams, because the basis in the wording of tawliyah and murabahah is that the contract is tawliyah and murabahah; not just the naming, so it is based on the first contract to realize the origin which is tawliyah and murabahah. [2] His saying: 'he has a choice in both' means in murabahah and tawliyah both. If he wishes, he takes it for the full price, and if he wishes, he returns it, because they both executed a contract by choice with a price they named, so it is concluded with that entire price, just as if they bargained. And mentioning tawliyah and murabahah is like a description for encouragement and desire. So the buyer has a choice when it is lost, just as if he found the commodity defective. (3) His saying: 'because...etc.' Its summary: it is a new purchase, and its rulings are disconnected from the first contract. And according to Abu Hanifa, before the second purchase, it is possible that he may discover a defect and return it, so the profit he profited is lost. So when he buys it a second time, that profit is confirmed. So for the second buyer, there is a semblance that the profit has been obtained through it, so it is not disconnected from the first. [1] His saying: 'And according to Abu Hanifa...etc.' Its summary: The profit the buyer made the first time has a semblance of being obtained by the second contract, because the profit in the first sale was on the verge of falling away if the buyer returned the commodity due to a defect and reclaimed all the price, voiding his right to profit. By the second purchase, security from that occurs, and the first profit is confirmed. And confirmation has a semblance of creation, so that witnesses of divorce before consummation are liable for half the dower if they retract, because their testimony confirmed half the dower that was on the verge of falling away by apostasy, and the husband's right became established. And murabahah sale is prevented by semblance, just as it is prevented by reality.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling when a buyer sells an item at a profit (murabahah) or at cost (tawliyah), then later repurchases the same item. The text first states the positions of the three imams. According to Abu Hanifa, in the case of tawliyah, the purchaser reduces the price by the amount of the original profit; in murabahah, he also reduces, but with a choice (khiyar) for the buyer. According to Abu Yusuf, in both types the reduction is mandatory. According to Muhammad, the buyer has a choice in both: either to take at the full stated price or to return. The reasoning is that the second purchase is a new contract disconnected from the first, so the original profit should not affect the second transaction. However, Abu Hanifa argues that before the second purchase, there was a possibility that the first buyer might return the item due to a defect, voiding the profit. By repurchasing, the profit becomes confirmed, creating a semblance (shubha) that the profit was obtained through the second contract. This semblance prevents the second sale from being treated as disconnected. The commentator clarifies that the semblance is strong enough to affect rulings, giving the analogy of divorce witnesses who become liable for half the dower if they retract after confirming a divorce before consummation. Thus, murabahah is prevented by mere semblance just as by reality. The practical consequence: when the buyer repurchases after making a profit, he cannot sell again on murabahah using the original cost basis; rather, he must use the second purchase price, because the first profit is considered tainted by the semblance of being part of the second contract.

Key Terms
مرابحة (murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the original cost and adds a specified profit; the buyer agrees to that price.
تولية (tawliyah): A sale at cost without profit; the seller transfers the item at the same price he bought it.
خيانة (khianah): Breach of trust when the seller misstates the original cost in murabahah or tawliyah.
شبهة (shubha): A semblance or doubt that affects legal rulings, here preventing the murabahah from being valid based solely on the second purchase price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if a buyer purchases an item for 10, sells it on murabahah for 15, then repurchases it for 10, and now wants to sell it again on murabahah?
A) He may sell it on murabahah stating the cost as 10, as per all imams.
B) According to Abu Hanifa, he must deduct the first profit and state the cost as 5.
C) According to Abu Yusuf, he has the option to sell at cost 10 or return.
D) The sale is invalid because of the semblance of profit.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on repurchase after profit sale hinges on whether the second purchase is treated as a new contract (Sahibayn) or as confirming the first profit via shubha (Abu Hanifa), leading to different cost bases in subsequent murabahah.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Murabahah Cost Basis from an Indebted Slave's Transaction
Ruling on murabahah cost basis and qiyas with shuf'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the cost basis in a murabahah sale when a master purchases from his indebted slave, and explain the qiyas with shuf'ah.

Core Idea
In murabahah, the master's cost is the slave's original purchase price, not the price paid to the slave, to prevent fraud.

When a master buys from his indebted slave, the cost basis for a subsequent murabahah sale is not the price the master paid, but the original price the slave paid. This prevents deception in trust-based sales. The Hanafi school supports this with an analogy to pre-emption (shuf'ah). This lesson unpacks that ruling and its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
An indebted slave (whose debts equal or exceed his value) buys an item, then sells it to his master at a lower price.
2
State the ruling
When the master sells that item on murabahah (cost-plus sale), he must declare the slave's original cost, not the discounted price he paid.
3
Trace the proof – Qiyas with Shuf'ah
The analogy: In pre-emption (shuf'ah), if a pre-emptor waives his right in the first sale, he can still claim it in a later re-sale of the same property. This shows that a second transaction can be connected to the first, reviving rights. Similarly, the master's purchase is connected to the slave's original purchase, so the cost basis reverts to the first price.
The illa (effective cause) is the connection between the two transactions; the second sale does not sever the original cost.
4
Understand the condition
The slave must be indebted (muhit dainuhu biraqabatihi) for the ruling to apply. Without debt, the slave's sale to the master is invalid because the master already owns the slave and his acquisitions, making the transaction pointless.
5
Extend to other cases
The same ruling applies if the master sells to the slave at a higher price – the first price is used. It also applies to the mukatib (slave under kitabah) due to similar suspicion of collusion.

Source Text

[٢] قوله: المحيط دينه برقبته؛ وفائدة هذا القيد أنه لو لم يكن على العبد دين فباع من مولاه شيئاً لم يصح؛ لأنه لا يفيد المولى شيئاً لم يكن له قبل البيع، لا ملك الرقبة ولا ملك التصرف. [٣] قوله: فباعه؛ وكذلك إن كان المولى اشتراه فباعَهُ من العبد بخمسة عشر؛ لأنَّ هذا العقد وإن كان صحيحاً في نفسه لكن فيه شبهة العدم؛ لأنَّ العبد يملكه، وما في يدِهِ لا يخلو عن حقه فاعتبر عدماً في حق المرابحة، وبقي الاعتبار للأول. فيصير كأنَّ العبد اشتراه للمولى بعشرة في الفصل الأول، وكأنه يبيعه للمولى في الفصل الثاني، فيعتبر الثمنُ الأوّل، والمكاتب كالمأذون؛ لوجودِ التهمة بل كلُّ مَن لا
(And the profit of a master purchasing from his indebted slave whose debt surrounds his value, upon what his seller bought): That is, the indebted slave whose debt surrounds his value bought a cloth for ten, and sold it to his master for five. [2] His saying: 'whose debt surrounds his value'; the benefit of this condition is that if the slave had no debt, his sale to his master would not be valid, because it gives the master nothing he did not already have, neither ownership of the slave nor the right to dispose. [3] His saying: 'he sold it'; and likewise if the master bought it and then sold it to the slave for fifteen; because this contract, although valid in itself, contains a semblance of nonexistence, because the slave owns it, and what is in his hand is not free from his right, so it is considered nonexistent for the purpose of murabahah, and consideration remains with the first. Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of murabahah when an indebted slave (ma'dhun muhit dainuhu biraqabatihi) engages in a transaction with his master. The scenario: a slave whose debts equal or exceed his value buys a cloth for ten dirhams, then sells it to his master for only five dirhams. Now, if the master wants to sell this cloth on murabahah, he states that it cost him ten dirhams (the original price), not five. The reason is that the sale from the slave to the master is considered tainted: although valid in itself, it is as if the master did not truly own the cloth because the slave's debts encumber him, and the sale price (five) is not a true reflection of cost. To avoid fraud in murabahah, the basis is the original price paid by the slave. The commentator draws an analogy with pre-emption (shuf'ah): if a pre-emptor waives his right in the first contract, he can still claim pre-emption in a subsequent contract, meaning the later transaction is connected to the earlier one. Similarly, the slave's purchase is considered as if it were for the master, so the master's cost basis is the first price. The same applies if the master buys from the slave and then sells back at a higher price: the second transaction is tainted by the slave's interest, so the first price is used. Abu Hanifa's detailed proof is repeated, emphasizing that the confirmation of profit by a second transaction creates a semblance that affects the ruling. The condition 'muhit dainuhu biraqabatihi' is crucial: if the slave had no debt, the sale to the master would be invalid because it gives the master nothing new (he already owns the slave and his acquisitions). This shows that the indebtedness creates a genuine separation of ownership. Finally, the mukatib (slave under manumission contract) is treated similarly due to suspicion of collusion.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who is permitted by his master to engage in trade.
محوط دينه برقبته (muhit dainuhu biraqabatihi): A slave whose debts equal or exceed his market value, such that his entire value is encumbered.
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption to purchase property that was sold to another, under certain conditions.
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitabah to earn his freedom by paying installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
An indebted slave (muhit dainuhu biraqabatihi) buys a cloth for 10 dirhams, then sells it to his master for 5 dirhams. If the master wants to sell the cloth on murabahah, what cost should he state?
A) 5 dirhams, because that is what he paid.
B) 10 dirhams, because the slave's original purchase is the true cost basis.
C) 15 dirhams, because he must add a profit.
D) The sale is invalid, so he cannot sell on murabahah.

Key Takeaway
The master's murabahah cost basis is the slave's original purchase price due to the taint of the subsequent transaction, supported by qiyas with shuf'ah.

The Second Purchase Confirms Profit: Murabahah with Master and Authorized Slave
Explains how a second purchase confirms profit in murabahah between master and authorized slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a second purchase confirms profit in murabahah and apply the ruling to the case of a master buying from his authorized slave.

Core Idea
A second purchase after a related-party sale confirms profit, allowing murabahah based on the first cost.

In murabahah, the seller must disclose the true cost. But what if the item was previously sold between related parties, like a master and his slave? The second purchase creates a semblance of confirmation, making the original profit reliable. This lesson unpacks Abu Hanifa's ruling and the powerful analogy with witnesses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Transaction
Examine the given scenario: an authorized slave in debt buys a garment for 10, sells it to his master for 5, then the master buys it again from a third party. Determine what happens when the master sells on murabahah.
The key is that the second purchase is from a third party, not from the slave.
2
Analyze the Profit Confirmation
Understand Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the profit from the first sale was uncertain (might be lost if the slave returned the item). The second purchase removes that uncertainty, confirming the profit. This confirmation is treated as a semblance (shubhah) of actual profit, which is sufficient for murabahah.
The profit is not physically earned by the second purchase, but legally considered as such for cost disclosure.
3
Understand the Shubhah Concept
Learn from the analogy of witnesses to divorce: when witnesses testify to a divorce before consummation, they confirm the half-dower that was at risk. Similarly, the second purchase confirms the profit that was at risk.
Shubhah is used in Hanafi fiqh to treat something as effective even when not fully realized.
4
Check the Condition of Debt
Note that the authorized slave must have debt encompassing his net worth; otherwise the initial sale to the master would be invalid. This condition ensures the slave has a real interest in the transaction.
If no debt, the slave's sale to the master gives nothing new, so no profit basis.
5
Apply the Ruling
Conclude that in murabahah, the master states the cost as the original price paid by the slave (10), not the second purchase price, because the second purchase only confirms the first profit.
This is the Mufta bihi position from Abu Hanifa and the majority.

Source Text

كتاب البيع / باب الإقالة والمرابحة والتولية (٢) ١٨٥ ورابح سيد شرى من مأذونه المحيط دينه برقبته على ما شَرَى بائعه ولأبي حنيفة له أن قبل الشراءَ الثَّانِي يُحْتَمَلُ أَن يَطْلِعَ على عيب فَيَرُدَّهُ عليه ، فيسقط الرِّبْحُ الذي ريحه ، فإذا اشتراه ثانياً تأكد ذلك الربح ، فصار للمشتري الثاني شبهة أن الربح قد حصل به، فلا يكون منقطع الأحكام عن الأول . ( ورابح سيد شري من مأذونه المحيط دينه برقبته على ما شَرَى بائعه) : أي اشترى العبد المأذون المحيط دينه برقبته ثوبا بعشرة ، فباعه من مولاه بخمسة . فالمولى إن باعه مرابحة ، يقول : قام على بعشرة . بشهادة أنَّ الشفيع إذا سلَّم الشفعة في العقدِ الأوّل، فيقدر على الشفعة في العقد الثاني ، فالمرابحة تبتني عليه ؛ ولهذا لو باعَه المشتري من ثالث ثمَّ اشتراه الأول منه يبيعُهُ مرابحة الأخير، وصار كما لو باعه بعرض يساوي خمسة عشر، ثم اشتراه بعشرة ، فإنّه بالثمن يبيعه مرابحة بعشرة. عشر، [١]قوله : ولأبي حنيفة .. الخ ؛ حاصلهُ أنَّ ما ربح المشتري أوَّل مرَّة له شبهة الحصول بالعقد الثاني ؛ لأنَّ الربح في البيع الأول كان على شرف السقوط بأن يرد المشتري المبيع لظهور عيب فيه ويستردَّ منه كلَّ الثمن فيبطل حقه في الربح، وبالشراء ثانياً وقع الأمن من ذلك، وتأكد الربح الأول. وللتأكيد شبهة بالإيجاب حتى غرمَ شهودُ الطَّلاقِ قبل الدخول نصف الصداق إذا رجعوا ؛ لأنَّ شهادتهم أكدت نصف المهر الذي كان على شرف السقوط بالردّة، وتمكّن ابن الزوج، وبيع المرابحة يمتنع بالشبهة، كما يمتنع بالحقيقة. [٢] قوله : المحيط دينه برقبته ؛ وفائدة هذا القيد أنه لو لم يكن على العبد دين فباع من مولاه شيئاً لم يصح؛ لأنه لا يفيد المولى شيئاً لم يكن له قبل البيع، لا ملك الرقبة ولا ملك التصرف. [٣] قوله : فباعه ؛ وكذلك إن كان المولى اشتراه فباعَهُ من العبد بخمسة عشر ؛ لأنَّ هذا العقد وإن كان صحيحاً في نفسه لكن فيه شبهة العدم ؛ لأنَّ العبد يملكه، وما في يدِهِ لا يخلو عن حقه فاعتبر عدماً في حق المرابحة، وبقي الاعتبار للأول. فيصير كأنَّ العبد اشتراه للمولى بعشرة في الفصل الأول، وكأنه يبيعه للمولى في الفصل الثاني، فيعتبر الثمنُ الأوّل، والمكاتب كالمأذون ؛ لوجودِ التهمة بل كلُّ مَن لا  ١٨٦ · زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية كمأذون شرى من سيده ، وربُّ المال على ما شراهُ ضاربَهُ بالنصف أولاً ، ونصف ما ريح بشرائِهِ ثانيا منه، فإن اعورت المبيعة ، أو وطئت ثيباً برقبته ؛ لأنه كمأذون شرى من سيده : أي إذا اشترى المولي بعشرة، ثم باعه من مأذونِهِ المحيط دينه برقبته بخمسة عشر ، فالمأذونُ إن باعَهُ مرابحة يقولُ قام عليَّ بعشرة ؛ لأن بيع المولي من عبده المأذون وشراءه منه اعتبر عدماً في حق المرابحة لثبوته" مع المنافي. وإنما قال : المحيط دينه حينئذ يكونُ للعبد المأذون ملك، أمَّا المأذون الذي لا دين عليه فلا ملك له، فلا شبهة في أن البيع الثاني لا اعتبار له ، أما إذا كان عليه دين محيط فحينئذٍ يكونُ البيعُ الثَّاني بيعاً، ومع ذلك لا اعتبار له في حق المرابحة فيثبت الحكم بالطَّريقِ الأُولى فيما لا دين علي. ورب المال على ما شراهُ صَارَبَهُ بالنصف أولاً ، ونصف ما ريح بشرائِهِ ثانياً منه أي اشترى المضارب بالنصف ثوبا بعشرة وباعَهُ من رب المال بخمسة عشر، فالنُّوبُ قام على ربِّ المال" باثني عشر ونصف . ( فإن اعورت المبيعة، أو وطنت ثيباً تقبل شهادته له كالأصول والفروع، وأحد الزوجين ، أو أحد المتفاوضين، كذلك كما صرحوا به. [١]قوله : لثبوته ؛ أي لثبوتِ العقدِ وجوازه ؛ لقـيـام الـديـن مـع وجود المنافي للجواز، وهو كون العبد ملكاً للمولى، فصار كأنه باع ملك نفسه من نفسه، وكذا في الشراء. [٢] قوله : قام على ربِّ المال...الخ ؛ فيقول : قام عليَّ باثني عشر ونصف، هذا عندنا ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ منهما يستفيد ملك اليدِ بهذا العقد، وإن لم يستفد ملك الرقبة فيعتبر العقد مع شبهة العدم ؛ لأنَّ المضارب وكيل عن ربِّ المال في البيع الأول من ه فجعلَ البيعُ الثاني عدماً في حق نصف الربح. وجه، وعند زفر : لا يجوز بيع ربُّ المال من المضارب، ولا بيع المضارب منه ؛ لا نعدام الربح ؛ لأنَّ الربح يحصل إذا بيع من الأجنبي، إذ البيع تمليك مال بمال غيره ، وهو يشتري ماله بماله. [٣]قوله : أو وطئت ؛ والحالُ أنها ثيباً، ولم ينقصها الوطء سواء كان الواطئ مولاها أو غيره ؛ ولذا أتى بصيغة المجهول، كذا في «مجمع الأنهر»
It is permissible for the master to profit from what he bought from his authorized slave whose debt encompasses his net worth, based on what he (the slave) bought. Abu Hanifa said: He has the right to do so before the second purchase, because it is possible that he might discover a defect and return it to him, thereby cancelling the profit he made. But if he buys it a second time, that profit becomes confirmed. So the second buyer has a semblance of the profit having been obtained through him, and thus he is not severed from the rulings of the first transaction. (And the master profits from what he bought from his authorized slave whose debt encompasses his net worth, based on what he bought) meaning: the authorized slave whose debt encompasses his net worth bought a garment for ten, then sold it to his master for five. If the master then sells it on a murabahah basis, he says: it cost me ten. This is supported by the case of the pre-emptor: if he waives pre-emption in the first contract, he can still claim pre-emption in the second contract, so murabahah is built upon it. For this reason, if the buyer sold it to a third party, then the first buyer bought it from him, he sells it on murabahah based on the last purchase. And it is as if he sold it for goods worth fifteen, then bought it for ten, then he sells it on murabahah for ten. [1] His statement: "And for Abu Hanifa... etc." Its summary: that what the first buyer profited has a semblance of being obtained through the second contract, because the profit in the first sale was on the verge of being lost by the buyer returning the sold item due to a defect and reclaiming the entire price, thereby nullifying his right to profit. By the second purchase, security from that is achieved, and the first profit is confirmed. Confirmation has a semblance of causation, so much so that witnesses of divorce before consummation are liable for half the dower if they retract, because their testimony confirmed half the dower that was on the verge of being lost by apostasy and the possibility of the husband's child, and murabahah sale is prevented by semblance, just as it is prevented by reality. [2] His statement: "whose debt encompasses his net worth"; the benefit of this restriction is that if the slave had no debt, then selling something to his master would not be valid, because it does not give the master anything he did not already own, neither ownership of the slave's person nor ownership of disposal. [3] His statement: "then he sold it"; similarly if the master bought it and then sold it to the slave for fifteen, because this contract, although valid in itself, has a semblance of non-existence, because the slave owns it, and what is in his hand is not free from his right, so it is considered non-existent with respect to murabahah, and consideration is given to the first. Thus it becomes as if the slave bought it for the master for ten in the first scenario, and as if he sold it to the master in the second scenario, so the first price is considered. And the mukatab is like the authorized slave because of the existence of suspicion, rather everyone who does not... [PAGE_186] ...like an authorized slave buying from his master, and the capital owner based on what his mudarib bought with half first, and half of what he profited from his second purchase from him. Then if the sold item becomes defective, or is deflowered while a virgin... [1] His statement: "because of its establishment"; meaning the establishment and validity of the contract due to the existence of debt despite the presence of a factor contrary to validity, which is that the slave is owned by the master, so it becomes as if he sold his own property to himself, and similarly in purchase. [2] His statement: "he says: it cost the capital owner... etc."; he says: it cost me twelve and a half. This is according to us, because each of them obtains ownership of possession through this contract, even if he does not obtain ownership of the property, so the contract is considered with a semblance of non-existence, because the mudarib is an agent of the capital owner in the first sale from him, so the second sale is considered non-existent with respect to half the profit. According to Zufar: It is not permissible for the capital owner to sell to the mudarib, nor for the mudarib to sell to him, due to the non-existence of profit, because profit is obtained when sold to a third party, since sale is the exchange of property for another's property, and he is buying his own property with his own property. [3] His statement: "or is deflowered"; while she is a virgin, and the deflowering does not diminish her, whether the deflowerer is her master or someone else; hence the passive voice is used, as in Majma' al-Anhur.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqayah, discussing specific rulings of murabahah sales involving related parties. The central issue is when a buyer (like a master from his authorized slave, or a mudarib from the capital owner) makes a second purchase of an item previously sold, how does that affect the cost basis for a subsequent murabahah sale? The author explains that according to Abu Hanifa, if a master buys from his authorized slave, the profit from the first sale is uncertain because the item might be returned due to a defect. But when the master buys it again (from a third party), that profit becomes confirmed. Therefore, when selling on murabahah, the master can base his cost on the first price (ten) because the second purchase only confirms the first profit. The author supports this with an analogy: witnesses who testify to a divorce before consummation confirm half the dower, even if they later retract, they become liable. Similarly, the semblance of confirmation here suffices to establish the ruling. The passage also details the condition that the slave must have debt encompassing his net worth; otherwise, the initial sale to the master is invalid as it gives nothing. If the master sells to the slave, it is also considered effectively non-existent for murabahah due to the conflict of interest. The same principle applies to mudarabah: if the mudarib buys from the capital owner, the second purchase is disregarded for half the profit, and the cost is averaged. Finally, the author notes Zufar's opposing view that such transactions are entirely invalid because profit only arises from sale to a stranger.

Key Terms
المرابحة (al-murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a known profit.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who has been granted permission by his master to engage in trade.
شبهة (shubhah): A semblance or doubt that influences the legal ruling; here it is used to treat a transaction as effective despite underlying issues.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main legal effect of the master's second purchase in the scenario?
A) It nullifies the earlier sale between master and slave.
B) It confirms the profit from the first sale, making it valid as a basis for murabahah.
C) It gives the master a new cost basis equal to the second purchase price.
D) It renders the entire transaction void due to lack of genuine profit.

Key Takeaway
A second purchase after a related-party sale confirms the earlier profit, allowing murabahah to be based on the original cost.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
10 min
Murabahah between Capital Owner and Mudarib: Zufar's Dispute
Explains the ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on murabahah involving mudarib and capital owner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position on murabahah between mudarib and capital owner and contrast it with Zufar's dissenting view.

Core Idea
In mudarabah, a second purchase between the parties is treated as non-existent for half the profit, but Zufar rejects any profit in such sales.

When a mudarib (agent) buys goods on behalf of the capital owner, then sells them to the capital owner, can the capital owner later sell on murabahah? The Hanafi school says yes, using an average cost, but Zufar disagrees entirely. This lesson explores the principle of semblance and the practical consequences of the disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mudarabah Scenario
Understand the transaction: a mudarib buys a garment for 10 with half-profit sharing, then sells it to the capital owner for 15. The capital owner wants to sell on murabahah. What is the cost basis?
The mudarib is an agent for the capital owner in the first purchase.
2
Apply the Hanafi Ruling
According to Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn, the capital owner can sell on murabahah stating: 'It cost me 12.5'. The second sale is considered non-existent for half the profit, so the cost is averaged: (10 + 15)/2 = 12.5.
The second sale is valid but ignored for profit calculation in murabahah.
3
Analyze the Reasoning
The second purchase creates a semblance of non-existence because the mudarib is selling to the principal. The ruling applies the same shubhah concept: the first profit is confirmed by the second transaction.
The mudarib case parallels the master-slave case but with half-profit computation.
4
Consider Zufar's Objection
Zufar argues that no profit exists because the mudarib is buying from his own principal. Sale requires exchange of one property for another, and here it's like buying one's own property.
Zufar's view is based on strict qiyas, ignoring the shubhah.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Under the majority view, the capital owner can claim 12.5 as cost. Under Zufar, the entire transaction is invalid for murabahah, so the capital owner cannot profit from it.
The dispute has practical implications for how capital owners report costs.

( فإن اعورت المبيعة، أو وطنت ثيباً تقبل شهادته له كالأصول والفروع، وأحد الزوجين ، أو أحد المتفاوضين، كذلك كما صرحوا به. [١]قوله : لثبوته ؛ أي لثبوتِ العقدِ وجوازه ؛ لقـيـام الـديـن مـع وجود المنافي للجواز، وهو كون العبد ملكاً للمولى، فصار كأنه باع ملك نفسه من نفسه، وكذا في الشراء. [٢] قوله : قام على ربِّ المال...الخ ؛ فيقول : قام عليَّ باثني عشر ونصف، هذا عندنا ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ منهما يستفيد ملك اليدِ بهذا العقد، وإن لم يستفد ملك الرقبة فيعتبر العقد مع شبهة العدم ؛ لأنَّ المضارب وكيل عن ربِّ المال في البيع الأول من ه فجعلَ البيعُ الثاني عدماً في حق نصف الربح. وجه، وعند زفر : لا يجوز بيع ربُّ المال من المضارب، ولا بيع المضارب منه ؛ لا نعدام الربح ؛ لأنَّ الربح يحصل إذا بيع من الأجنبي، إذ البيع تمليك مال بمال غيره ، وهو يشتري ماله بماله. [٣]قوله : أو وطئت ؛ والحالُ أنها ثيباً، ولم ينقصها الوطء سواء كان الواطئ مولاها أو غيره ؛ ولذا أتى بصيغة المجهول، كذا في «مجمع الأنهر»
It is permissible for the master to profit from what he bought from his authorized slave whose debt encompasses his net worth, based on what he (the slave) bought. Abu Hanifa said: He has the right to do so before the second purchase, because it is possible that he might discover a defect and return it to him, thereby cancelling the profit he made. But if he buys it a second time, that profit becomes confirmed. So the second buyer has a semblance of the profit having been obtained through him, and thus he is not severed from the rulings of the first transaction. (And the master profits from what he bought from his authorized slave whose debt encompasses his net worth, based on what he bought) meaning: the authorized slave whose debt encompasses his net worth bought a garment for ten, then sold it to his master for five. If the master then sells it on a murabahah basis, he says: it cost me ten. This is supported by the case of the pre-emptor: if he waives pre-emption in the first contract, he can still claim pre-emption in the second contract, so murabahah is built upon it. For this reason, if the buyer sold it to a third party, then the first buyer bought it from him, he sells it on murabahah based on the last purchase. And it is as if he sold it for goods worth fifteen, then bought it for ten, then he sells it on murabahah for ten. [1] His statement: "And for Abu Hanifa... etc." Its summary: that what the first buyer profited has a semblance of being obtained through the second contract, because the profit in the first sale was on the verge of being lost by the buyer returning the sold item due to a defect and reclaiming the entire price, thereby nullifying his right to profit. By the second purchase, security from that is achieved, and the first profit is confirmed. Confirmation has a semblance of causation, so much so that witnesses of divorce before consummation are liable for half the dower if they retract, because their testimony confirmed half the dower that was on the verge of being lost by apostasy and the possibility of the husband's child, and murabahah sale is prevented by semblance, just as it is prevented by reality. [2] His statement: "whose debt encompasses his net worth"; the benefit of this restriction is that if the slave had no debt, then selling something to his master would not be valid, because it does not give the master anything he did not already own, neither ownership of the slave's person nor ownership of disposal. [3] His statement: "then he sold it"; similarly if the master bought it and then sold it to the slave for fifteen, because this contract, although valid in itself, has a semblance of non-existence, because the slave owns it, and what is in his hand is not free from his right, so it is considered non-existent with respect to murabahah, and consideration is given to the first. Thus it becomes as if the slave bought it for the master for ten in the first scenario, and as if he sold it to the master in the second scenario, so the first price is considered. And the mukatab is like the authorized slave because of the existence of suspicion, rather everyone who does not... [PAGE_186] ...like an authorized slave buying from his master, and the capital owner based on what his mudarib bought with half first, and half of what he profited from his second purchase from him. Then if the sold item becomes defective, or is deflowered while a virgin... [1] His statement: "because of its establishment"; meaning the establishment and validity of the contract due to the existence of debt despite the presence of a factor contrary to validity, which is that the slave is owned by the master, so it becomes as if he sold his own property to himself, and similarly in purchase. [2] His statement: "he says: it cost the capital owner... etc."; he says: it cost me twelve and a half. This is according to us, because each of them obtains ownership of possession through this contract, even if he does not obtain ownership of the property, so the contract is considered with a semblance of non-existence, because the mudarib is an agent of the capital owner in the first sale from him, so the second sale is considered non-existent with respect to half the profit. According to Zufar: It is not permissible for the capital owner to sell to the mudarib, nor for the mudarib to sell to him, due to the non-existence of profit, because profit is obtained when sold to a third party, since sale is the exchange of property for another's property, and he is buying his own property with his own property. [3] His statement: "or is deflowered"; while she is a virgin, and the deflowering does not diminish her, whether the deflowerer is her master or someone else; hence the passive voice is used, as in Majma' al-Anhur.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqayah, discussing specific rulings of murabahah sales involving related parties. The central issue is when a buyer (like a master from his authorized slave, or a mudarib from the capital owner) makes a second purchase of an item previously sold, how does that affect the cost basis for a subsequent murabahah sale? The author explains that according to Abu Hanifa, if a master buys from his authorized slave, the profit from the first sale is uncertain because the item might be returned due to a defect. But when the master buys it again (from a third party), that profit becomes confirmed. Therefore, when selling on murabahah, the master can base his cost on the first price (ten) because the second purchase only confirms the first profit. The author supports this with an analogy: witnesses who testify to a divorce before consummation confirm half the dower, even if they later retract, they become liable. Similarly, the semblance of confirmation here suffices to establish the ruling. The passage also details the condition that the slave must have debt encompassing his net worth; otherwise, the initial sale to the master is invalid as it gives nothing. If the master sells to the slave, it is also considered effectively non-existent for murabahah due to the conflict of interest. The same principle applies to mudarabah: if the mudarib buys from the capital owner, the second purchase is disregarded for half the profit, and the cost is averaged. Finally, the author notes Zufar's opposing view that such transactions are entirely invalid because profit only arises from sale to a stranger.

Key Terms
مضارب (mudarib): The agent in a mudarabah contract who manages trade with the capital provided by the capital owner.
رب المال (rabb al-mal): The capital owner (principal) in a mudarabah contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi majority, why is the second sale between mudarib and capital owner considered non-existent for half the profit?
A) Because the mudarib is an agent for the capital owner in the first purchase.
B) Because the sale is completely invalid.
C) Because the capital owner does not own the goods.
D) Because profit is only realized when both parties are unrelated.

Key Takeaway
In mudarabah, a second sale between capital owner and mudarib is treated as non-existent for half the profit, requiring an averaged cost for subsequent murabahah.

Murabahah Cost Basis for Inter-Related Sales
Ruling on murabahah cost base for inter-related sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct cost basis in a murabahah sale when the seller is legally connected to the buyer (master/slave or mudarib/owner) and apply the Hanafi principle of disregarding intermediate sales for profit calculation.

Core Idea
In murabahah, an intermediate sale between legally connected parties is ignored; cost is the original purchase price.

This lesson examines two situations where a sale occurs between parties who are not fully independent: a master and his authorized slave (ma'dhun), and a mudarib and the capital owner (rabb al-mal). In both cases, the Hanafi school treats the intermediate sale as non-existent for murabahah purposes, preserving the original cost basis. Understanding this prevents misrepresenting profit in cost-plus sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage discusses two distinct cases: (1) a master sells an item to his ma'dhun (authorized slave) and (2) a mudarib sells an item to the capital owner (rabb al-mal). In each, the resale on murabahah requires a specific cost basis.
Focus on the legal relationship between buyer and seller in each case.
2
Analyze the master-ma'dhun scenario
The master buys for 10, sells to ma'dhun for 15. The ma'dhun, when reselling on murabahah, states his cost as 10, not 15. This is because the sale between master and ma'dhun is considered legally non-existent for murabahah (though the sale itself is valid). The condition is that the ma'dhun's debts cover his value (muhit dinuhu biraqabatihi), giving him ownership.
If the ma'dhun has no debt, he has no ownership, so the second sale is definitely not considered; the ruling applies a fortiori.
3
Apply the principle to the mudarib-owner scenario
The mudarib buys a cloth for 10 under a mudaraba with half profit share, then sells it to the owner for 15. For murabahah, the owner's cost is 12.5 (10 original + half of 5 profit). This accounts for the profit-sharing nature of mudaraba; the inter-sale effectively distributes profit.
The mudarib's sale is valid but the profit element must be adjusted for murabahah.
4
Understand the rationale
Both rulings stem from the principle that a transaction between legally connected parties (master/ma'dhun as one entity in ownership, mudarib/owner as profit-sharers) is disregarded for cost calculation in murabahah to prevent circular or double counting of profit.
The Hanafi school emphasizes disclosure of true cost; ignoring the inter-sale ensures honesty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كمأذون شرى من سيده : أي إذا اشترى المولي بعشرة، ثم باعه من مأذونِهِ المحيط دينه برقبته بخمسة عشر ، فالمأذونُ إن باعَهُ مرابحة يقولُ قام عليَّ بعشرة ؛ لأن بيع المولي من عبده المأذون وشراءه منه اعتبر عدماً في حق المرابحة لثبوته مع المنافي. وإنما قال : المحيط دينه حينئذ يكونُ للعبد المأذون ملك، أمَّا المأذون الذي لا دين عليه فلا ملك له، فلا شبهة في أن البيع الثاني لا اعتبار له ، أما إذا كان عليه دين محيط فحينئذٍ يكونُ البيعُ الثَّاني بيعاً، ومع ذلك لا اعتبار له في حق المرابحة فيثبت الحكم بالطَّريقِ الأُولى فيما لا دين علي. ورب المال على ما شراهُ ضاربَهُ بالنصف أولاً ، ونصف ما ريح بشرائِهِ ثانياً منه : أي اشترى المضارب بالنصف ثوبا بعشرة وباعَهُ من رب المال بخمسة عشر، فالنُّوبُ قام على ربِّ المال باثني عشر ونصف .
Like a ma'dhun who bought from his master: i.e., if the master bought for ten, then sold it to his ma'dhun whose debt covers his value for fifteen, then the ma'dhun when he sells it on murabahah says, 'It cost me ten,' because the sale of the master to his ma'dhun servant and his purchase from him is considered non-existent regarding murabahah due to its being established despite the contrary. He only said 'whose debt covers his value' because then the ma'dhun servant has ownership. As for the ma'dhun who has no debt, he has no ownership, so there is no doubt that the second sale is not considered. But if he has a covering debt, then the second sale is a sale, yet it is still not considered regarding murabahah, so the ruling is established a fortiori for the one without debt. And the owner's capital for what his mudarib bought in the first half, and half of what he gained from his purchase in the second half: i.e., the mudarib bought a cloth for ten with half [profit share] and sold it to the owner for fifteen, so the cloth cost the owner twelve and a half.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage states a ruling regarding murabahah (cost-plus sale) in two scenarios involving a mudarib (agent in a mudaraba partnership) and the rabb al-mal (capital owner). The first scenario: a master buys an item for ten, then sells it to his own ma'dhun servant (who is authorized to trade and whose debts exceed his value) for fifteen. When the ma'dhun later resells it on murabahah, he must state his cost as ten (not fifteen) because the sale between master and servant is legally considered non-existent for murabahah purposes. This is because the master and servant are essentially one legal entity when the servant's debts equal his value; the sale is valid but ignored for profit calculation. The second scenario: a mudarib buys a cloth for ten under a mudaraba with half profit share, then sells it to the rabb al-mal for fifteen. For murabahah, the cost to the rabb al-mal is twelve and a half (the original ten plus half the profit, which is 2.5). This is because each party gains half the profit from this inter-sale, following the Hanafi principle that such transactions are valid but adjusted for murabahah.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave authorized by his master to engage in trade, with debts covering his value.
مرابحة (murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a known profit margin.
مضارب (mudarib): The agent in a mudaraba partnership who provides labor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the master-servant sale is invalid.
B) Because the sale is considered non-existent for murabahah due to legal unity.
C) Because the ma'dhun cannot make a profit.
D) Because the item's value did not increase.

Key Takeaway
In murabahah, an intermediate sale between legally connected parties does not alter the original cost basis.

Disclosure of Post-Purchase Defects in Murabahah
Ruling on whether seller must disclose defects occurring after purchase in murabahah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on disclosure of post-purchase defects in murabahah, contrast it with the views of Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, and identify the practical consequence (thamar al-khilaf).

Core Idea
In Hanafi murabahah, the seller need not disclose the cause of a defect because attributes have no fixed price share—but the defect itself must be disclosed to avoid cheating.

Murabahah is a cost-plus sale based on trust. A common question is whether the seller must disclose that a defect occurred in the item while it was in his possession (e.g., blindness). This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling, the opposing views of Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, and the reasoning behind the difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the core ruling: the seller does not have to mention that he bought the item sound and it became defective. The defect itself must still be disclosed to avoid cheating.
Disclosure of the defect (e.g., that the item is now blind) is obligatory; only the timing is not required.
2
Identify the opposing positions
Abu Yusuf (and Zufar) hold that the seller must disclose if the defect causes a price reduction that people would not overlook. Al-Shafi'i requires disclosure in all cases because attributes have a portion of the price.
3
Analyse the Hanafi dalil
The Hanafi reasoning: the price is for the substance; attributes are subsidiary and have no quantified share. The sale is built on trust, so the seller is not required to specify temporary changes.
This does not mean attributes never affect price—they do, but holistically, not as a separate element.
4
Analyse the opposing dalil
Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i argue that a defect reduces the price, so concealing the change is dishonest. Al-Shafi'i assigns a portion of the price to each attribute.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Hanafi, the seller may omit mention of the defect's timing. Under Abu Yusuf, if the decrease is significant, he must disclose. Under Shafi'i, he must always disclose. The practical effect is on the buyer's awareness and the seller's liability.
Later Hanafi authorities (e.g., Zufar, Abu al-Layth) considered Zufar's stricter view as stronger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رابح بلا بيان: أي لا يَجب عليه أن يقول إنِّي اشتريتها سليمة فاعورت في يدي، وعند أبي يوسف الله والشَّافِعِيُّ لزمَهُ بيانُ هذا؛ لأَنَّهُ لا شَكٍّ أَنَّهُ ينقصُ الشَّمنُ بالإعورار، وما قيل : إن الأوصاف لا يُقابلها شيءٌ مــن الثَّمن ، فمعناه أنَّ الأوصاف لا يكون لها حصة معلومة من الثَّمن لا أنَّ الثَّمَنَ لا يزيد بسبب الوصف ولا ينقُصُ بفواتِهِ، على أن هذا البيع مبني على الأمانة، فالاحتياطات السابقة لا تُناسب هذا.
[1] قوله : رابح ؛ لأنه لم يحتبس عنده شيء بمقابلة الثمن ؛ لأنَّ الفائت وصف، لا يقابله شيء من الثمن بمجرد العقد ؛ لكونه تبعاً ؛ ولهذا لو حدث بالمبيع عيب وهو قبل القبض لا يسقط شيء من الثمن غير أن المشتري يتخير بين اخذه وتركه. Profiting without disclosure: i.e., he does not have to say, 'I bought it sound, then it became blind in my hand.' According to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, he must disclose this, because there is no doubt that the price decreases due to blindness. And what was said that attributes are not compensated by anything from the price means that attributes do not have a known portion of the price, not that the price does not increase due to a description nor decrease due to its loss. Moreover, this sale is based on trust, so the previous cautions are not appropriate here.
[1] His saying: Profitable because he did not withhold anything in exchange for the price; because the lost attribute is not compensated by anything from the price merely by the contract, being subsidiary. Hence if a defect occurs in a sold item before possession, nothing is deducted from the price except that the buyer has the option to take or leave. [2] His saying: i.e., he does not have to say...; but disclosure of the defect itself is necessary due to his saying, 'Whoever cheats us is not of us,' so concealing it is not permissible. [3] His saying: according to Abu Yusuf; and what is understood from al-Hidayah is that it is a riwaya from him, and Zufar also said it. Abu al-Layth said: Zufar's view is better, and we adopt it, and it was preferred in al-Fath. There is a refined investigation here to be sought from the detailed works. It is said: if the decrease is of an amount that people do not overlook, he should not sell it on murabahah without disclosure. And from his words, it is understood that if it decreases due to a change in price by God's command, he does not have to specify, a fortiori that he bought it when it was expensive. Similarly, if the garment yellows due to prolonged storage or becomes dirty, as in al-Bahr. [4] His saying: and al-Shafi'i; based on his view that attributes have a portion of the price without distinction between the defect being from a heavenly calamity or human action, as in al-Inayah. [0] His saying: and what was said...; this repels an objection that since it is established that attributes are not compensated by anything from the price, how can it be true that the price decreases due to blindness? Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'Rābiḥ bilā bayān' (profiting without disclosure). It addresses the ruling in murabahah (cost-plus sale) when a defect occurs in the seller's possession after purchase. The main ruling, attributed to Abu Hanifa, is that the seller does not have to disclose such a defect (e.g., the item became blind) because the defect affects the attribute, not a quantified part of the price. The contract of murabahah is based on trust and the price is for the substance, not attributes which are subsidiary. However, Abu Yusuf (and Zufar) hold that the seller must disclose if the defect causes a price reduction that people would not overlook. Al-Shafi'i also requires disclosure, as his school assigns a portion of the price to attributes. The commentary then clarifies that this does not apply to defects that are not price-reducing (like normal wear) or changes due to market fluctuations. The Hanafi position is that disclosure of the defect itself is obligatory (due to the hadith against cheating), but the specific circumstance of the defect occurring in the seller's hand need not be mentioned. The passage ends with a resolution of an objection: attributes have no specific price share, but they do affect the value; thus a defect can lower the price even if not explicitly quantified.

Key Terms
إعورار (i'warar): Becoming blind (defect in the item).
ربح بلا بيان (ribh bila bayan): Making profit without disclosing that a defect occurred in the seller's possession.
وصف (wasf): Attribute of the item, such as being sound or blind.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the defect does not reduce the price.
B) Because attributes have no fixed portion of the price; they are subsidiary.
C) Because the buyer must assume all risks after sale.
D) Because the seller is not liable for defects after purchase.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi murabahah, the seller must disclose the defect itself, but not the fact that it occurred after his purchase, because attributes have no quantified price share.

Disclosure of Description Defects in Murābaḥah
Inter-madhhab dispute on description defects in murābaḥah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa does not require disclosure of description defects in murābaḥah and evaluate the opposing arguments.

Core Idea
In murābaḥah, description loss (like blindness) does not require disclosure because descriptions have no specified share of the price.

This lesson examines a key dispute in murābaḥah sales: must a seller disclose a lost quality (e.g., a slave's eye becoming blind) that occurred after purchase but before resale? We will follow the Hanafi reasoning that descriptions are subsidiary and not priced separately, versus the view of Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i who require disclosure for any value-affecting defect. The discussion also introduces Zufar's nuanced position, which is later preferred.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify Abu Hanifa's view: in a murābaḥah sale, the seller is not obliged to disclose a defect that is a loss of a description (e.g., an eye becoming blind).
This is based on the idea that descriptions are subsidiary and have no allocated portion of the price.
2
Identify the opposing view
Note that Abu Yusuf (within the Hanafi school) and al-Shafi'i require disclosure because the price undoubtedly decreases.
For al-Shafi'i, descriptions have a share of the price; for Abu Yusuf, trust demands full honesty.
3
Analyze the Hanafi dalil
Examine the claim that descriptions are not matched by anything from the price; the seller is truthful about the cost; the buyer should have asked.
The Hanafi response argues that the buyer's own negligence relieves the seller of the duty to volunteer information.
4
Analyze the opposing dalil
Understand the opposing argument: the price decreases due to the defect, so silence is deceptive.
Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i hold that any value-reducing defect must be disclosed.
5
Determine the tarjih
See that the author adopts Zufar's view: disclosure is required only if the defect reduces value by an amount people would not overlook.
This balances practicality with preventing deception.

Source Text
