كتاب البيع / باب الإقالة والمرابحة والتولية
رابح بلا بيان
رابح بلا بيان: أي لا يَجب عليه أن يقول إنِّي اشتريتها سليمة فاعورت في يدي، وعند أبي يوسف والشافعي لزمَهُ بيانُ هذا؛ لأَنَّهُ لا شَكٍّ أَنَّهُ ينقصُ الشَّمنُ بالإعورار، وما قيل: إن الأوصاف لا يُقابلها شيءٌ مــن الثَّمن، فمعناه أنَّ الأوصاف لا يكون لها حصة معلومة من الثَّمن لا أنَّ الثَّمَنَ لا يزيد بسبب الوصف ولا ينقُصُ بفواتِهِ، على أن هذا البيع مبني على الأمانة، فالاحتياطات السابقة لا تُناسب هذا.
[1] قوله: رابح؛ لأنه لم يحتبس عنده شيء بمقابلة الثمن؛ لأنَّ الفائت وصف، لا يقابله شيء من الثمن بمجرد العقد؛ لكونه تبعاً؛ ولهذا لو حدث بالمبيع عيب وهو قبل القبض لا يسقط شيء من الثمن غير أن المشتري يتخير بين أخذه وتركه.
[2] قوله: أي لا يجب عليه أن يقول...الخ؛ وأما بيان نفس العيب فلا بد منه؛ لقوله عليه السلام: «مَن غشنا فليس منا»، فلا يجوز إخفاؤه.
[3] قوله: عند أبي يوسف؛ والمفهوم من «الهداية» أنه رواية عنه، وبه قال زفر، وقال أبو الليث: وقول زفر أجود، وبه نأخذ، ورجحه في «الفتح»، وهاهنا بحث نفيس ليطلب من المبسوطات. ه: إنه إن نقصه قدراً لا يتغابنُ النَّاسُ فيه ولا يبيعه مرابحة بلا بيان، وعن ودل كلامه على أنه لو نقص بتغيرِ السعر بأمر الله لا يجب عليه أن يعين بالأولى أنه اشتراه في حال غلائه، وكذا لو اصفر الثوبُ بطولِ مكثِهِ أو توسّخ، كذا في «البحر»
[4] قوله: والشافعي؛ بناء على مذهبه أن للأوصاف خصته من الثمن من غير فصل بين ما كان التعيبُ بآفةٍ سماوية أو بصنع العباد، كذا في «العناية».
[0] قوله: وما قيل...الخ؛ هذا دفع دخل تقريره أنه لما ثبت أنّ الأوصاف لا يقابلها شيء من الثمن فكيف يصدقُ أنه ينقض الثَّمن بالإعورار، وحاصلُ الدفع: أولاً: أنه ليس معناه أنَّ الوصف لا دخل له في ازدياد الثمن ونقصانه، بل معناه أن الأوصاف لا يكون لها حصة معينة من الثمن، فإذا بيع الغلام الجميل بعشرة ألف درهم لا يكون قيمة ذاته ثمانية آلاف درهم، وألفان بمقابلة حسنه. وثانياً: أنَّ بناء المرابحة على الأمانة، فالاحتياطات السابقة لا تناسب كتمان العيب، والله تعالى ولي الغيب.
لكنَّا نُجيب بأنه لم يأتِ من البائع غرور، فإنَّه صادق في قوله: قامت علي بكذا، لكن المشتري اغتر فيه بحماقتِهِ فعليه أن يسألَهُ أَنَّكَ اشتريت بكذا سليمة أو معورة؛ ليَتَبَيَّنَ له الحال، فإذا قصَّرَ في ذلك لا يجب على البائع كشف حال لم يسأل عنها.
Book of Sales / Chapter of Rescission, Murābaḥah, and Tawliyah
Making a profit without disclosure: That is, it is not obligatory upon him to say, 'I bought it sound and it became blind in my hand.' And according to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, disclosure of this is required, because there is no doubt that the price decreases due to blindness. And what is said: that descriptions are not matched by anything from the price, its meaning is that descriptions do not have a specified share from the price, not that the price does not increase due to a description nor decrease due to its loss. Moreover, this sale is based on trust, so the aforementioned precautions are not suitable for this.
[1] His saying: 'Making a profit' because nothing is held back by him in exchange for the price; because what is lost is a description, nothing in the price corresponds to it by mere contract, because it is subsidiary; therefore, if a defect occurs in the sold item before possession, nothing of the price is dropped, but the buyer has the choice between taking it or leaving it.
[2] His saying: 'i.e., it is not obligatory for him to say... etc.'; as for disclosure of the defect itself, it is necessary because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'He who cheats us is not of us,' so concealing it is not permissible.
[3] His saying: 'According to Abu Yusuf'; and what is understood from al-Hidayah is that it is a transmission from him, and Zufar said it, and Abu al-Layth said: The statement of Zufar is better, and we adopt it, and it was preferred in al-Fath. And here there is a refined discussion to be sought from the extensive works. H: He said: If it decreases by an amount that people do not overlook in mutual dealings, he does not sell it on murābaḥah without disclosure. And some of his words indicate that if it decreased due to a change in price by the command of God, it is not obligatory for him to specify, a fortiori, that he bought it during its high price. Similarly, if the garment becomes yellow due to prolonged storage or becomes dirty, as in al-Bahr.
[4] His saying: 'And al-Shafi'i'; based on his school that descriptions have a share of the price without distinction between the defect being a heavenly affliction or an act of servants, as in al-Inayah.
[0] His saying: 'And what was said... etc.'; this is a rebuttal of an objection: its summary is that since it is established that descriptions are not matched by anything from the price, how can it be true that the price decreases due to blindness? The summary of the rebuttal is: First, it is not that the description has no role in the increase or decrease of the price, rather it means that descriptions do not have a specific share from the price. So if a handsome slave boy is sold for ten thousand dirhams, his intrinsic value is not eight thousand dirhams and two thousand for his beauty. Second, that the construction of murābaḥah is on trust, so the aforementioned precautions are not suitable for concealing the defect, and Allah is the knower of the unseen.
But we respond that there is no deception from the seller, for he is truthful in his saying: 'It cost me such-and-such,' but the buyer is deceived by his own foolishness; he should ask him: 'Did you buy it sound or blind?' so that the situation becomes clear to him. So if he is negligent in that, it is not obligatory upon the seller to disclose a state that he was not asked about.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the obligation of disclosure in a murābaḥah (cost-plus) sale. The discussion centers on whether a seller, when reselling an item at a profit, must inform the buyer of a defect that occurred after the seller's purchase but before the resale, specifically a defect that is a loss of a description (like an eye becoming blind) rather than a reduction in the physical substance of the item. Abu Hanifa's position, supported by the author, is that disclosure is not required for such a defect because the loss is merely a description, which does not have a specified share of the price at the time of the original contract. The author explains that descriptions are subsidiary and do not independently affect the price; therefore, the seller is truthful in stating the cost without mentioning the defect. The buyer, if concerned, should ask whether the item was sound or defective when purchased. Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, however, require disclosure because the defect undeniably reduces the market value, and any reduction affects the price. The author rebuts by distinguishing between the price of the actual item and the value attributed to descriptions; descriptions do not have a specific monetary share, so their loss does not require a price reduction in the murābaḥah transaction, which is built on trust. The author also notes a view from Zufar that if the defect is significant (amounting to a difference that people would not overlook), then disclosure is necessary. The passage ends with the Hanafi response that the buyer's own negligence in not asking absolves the seller of the obligation to volunteer the information.

Key Terms
مرابحة (murābaḥah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost price and adds a known profit margin.
وصف (waṣf): A description or quality of an item, considered subsidiary to the substance in determining the price.
إعوار (iʿwār): The condition of an eye becoming blind; a defect that is a loss of a description.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in this passage, when must a seller in a murābaḥah sale disclose that a slave's eye became blind after purchase?
A) Always, because it reduces the price.
B) Never, because it is a description loss.
C) Only if the reduction in value is significant (people would not overlook it).
D) Only if the buyer specifically asks.

Key Takeaway
Disclosure in murābaḥah depends on whether the defect affects a substantive part or merely a description; descriptions do not have a fixed share of the price.

Disclosure for Loss of a Part in Murābaḥah
Rulings on disclosure obligations in murābaḥah for specific defects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three cases where a seller must disclose a defect in murābaḥah and explain the underlying principle of 'retention of a part' that triggers the disclosure obligation.

Core Idea
Disclosure is required when a defect involves removal of a part (eye or hymen) because the seller retains that part's share of the price.

This passage from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāyah clarifies that in a murābaḥah sale, not every defect must be disclosed. The obligation arises specifically when the defect involves the physical removal of a part of the sold item—like an eye or the hymen—because the seller retains that part (or its compensation) mentally, making it unfair to sell the remainder at full profit without disclosure. We will examine the three cases given and the legal reasoning behind each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three specific cases
Read the passage and list the three situations where the seller must disclose a defect: gouged eye of a slave, deflowering of a virgin slave girl, and a mouse's gnawing (though the last is incomplete in the text).
The mouse gnawing case is cut off; focus on the first two for complete analysis.
2
Understand the rationale: 'retention of a part'
The text explains that in these cases, a part of the sold item (the eye or the hymen) is removed. The seller retains that part (or its value) in his ownership, so he cannot sell the rest for the full price without disclosing. This is the core principle.
Contrast with defects that do not remove a part, e.g., blindness (loss of function) or a mere scratch.
3
Distinguish between damage by another vs. self-inflicted
If the defect is caused by the slave itself (e.g., self-gouging), it is treated like a heavenly affliction—no disclosure needed. If caused by another (master or stranger), disclosure is required.
The passage says: 'if it is gouged by the act of the sold item itself, it is like being defective by a heavenly affliction'.
4
Apply the rule for third-party damage
Even if a stranger causes the damage and the seller does not take compensation (arsh), disclosure is still required because the seller has a right to compensation that he may claim later.
The text explicitly states: 'whether he took compensation or not'.
5
Generalize the principle
From these cases, derive a rule: disclosure is mandatory in murābaḥah when the defect involves the removal of a physical part of the item that has a distinct share of the price, provided the damage is caused by someone other than the item itself.
This principle can be extended by qiyas to similar cases (e.g., loss of a tooth or ear).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن فقئت، أو وطئت بكراً لزمه بيانُه، وقرض فأر
[1] قوله: وإن فقئت؛ بمباشرة الغير سواء فقأها المولى أو الأجنبي بأمر المولى أو بدونه، وإنما قلنا: بمباشرة الغير؛ لأنه إذا فقأ بفعل نفس المبيع فهو بمنزلة ما لو تعيَّبَ بآفة سماوية، وحكمه ما مرَّ من عدم لزوم البيان أو وطئت حال كونهما بكراً، سواء كان الواطئ مولاها أو غيره؛ لزمه بيانه: أي يبيعه مرابحة بشرط أن يبين البائع العيب حيث احتبس عنده جزء المبيع، وهو العذرة والعين. ولأنَّ إزالة العذرة وإخراج العين عند كونها في ملكه فلا يملك بيع الباقي بكل الثمن مرابحة وتولية، إذ الأوصافُ إذا صارت مقصودة بالإتلاف صار بها حصة من الثمن بلا خلاف أمّا إذا فقأها الأجنبي فيجب البيان أخذ أرشها أو لا؛ لأنه لما فقاً الأجنبي أوجب عليه ضمان الأرش، ووجوب ضمان الأرش سبب لأخذ الأرش فأخذ حكمه، كذا في «مجمع الأنهر»، وغيره.
[2] قوله: وقرض؛ - بالقاف المفتوحة -، وهكذا في سائر النسخ الموجودة عندنا، وهو مأخوذ من قرض الثوب بالمقراض: إذا قطعه. وفي «الكفاية» نقلا عن «الفوائدِ الظهيرية»: القرض: بفاء معجمة من فوقها
And if it (the eye) is gouged out, or if a virgin is deflowered, disclosure of it is required; and a mouse's gnawing.
[1] His saying: 'And if it is gouged out' – by the direct action of another, whether the master or a stranger, by the master's command or without it. And we only say: by the direct action of another, because if it is gouged by the act of the sold item itself, it is like it being defective by a heavenly affliction, and its ruling is what passed: no necessity of disclosure. Or she is deflowered while being a virgin, whether the fornicator is her master or someone else; disclosure is required: i.e., he sells it on murābaḥah with the condition that the seller discloses the defect, because a part of the sold item is retained by him, namely the hymen and the eye. And because removing the hymen and extracting the eye while they are in his ownership, he does not have the right to sell the remainder for the full price on murābaḥah or tawliyah, because when descriptions become intended by destruction, a share of the price comes to them without dispute. But if a stranger gouged it, disclosure is required whether he took compensation (arsh) or not, because when the stranger gouged it, it necessitated guaranteeing the arsh, and the obligation of guaranteeing the arsh is a cause for taking the arsh, so it takes its ruling. As in Majma' al-Anhur and others.
[2] His saying: 'And qarḍ' – with a fatah on the qaf, and likewise in all copies available to us. It is derived from 'qarḍ al-thawb bil-miqrāḍ' meaning 'he cut it'. And in al-Kifayah, quoting from al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyyah: 'al-qarḍ' with a fa' with three dots above.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage provides specific rulings on when a seller must disclose defects in a murābaḥah sale. The author discusses three cases: (1) If the eye of a slave is gouged out by the direct action of someone other than the slave itself, disclosure is required. (2) If a virgin slave girl is deflowered (whether by her master or someone else), disclosure is required. (3) If a mouse gnaws the garment (the text is cut off but indicates a ruling). The rationale is that in these cases, the defect involves the removal of a part (the eye or the hymen) that is considered a separate entity with a share of the price. The seller retains that part (the hymen or the eye) mentally, so he cannot sell the remainder for the full price without disclosing. If the damage is caused by the slave itself (self-inflicted), it is treated like a heavenly affliction, and no disclosure is needed. For the mouse gnawing, the author notes the spelling of the word and references a variant. The overall principle: when a defect involves the loss of a part of the item that has a distinct value, disclosure is obligatory.

Key Terms
فقء (faq'): To gouge out (the eye), causing the loss of the eye as a physical part.
عذرة (ʿudhrah): The hymen.
أرش (arsh): Compensation paid for a physical defect when someone else damages the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a slave's eye is gouged out by a stranger, what is the ruling regarding disclosure in a murābaḥah sale?
A) Disclosure is required only if the seller receives compensation (arsh).
B) Disclosure is required regardless of whether the seller takes compensation.
C) Disclosure is not required because it is caused by a stranger.
D) Disclosure is not required because it is like a heavenly affliction.

Key Takeaway
In murābaḥah, disclosure is wajib when the defect involves removal of a physical part (eye, hymen) caused by another, as the seller retains that part's value; self-inflicted damage is exempt.

Disclosure of Defects in Murābaḥah Sale
Rulings on when defects must be disclosed in cost-plus sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between defects that require disclosure in murābaḥah and those that do not, and explain the buyer's remedy when the seller conceals a credit term.

Core Idea
Disclosure is wajib only for defects that affect the price as a separate element; subsidiary attributes need not be disclosed.

This passage delves into the seller's obligation to disclose defects in a murābaḥah (cost-plus) sale. The Hanafi school draws a careful distinction: defects that occur through the seller's act or are directly tied to the price must be revealed, while pre-existing subsidiary attributes do not require disclosure. A special rule applies when the seller buys on credit and sells at a profit without mentioning the credit term—this is considered treachery, giving the buyer an option to rescind.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of defects
The passage distinguishes defects that are 'subsidiary attributes' (tābiʿah) vs. 'intended by destruction' (maqṣūdah bil-itlāf). For example, being one-eyed vs. being gouged.
This classification determines the disclosure obligation.
2
Apply the rule to the first scenario
For a slave purchased with a pre-existing condition (one-eyed), the seller need not disclose because the attribute is not specifically priced. But if the eye is gouged in his possession, disclosure is required.
The timing and nature of the defect matter.
3
Analyze the credit sale scenario
When the seller buys on credit (nasī'ah) and sells at a profit without disclosing, the credit term has a financial value separate from the item. Concealing it is treachery, so the buyer has khiyār (option).
The credit term is analogous to a separate item.
4
Compare with the sound-limbed slave objection
The objection that healthy limbs increase price yet need not be disclosed is answered: the increase is not specified, whereas credit price increase is stipulated.
Stipulation makes the difference.
5
Determine the buyer's remedy
If the buyer destroys the item before discovering the treachery, he must pay the full price. If he discovers before destruction, he can take it at full price or return it.
The option (khiyār) is similar to khiyār al-ʿayb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن فقئت، أو وطئت بكراً لزمه بيانُه، وقرض فأر. لكنَّا نُجيب بأنه لم يأتِ من البائع غرور، فإنَّه صادق في قوله: قامت علي بكذا، لكن المشتري اغتر فيه بحماقَتِهِ فعليه أن يسألَهُ أَنَّكَ اشتريت بكذا سليمة أو معورة؛ ليَتَبَيَّنَ له الحال، فإذا قصَّرَ في ذلك لا يَحِبُ على البائع كشف حال لم يسأل عنها. وإن فقئت، أو وطئت بكراً لزمه بيانُه، وقرض فأر. وقرض - بالقاف المفتوحة -، وهكذا في سائر النسخ الموجودة عندنا، وهو مأخوذ من قرض الثوب بالمقراض: إذا قطعه. وفي «الكفاية» نقلا عن «الفوائد الظهيرية»: القرض: بفاء معجمة من فوقها. وحرق نار للثوب المشترى كالأُولَى، وتكسره بنشره وطيه كالثانية. ومن شرى بنسأ ورابح بلا بيان خُيْرَ مشتريه، فإن أتلفَهُ ثُمَّ عَلِمَ لَزِمَهُ كُلُّ ثَمَنِه. قوله: كالأولى؛ أي كالمسألة الأولى في عدم لزوم البيان لما ذكر في الإعورار من أنَّ الأوصاف تابعة لا يقابلها الثمن. قوله: كالثانية؛ أي كالمسألة الثانية في لزوم البيان، لما ذكر في فقء العين من أنَّ الأوصاف صارت مقصودة بالإتلاف. قوله: من شرى؛ ثوباً مثلاً بنسأ أي بعشرة نسيئة مثلاً ورابح: أي باعه بربح واحد حالاً مثلاً بلا بيان: أي لم يبيّن أنه اشتراه نسيئة، فعَلِمَ المشتري خيانته، خُير مشتريه إن شاء أخذه بكل الثمن، أو ردَّه؛ لأنَّ للأجل شبهاً بالمبيع، ألا ترى أنه يزادُ في الثمن؛ لأجل الأجل. والشبهة في باب المرابحة تلحق بحقيقة المبيع احتياطاً، فصار كأنه اشترى شيئين وباع أحدهما مرابحة بثمنهما، وهذا ممنوع جدا، والإقدام على المرابحة يقتضي السلامة عن مثل هذه الخيانة، فإذا ظهرت الخيانة بعدم بيان أنه نسيئة يخيرُ في الأخذ والردّ، كما يُخيرُ عند ظهور العيب في المبيع. والحاصل أن عدم بيان أصل الأجل خيانة، وكذا بيان بعضه وإخفاء بعضه الآخر. فإن قيل: إنَّ الغلام السليم الأعضاء يزادُ في ثمنه؛ لأجل سلامة الأعضاء بالنسبة إلى غير السليم، وإن فاتت سلامة الأعضاء لم يجب البيانُ على البائع كما مرَّ في مسألة الاعورار. أجيب: بأن الزيادة هناك ليست منصوصاً عليها أنها في مقابلة السلامة، وما نحن فيه هو أن يقول: إن أجلتني مدَّةَ كذا فثمنه يكون كذا، بزيادة مقدار، فتثبت زيادة الثمن بالشرط، ولا يثبت ذلك في سلامة الأعضاء. قوله: فإن أتلفه؛ أي المشتري المبيع، بأن باعه أو بوجه آخر، ثمَّ عَلِمَ.
And if [the eye] is gouged, or [the slave] is deflowered while a virgin, disclosure becomes obligatory upon him, and a mouse’s gnawing. But we answer that no deception from the seller occurred, for he is truthful in his statement: 'It cost me such-and-such,' but the buyer is deceived by his own foolishness—he should have asked him: 'Did you buy it sound or defective?' so the matter becomes clear for him. If he failed in that, it is not incumbent upon the seller to disclose a situation about which he was not asked. And if [the eye] is gouged, or [the slave] is deflowered while a virgin, disclosure becomes obligatory, and a mouse’s gnawing. And 'qarḍ'—with an open qāf—and thus in all the copies we have, and it is derived from 'qarḍ al-thawb bil-miqrāḍ': when he cuts it. And in al-Kifāyah, quoting al-Fawā’id al-Ẓahīriyyah: al-qarḍ is with a dotted fā'. And burning fire on the purchased cloth is like the first case, and its tearing by unfolding and folding is like the second. And whoever buys on credit and then sells at a profit without disclosure, his buyer has the option: if he destroys it then later knows, the entire price is binding upon him. His statement: 'Like the first'—i.e., like the first case in the non-requirement of disclosure, as mentioned in the case of the one-eyed [slave] that attributes are subsidiary, not countervalued by the price. His statement: 'Like the second'—i.e., like the second case in the requirement of disclosure, as mentioned in the gouging of the eye that the attributes became intended by the destruction. His statement: 'Whoever buys'—e.g., a cloth on credit, i.e., for ten [dirhams] on credit, and then sells at a profit, e.g., immediately for a profit, without disclosure: i.e., he did not disclose that he bought it on credit. So the buyer learns of his treachery; his buyer has the option: if he wishes, he takes it at the full price, or he returns it, because the term [of credit] has resemblance to the sold item—do you not see that the price increases due to the term? And resemblance in the chapter of murābaḥah is attached to the reality of the sold item as a precaution, so it is as if he bought two things and sold one of them at a profit for the price of both. And this is strictly prohibited, and proceeding with murābaḥah requires safety from such treachery. So when the treachery appears from failing to disclose that it is on credit, he has the option to take or return, just as he has the option when a defect appears in the sold item. The summary is that failing to disclose the original term is treachery, and likewise disclosing part and hiding part of it. If it is said: 'A handsome, complete-limbed slave increases in price due to his sound limbs compared to an unsound one, and if the soundness of limbs is lost, disclosure is not required from the seller, as passed in the case of the one-eyed.' The answer: The increase there is not specified as being in exchange for the soundness, while in our case, he says: 'If you give me a term of such-and-such, then its price will be such-and-such, with an increase in amount.' So the price increase is established by the condition, and that does not apply to soundness of limbs. His statement: 'If he destroys it'—i.e., the buyer destroys the sold item, by selling it or in another way, then he knows.

Commentary
This passage discusses the obligation of disclosure (bayān) in murābaḥah (cost-plus sale). The author presents several scenarios: (1) If a slave's eye is gouged or the slave is deflowered while a virgin, the seller must disclose this defect. (2) However, if the defect is a mouse's gnawing, burning fire, or tearing by unfolding/folding, disclosure may or may not be required depending on the nature of the defect. The commentary explains that for defects that occur by the seller's action or are directly attributable to the seller's possession, disclosure is necessary because the attribute becomes a substantial part of the price. In contrast, for pre-existing defects like being one-eyed, disclosure is not required because attributes are subsidiary and not specifically priced. A key distinction is made regarding credit (nasī'ah): if the seller buys on credit and then sells at a profit without disclosing the credit term, the buyer has an option to rescind or accept at the full price. This is because the credit term has a financial value similar to a separate item, and concealing it is considered treachery (khiyānah). The argument against analogizing to the sound-limbed slave is that the price increase for credit is explicitly stipulated, whereas the increase for soundness is not specified.

Key Terms
مرابحة (murābaḥah): A sale where the seller discloses the original cost and adds a known profit margin.
بيان (bayān): Disclosure of a defect or condition affecting the value of the sold item.
نسيئة (nasī'ah): A sale on credit where payment is deferred.
غرور (ghurūr): Deception; here, it is not imputed to the seller if he was truthful about his cost.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a murābaḥah sale, if the seller bought an item on credit (nasī'ah) and then sells it at a profit without disclosing the credit term, what is the buyer's right?
A) The buyer must accept the sale at the stated profit.
B) The buyer has the option to either take the item at the full price or return it.
C) The sale is void automatically.
D) The buyer can only demand a reduction in price.

Key Takeaway
In murābaḥah, the seller must only disclose defects that are directly tied to the price (like credit terms) or occur in his possession; subsidiary attributes need not be disclosed.

Murabaha with Hidden Credit: Buyer's Option (Khiyar)
Sharh explaining the ruling and its analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for murabaha when the seller fails to disclose a credit purchase, and explain the proof (istihsan) and the analogy (shabah) used to establish the buyer's option.

Core Idea
Hidden credit is like a defect: the buyer gets the option to return because the term (ajal) resembles the sold item and affects the price.

In murabaha (cost-plus sale), full transparency is required. If a seller buys an item on credit and then sells it immediately at a profit without disclosing the credit, the buyer may feel cheated. The Hanafi school gives the buyer the option (khiyar) to accept or return the item, treating the hidden credit as akin to a defect. This lesson unpacks the ruling from the sharh, its proof via istihsan, and the hidden analogy that supports it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Determine the exact ruling stated: the murabaha contract is valid but the buyer has the option to take or return upon discovering the hidden credit.
2
Trace the proof (istihsan)
The text uses istihsan (juristic preference) because a strict analogy would not give the option. The credit term (ajal) resembles the sold item in that it directly affects the price, so it is treated like a defect.
The proof is not a text but a reasoning of shabah and precaution.
3
Analyze the analogy (shabah)
The hidden credit is analogized to a defect: just as a buyer can return a defective item, here he can return because the undisclosed credit undermines his consent. The passage refutes a counter-analogy (healthy slave) by noting the price increase is explicitly conditioned on credit.
This is a qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) based on the illa of price-affecting attribute.
4
Apply the ruling to the destruction case
If the buyer destroys the item before learning of the hidden credit, he must pay the full price because the option to return is no longer possible and the semblance of cheating does not reduce the price.
5
Note the scope and exceptions
The option applies only when the credit is entirely hidden; if the seller discloses partly, it is still cheating. The ruling does not apply to attributes that are not explicitly priced, like health of a slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحرق نار للثوب المشترى كالأولى، وتكسره بنشره وطيه كالثانية. ومن شرى بنسأ ورابح بلا بيان خُير مشتريه، فإن أتلفه ثم علم لزمه كل ثمنه. نص على هذا صدر الإسلام أبو اليسير. قوله: كالأولى؛ أي كالمسألة الأولى في عدم لزوم البيان لما ذكر في الأعور من أن الأوصاف تابعة لا يقابلها الثمن. قوله: كالثانية؛ أي كالمسألة الثانية في لزوم البيان، لما ذكر في فقء العين من أن الأوصاف صارت مقصودة بالإتلاف. قوله: من شرى؛ ثوباً مثلاً بنسأ أي بعشرة نسيئة مثلاً ورابح: أي باعه بربح واحد حالاً مثلاً بلا بيان: أي لم يبين أنه اشتراه نسيئة، فعلم المشتري خيانته، خُير مشتريه إن شاء أخذه بكل الثمن، أو رده؛ لأن للأجل شبهاً بالمبيع، ألا ترى أنه يزاد في الثمن؛ لأجل الأجل. والشبهة في باب المرابحة تلحق بحقيقة المبيع احتياطاً، فصار كأنه اشترى شيئين وباع أحدهما مرابحة بثمنهما، وهذا ممنوع جدا، والإقدام على المرابحة يقتضي السلامة عن مثل هذه الخيانة، فإذا ظهرت الخيانة بعدم بيان أنه نسيئة يخير في الأخذ والرد، كما يخير عند ظهور العيب في المبيع. والحاصل أن عدم بيان أصل الأجل خيانة، وكذا بيان بعضه وإخفاء بعضه الآخر. فإن قيل: إن الغلام السليم الأعضاء يزاد في ثمنه؛ لأجل سلامة الأعضاء بالنسبة إلى غير السليم، وإن فاتت سلامة الأعضاء لم يجب البيان على البائع كما مر في مسألة الأعور. أجيب: بأن الزيادة هناك ليست منصوصاً عليها أنها في مقابلة السلامة، وما نحن فيه أن يقول: إن أجلتني مدة كذا فثمنه يكون كذا، بزيادة مقدار، فتثبت زيادة الثمن بالشرط، ولا يثبت ذلك في سلامة الأعضاء، كذا في «العناية». قوله : فإن أتلفه؛ أي المشتري المبيع، بأن باعه أو بوجه آخر، ثم علم المشتري أن باعه كان اشتراه بنسأ، لزمه كل ثمنه المسمى، إذ ليس له إلا ولاية الرد، وهو غير ممكن لإتلافه، وأما أن يسقط شيء من الثمن بعد الإتلاف فلا؛ لأن في المسألة شبهة الخيانة لا حقيقتها، وفي الإسقاط يلزم خلاف المفروض.
And burning of fire for the purchased cloth is like the first, and its breaking by spreading and folding is like the second. And whoever buys on credit and sells at profit without disclosure, his buyer has the option. If he destroys it and then knows, he is liable for the entire price. The commentator said: 'like the first' means like the first case in that disclosure is not necessary because attributes are subsidiary and not met by price. 'Like the second' means like the second case in that disclosure is necessary because attributes become intended through destruction. He said: 'whoever buys' a cloth for example on credit, i.e., for ten dirhams on credit, and makes a profit, i.e., sells it for a profit immediately without disclosure, i.e., he did not disclose that he bought it on credit. When the buyer learns of his cheating, he has the option: if he wishes he takes it at the full price, or returns it. Because the term has similarity to the sold item. Do you not see that the price increases because of the term? And the resemblance in the chapter of murabaha is attached to the reality of the sold item by way of precaution. So it becomes as if he bought two things and sold one of them at profit with the price of both, but this is absolutely prevented. Undertaking murabaha requires safety from such cheating. So when the cheating appears by not disclosing that it was on credit, he has the option to take or return, just as he has the option when a defect appears in the sold item. In summary, not disclosing the principle of the term is cheating, as is disclosing some and hiding the rest. If it is said that a healthy-limbed slave increases in price because of the soundness of limbs compared to one not sound, and if the soundness of limbs is lost, the seller is not required to disclose, as passed in the case of the one-eyed. The answer is that the increase there is not explicitly stated to be in exchange for safety, whereas in our case he says: if you give me a term of such-and-such, then its price is such-and-such with an extra amount. Thus the increase in price is established by condition, and that is not established in soundness of limbs. So it is in al-‘Inaya. He said: 'if he destroys it' i.e., the buyer destroys the sold item, by selling it or in another way, then learns that the seller had bought it on credit, then he is liable for the entire named price, because he only has the authority to return, and it is not possible due to destruction. And that nothing of the price is dropped after destruction because in the case there is a semblance of cheating, not its reality, and in dropping it would contradict what was assumed.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage from ‘Umdat al-Ri‘ayah explains a key ruling in the chapter of murabaha (cost-plus sale). The matn states three scenarios: (1) a fire burns the purchased cloth, (2) it is broken by spreading and folding, and (3) the main case: someone buys an item on credit (nasi’ah) and then sells it at profit without disclosing the credit. In the first two scenarios, the rulings differ: for burning, no disclosure needed; for breaking, disclosure is required. The commentator explains that the first scenario is like the earlier case of a one-eyed slave (a‘war) where defects subsidiary to the sale need not be disclosed because the price is not in exchange for those attributes. The second scenario is like the case of poking out an eye (faq’ al-‘ayn) where the defect becomes the object of the sale through destruction. The core ruling is that in murabaha, if the seller fails to disclose that the original purchase was on credit, the buyer has the option (khiyar) to either accept the item at the stated price or return it. The rationale is that the credit term has a resemblance (shabah) to the sold item itself because it affects the price. The school applies istihsan (juristic preference) to treat this hidden term as akin to a defect, giving the buyer the option as in the case of a hidden defect. The passage then refutes an objection: if a healthy slave commands a higher price without requiring disclosure of health, why here? The answer is that the price increase for health is not explicitly conditioned, whereas the increase for credit is explicitly tied to the term. Finally, if the buyer destroys the item before learning of the hidden credit, he must pay the full price because the option to return is no longer possible, and no reduction is allowed since the matter involves a semblance of cheating, not actual fraud.

Key Terms
مرابحة (murābaḥah): A sale in which the seller discloses the original cost and adds a profit margin.
نسيئة (nasī’ah): A credit sale where payment is deferred to a later date.
خيار (khiyār): An option or right to rescind a contract upon discovering a defect or misrepresentation.
أجل (ajal): A fixed term or period for payment; here it refers to the credit duration.
شبهة (shubhah): A semblance or resemblance that creates doubt; in this context, the credit term has a resemblance to the sold item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the status of the murabaha contract when the seller fails to disclose that he bought the item on credit?
A) The contract is void (batil) and the buyer gets a refund.
B) The contract is valid but the buyer has the option to return the item.
C) The contract is valid with no option because credit is not a defect.
D) The contract is suspended (mawquf) until the seller discloses.

Key Takeaway
Hidden credit in murabaha is akin to a defect: the buyer gets the option to return because the term (ajal) resembles the sold item and affects the price, justified by istihsan.

Tawliya Sale: Buyer's Ignorance and Intra-Hanafi Compensation Dispute
Sharh of tawliya ruling and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on post-destruction compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a tawliya sale when the buyer is ignorant of the cost, explain the three Hanafi positions on compensation after destruction, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
If the buyer destroys the item and later discovers a hidden credit, the mufta bihi is to return only the difference between cash and credit prices.

Tawliya is a sale at cost. Ignorance of the original price invalidates the sale. But what happens if the buyer destroys the item and then learns that the seller had bought it on credit? Imams within the Hanafi school differ: some hold the buyer liable for the full price, others for the item's value, and the preferred view limits liability to the price difference. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the baseline ruling
The passage begins by stating that tawliya is like murabaha: the buyer has an option while the item exists, but no recourse if it is destroyed. If the buyer does not know the original cost, the sale is fasid due to jahalah.
2
Identify the disputing scenario
Focus on the case where the buyer destroys the item and later learns that the seller had purchased it on credit (i.e., a hidden term). The question is: what must the buyer pay?
3
Analyze each intra-Hanafi position
Three views are presented: (1) Abu Hanifa implicitly holds that the buyer pays the full cash price because return is impossible. (2) Abu Yusuf holds that the buyer returns the item's value (qimah) and reclaims the full price paid, analogizing to counterfeit coins. (3) Abu Ja'far holds that the buyer returns only the difference (fadl) between the cash price and the credit price.
Abu Yusuf's analogy: if someone unknowingly spends counterfeit coins, he must compensate the value.
4
Confirm the mufta bihi
The passage declares that the preferred view for fatwa is Abu Ja'far's: the buyer returns the difference between the cash and credit prices. This is taken from al-Bahr al-Raiq.
5
Consider the custom issue
The passage also discusses whether a customary installment plan must be disclosed. Two opinions are given: one requires disclosure (custom is like a condition), the other does not (price is just money). This is a secondary point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وكذا التولية؛ أي هي مثل المرابحة فيما ذكرناه من الخيار عند قيام المبيع، وعدم الرجوع حال هلاك لابتنائها على الثمن الأول، وينبغي أن يجعل قوله : وكذا التولية عائد إلى جميع ما ذكره للمرابحة، فلا بد من البيان في التولية أيضاً، في التعييب ووطء البكر، وبدونه في التعييب ووطء الثيب. وعن أبي يوسف له أنه يرد القيمة ويسترد كل الثمن، وهو نظير ما إذا استوفى الزيوف مكان الجياد، وعلم بعد الإنفاق، وقيل: يقوم بثمن حال ومؤجل، فيرجع بفضل ما بينهما، كذا في «الهداية». وقال أبو جعفر: المختار للفتوى الرجوع بفضل ما بينهما، كذا في «البحر»، ولو لم يكن الأجل مشروطاً في العقد، ولكنه معتاد التنجيم، قيل: لا بد من بيانه لأن المعروف كالمشروط، وقيل: يبيعه ولا يبينه؛ لأن الثمن ما كان إلا مالاً في العقد، أما لو فرضنا أنه باعه بلا شرط أجل، فلم ينفذه إلى شهر مطلاً، فلا شك أنه يبيعه مرابحة بالألف، كذا في «فتح القدير». قوله: فإن ولى رجلاً شيئاً أي باع له بالتولية، والحال أنه لم يعلم مشتريه قدره: أي بكم قام هذا الشيء على المولى فسد البيع؛ لجهالة الثمن، وجهالته تفضي إلى المنازعة، وهي تفسد البيع.
He said: 'And likewise tawliya' i.e., it is like murabaha in what we mentioned of the option when the sold item exists, and no recourse in case of destruction because it is based on the first price. And it is appropriate to make his statement 'and likewise tawliya' return to all that he mentioned for murabaha, so disclosure is necessary in tawliya as well, in deflowering and defloration of a virgin, and without it in deflowering and defloration of a non-virgin. And from Abu Yusuf that he returns the value and reclaims the entire price, and it is analogous to when one receives counterfeit coins in place of good ones and learns after spending. And it is said: he values it at cash and credit prices, then returns the excess between them. So it is in al-Hidayah. And Abu Ja'far said: the preferred for fatwa is the return of the excess between them. So it is in al-Bahr. And if the term was not stipulated in the contract, but it is customary to pay in installments, it is said: he must disclose it because what is customary is like a stipulated condition. And it is said: he sells it without disclosing it because the price is only money in the contract. However, if we suppose that he sold it without stipulating a term, and then did not deliver it for a month by delaying, there is no doubt that he sells it at profit for one thousand. So it is in Fath al-Qadir. He said: 'if he sold by tawliya' a man something, i.e., he sold to him by tawliya, while his buyer did not know its amount, i.e., how much this item cost the seller, then the sale is fasid due to ignorance of the price, and ignorance leads to dispute, and it invalidates the sale.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage from ‘Umdat al-Ri‘ayah discusses tawliya (sale at cost). The commentator first states that tawliya is similar to murabaha regarding the buyer's option when the item still exists, but no recourse if destroyed, because it is based on the original cost. The phrase 'and likewise tawliya' applies to all the rules mentioned for murabaha, including the need for disclosure of defects. The passage then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: when the buyer destroys the item and later learns of a hidden credit term, Abu Yusuf holds that the buyer should return the value of the item (qimah) and reclaim the entire price paid, analogizing to the case where someone receives counterfeit coins thinking they are good ones and then spends them. Another view (attributed to Abu Hanifa by implication) is that the buyer pays the full price. However, Abu Ja‘far states that the preferred fatwa is that the buyer returns only the difference between the cash price and the credit price. The passage also discusses the case where credit was not stipulated but is customary: two opinions are given—one requiring disclosure because custom is like a condition, and the other not requiring disclosure because the price is simply money. Finally, it reiterates that if in tawliya the buyer does not know the original cost, the sale is fasid (invalid) due to ignorance of the price, which leads to dispute.

Key Terms
تولية (tawliyah): A sale at the original cost price without profit.
جهالة (jahālah): Ignorance or uncertainty regarding a contract's essential element, such as price.
فاسد (fāsid): An invalid contract that is not void ab initio but is defective and requires rectification or cancellation.
قيمة (qīmah): The market value or worth of an item.
فضل (faḍl): Excess or difference between two prices (here, between cash and credit prices).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred position for fatwa, what does a buyer in a tawliya sale who destroys the item and then learns of a hidden credit term have to do?
A) Pay the full cash price.
B) Return the value of the item and reclaim the full price paid.
C) Return the difference between the cash price and the credit price.
D) The sale is void and no payment is needed.

Key Takeaway
In a tawliya sale where the buyer is ignorant of the cost, the sale is fasid; if the buyer destroys the item and later discovers a hidden credit, the mufta bihi is to return only the price difference.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Compensation After Consuming in an Invalid Tawliyah Sale
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tawliyah compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three Hanafi positions on compensation when a buyer consumes an item after an invalid tawliyah sale and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
When a tawliyah sale is invalid due to unknown cost, the consumed item requires compensation — but Hanafi authorities differ on how much.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a practical problem: what happens when a tawliyah sale is invalid because the buyer didn't know the cost, and then the buyer consumes the item? The primary ruling says the full price is due, but Abu Yusuf and later authorities offer alternative remedies. Understanding this dispute clarifies how Hanafi jurists balance fairness to both parties when perfect justice is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling
The passage first states that a tawliyah sale is fasid if the buyer does not know the cost. If the buyer learns the cost in the session, he has a choice (khiyar) to accept or reject.
This is the base ruling from the matn.
2
Recognize the problem after consumption
If the buyer consumes the item while still ignorant, the right of return is impossible. The primary ruling holds that the full named price is binding because there is only a semblance of betrayal, not actual betrayal.
The primary view emphasizes the seller's presumed honesty.
3
Study Abu Yusuf's analogy
Abu Yusuf analogizes to the case of defective coins: the buyer returns the value (qima) and recovers the full price. He argues that since the buyer cannot return the item, he should not suffer loss.
This is a qiyas-based reasoning.
4
Analyze the mufta bihi compromise
Abu Ja'far (al-Hinduwani) proposes that the buyer appraises the item at both cash and deferred prices and recovers the difference. This balances the interests: the seller keeps the contract price, and the buyer is compensated for the difference in value.
This view is recorded in al-Hidayah and al-Bahr and is chosen for fatwa.
5
Apply the mufta bihi ruling
If a modern case arises where a tawliyah sale is invalid and the item is consumed, the mufta bihi remedy is to calculate the spot and deferred values and award the difference to the buyer.
This is the operative fatwa today.

وقال أبو جعفر : المختار للفتوى الرجوع بفضل ما بينهما ، كذا في «البحر») ، ولو لم يكن الأجل مشروطاً في العقد ، ولكنَّه معتاد التنجيم، قيل: لا بدَّ من بيانِهِ لأن المعروف كالمشروط ، وقيل : يبيعه ولا يبينه ؛ لأنَّ الثمن ما كان إلا مالاً في العقد، أما لو فرضنا أنه باعه بلا شرط أجل، فلم ينفذه إلى شهر مطلاً ، فلا شك أنه يبيعه مرابحة بالألف ، كذا في «فتح القدير». [٢]قوله : فإن ولى ؛ رجلاً شيئاً أي باع له بالتولية، والحال أنه لم يعلم مشتريه (۳) قدره : أي بكم قام هذا الشيء على المولى فسد البيع ؛ لجهالة الثمن، وجهالته تفضي إلى المنازعة، وهي تفسد البيع.
فإن علم المشتري بكم قامَ عليه في المجلس قبل الافتراق خير المشتري بين أخذه وتركه ؛ لأنَّ جهالة الثمن فسادٌ في صلب العقد إلا أنه في مجلس العقدِ غير متقرّر ، فإذا علم في المجلس ارتفعت الجهالة، وإنّما يتخيّر ؛ لأنَّ الرِّضاء لم يتم قبله ؛ لعدم العلم كما في خيار الرؤية، وإن علم بعد الافتراق لا يصح البيع لتقرر الفساد، فلا يقبلُ الإصلاح.
Cream of the End for the Support of the Care. And similarly al-tawliyah. If he makes a tawliyah sale based on what it cost him, but the buyer does not know its amount, the sale is invalid. If he learns in the session, he has a choice. [1] And similarly al-tawliyah: if he makes a tawliyah sale based on what it cost him, and the buyer does not know its amount, the sale is invalid. If he learns in the session, the buyer has a choice: if he sold it, he had bought it on credit, then the entire named price is binding on him, for he only has the right of return, which is impossible because of his having consumed it. As for dropping something from the price after consumption, that is not allowed because in the matter there is a semblance of betrayal, not its reality, and in dropping [the price] would contradict the presumption. His statement: 'And similarly al-tawliyah' means it is like al-murabahah in what we mentioned of the option when the sold item exists, and non-recourse at the time of destruction because it is based on the first price. And it is appropriate to make his statement 'and similarly al-tawliyah' refer to everything he mentioned for murabahah, so disclosure is necessary in tawliyah as well, regarding defects and defloration of a virgin, and without it regarding defects and defloration of a non-virgin. And from Abu Yusuf: he returns the value and recovers the entire price, and this is analogous to the case where he took defective coins instead of good ones and learned after spending. And it is said: he appraises at a spot price and a deferred price, and recovers the difference between them, as in al-Hidayah. And Abu Ja'far said: the chosen for fatwa is to recover the difference between them, as in al-Bahr. And if the deferment was not stipulated in the contract but installment is customary, it is said: disclosure is necessary because the customary is like the stipulated, and it is said: he sells it and does not disclose it because the price was nothing but wealth in the contract. But if we assume that he sold without a stipulation of deferment and then delayed it for a month without payment, there is no doubt that he sells it as murabahah for one thousand, as in Fath al-Qadir. [2] His statement: 'then he makes a tawliyah' a man something, i.e., he sold it for him as tawliyah, while the buyer did not know its amount: i.e., how much this item cost the original seller, so the sale is invalid due to ignorance of the price, and ignorance of it leads to dispute, which invalidates the sale. If the buyer learns how much it cost him in the session before separation, the buyer has a choice between taking and leaving, because ignorance of the price is a defect in the essence of the contract, except that it is not settled in the session of the contract. So when he learns in the session, the ignorance is removed. And he has a choice because consent was not complete before that due to lack of knowledge, as in the option of inspection. And if he learns after separation, the sale is not valid because the defect is settled, so it does not accept correction.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya (a supercommentary on Sharh al-Wiqaya) discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding tawliyah (a sale where the seller passes on the item at cost) when the buyer is ignorant of the cost price and later consumes the item. The primary ruling, attributed to the earlier text, holds that if the buyer consumes the item after an invalid sale due to ignorance, he must pay the full named price because he only has the right to return (which is impossible) and no deduction is allowed, as the case involves only a semblance of betrayal (shubhat al-khiyana) not its reality. However, Abu Yusuf analogizes it to the case of defective coins: the buyer returns the value (qima) and recovers the full price. The mufta bihi view, as stated by Abu Ja'far (al-Hinduwani?), is that the buyer appraises the item at both a spot (cash) price and a deferred price, and recovers the difference between them. This is the chosen fatwa in the later Hanafi tradition. The passage also clarifies that in tawliyah, disclosure of defects and other conditions is required, similar to murabahah. The issue of customary deferment (ta'njim) is also discussed: if installment payment is customary but not stipulated, some say it must be disclosed (since custom is like a condition), while others say no disclosure is needed because the price is simply money.

Key Terms
تولية (tawliyah): A type of sale where the seller transfers ownership of an item at the exact cost he paid, without profit or loss.
مرابحة (murabahah): A sale where the seller explicitly states the cost and adds a known profit margin.
المجلس (al-majlis): The contractual session where the parties meet to negotiate and conclude the sale; in this context, the option to void the sale due to ignorance persists until the parties separate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi view in the passage, how is compensation determined when a buyer consumes an item after an invalid tawliyah sale due to ignorance of the cost?
A) The buyer pays the full named price with no deduction.
B) The buyer returns the value of the item and recovers the entire price paid.
C) The buyer appraises the item at spot and deferred price and recovers the difference.
D) The buyer returns the item if possible, or pays nothing.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi view for compensation after consuming an item in an invalid tawliyah sale is to recover the difference between the cash and deferred market values.

Ruling on Buyer Learning the Price in the Sale Session
Ruling on buyer's knowledge of price in the session

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a buyer learns the price during the contract session versus after parting, and explain the legal basis for the difference.

Core Idea
Ignorance of the price invalidates a sale, but if knowledge occurs in the session, the sale is saved; after the session, it is void.

This passage addresses a critical moment in sales: when the buyer discovers the actual price after agreeing to a sale. The Hanafi school holds that ignorance of the price is a fundamental defect, but the timing of discovery determines the outcome. Understanding this ruling clarifies the importance of the contract session (majlis) in curing defects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The buyer enters a sale without knowing the exact price (e.g., a murabaha where cost is to be disclosed).
2
Note the two timings
Knowledge may occur before parting (in the session) or after parting (after the session ends).
3
Apply the ruling for each timing
If in session: buyer has an option (khiyar) to take or leave. If after: sale is invalid (fasid) and cannot be corrected.
The session ends when the parties physically separate or explicitly terminate the contract.
4
Understand the reason
Ignorance of price is a corruption 'fi salb al-aqd' but it is not yet established (ghair mutaqarrir) during the session. Knowledge before parting removes the defect.
In Hanafi usul, a defect that is not yet fixed can be cured.
5
Compare to khiyar al-ru'yah
The author analogizes to the option of inspection: just as a buyer who hasn't seen the item has an option upon seeing it, here the buyer has an option upon learning the price, because consent depends on knowledge.
Both cases involve removing ignorance to perfect consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن علم المشتري بكم قامَ عليه في المجلس قبل الافتراق خير المشتري بين أخذه وتركه ؛ لأنَّ جهالة الثمن فسادٌ في صلب العقد إلا أنه في مجلس العقدِ غير متقرّر ، فإذا علم في المجلس ارتفعت الجهالة، وإنّما يتخيّر ؛ لأنَّ الرِّضاء لم يتم قبله ؛ لعدم العلم كما في خيار الرؤية، وإن علم بعد الافتراق لا يصح البيع لتقرر الفساد، فلا يقبلُ الإصلاح. وجي
If the buyer knows how much it cost him in the session before parting, the buyer has the choice between taking it and leaving it; because ignorance of the price is a corruption in the very contract, except that in the session of contract it is not yet established. So when he knows in the session, the ignorance is removed, and he only has a choice because consent was not completed before due to lack of knowledge, as in the option of inspection. And if he knows after parting, the sale is not valid because the corruption is established, so it does not accept correction.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of al-Hidayah/ al-Wiqayah. It addresses the situation where a buyer enters a sale without knowing the exact cost (in a murabaha or similar transaction). The ruling states that if the buyer learns the price before the session ends, he has an option (khiyar) to either proceed or cancel. The reason is that ignorance of the price is a fundamental defect, but during the session it is not yet fixed; once knowledge is obtained, the defect is removed. However, if he only learns after the session concludes, the defect is established and the sale becomes invalid and cannot be rectified. This is similar to the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah) in that consent depends on knowledge.

Key Terms
المجلس (al-majlis): The session of the contract, from commencement until the parties separate or decide to finalize.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option of inspection allowing the buyer to cancel a sale upon seeing the object if it does not meet expectations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if the buyer learns the actual price after leaving the contract session?
A) The sale is valid but he has the option of inspection.
B) The sale is invalid and cannot be corrected.
C) The sale is valid only if he accepts immediately.
D) The sale becomes a fāsid sale that can be rectified.

Key Takeaway
The timing of learning the price determines the validity of the sale: learning in the session gives an option, learning after the session invalidates the sale and cannot be cured.

Selling Before Possession: The Rule and Its Exception for Real Estate
Ruling on sale before possession with exception for real estate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling before possession, identify the exception for real estate and its condition, and explain the underlying cause (gharar).

Core Idea
Selling what you haven't taken possession of is void due to gharar, except for real estate safe from destruction.

In Hanafi fiqh, a buyer cannot resell a purchased item before taking possession because of the risk (gharar) that the first sale might dissolve. This lesson unpacks that ruling, its exception for real estate, and the causal reasoning that distinguishes this situation from an original seller's sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
Locate the statement: 'لم يجز بيع مشترى قبل قبضه' – it is not permissible to sell what has been purchased before taking possession. This is the foundational ruling.
The rule applies to all movable property unless specified.
2
Examine the Exception
Note the exception: 'إلا في العقار' – except real estate. But read the qualification from the commentary: it applies only when the real estate is not in a place where destruction is feared.
If the real estate is at risk (e.g., on a seashore), it is treated like movables.
3
Understand the Causal Explanation
The commentary explains that the prohibition is due to gharar: if the item is destroyed before possession, the first sale dissolves and the reseller would be selling another's property.
This gharar makes the resale void (batil).
4
Distinguish from the First Sale
The passage contrasts this with the original sale: in the first sale, the seller always sells his own property regardless of destruction, so no gharar exists. This distinction clarifies why the first sale is valid but the resale is not.
The difference hinges on ownership status before and after the sale.

Source Text

[۲] قوله : إلا في العقار ؛ وقيَّده في «التنوير» (۱) بما لا يخشى هلاكه ؛ لأنه لو كان بموضع لا يؤمن عليه ذلك ، لا يجوز كالمنقول ، ذكره في «البحر» (۲) معزياً إلى المحبوبي، وقال في «الاختيار»: حتى لو كان على شط البحر، أو كان المبيع علوا لا يجوز بيعه قبل القبض. انتهى. وقال في «النهاية»: قال بعض أصحابنا : في موضع لا يؤمن عليه ذلك، لا يجوز عنده كما في المنقول. وجوابه : في موضع لا يخشى عليه أن تصير بحرا، أو يغلب عليه الزيادة. انتهى.

Chapter: On explaining disposal of the sold item and price before its possession. And it is not permissible to sell what has been purchased before taking possession except in real estate. [1] He said: 'And it is not permissible... etc.' The point of mentioning these issues here is digression, because they are tied to an additional condition beyond a bare sale, like murabaha and tawliya; otherwise mentioning them in the chapter on invalid sales would be more appropriate, as is not hidden from one of sound taste. [2] He said: 'except in real estate'; and it is qualified in al-Tanwir by what is not feared for its destruction; because if it is in a place where that is not secure, it is not permissible like movable property. Mentioned in al-Bahr attributed to al-Mahbubi, and in al-Ikhtiyar: even if it is on the seashore, or the sold item is a ceiling, it is not permissible to sell before possession. End. And in al-Nihaya: Some of our companions said: in a place where that is not secure, it is not permissible according to him just like movable property. And its answer: in a place where it is not feared that it will become sea, or that it will be overwhelmed by increase. End. [3] He said: 'And the difference... etc.' Its explanation: The sale is not permissible before possession of the sold item if it is something that is moved and transported, because of the prohibition regarding it, and the cause is the uncertainty of the dissolution of the first contract in the event of the destruction of the sold item in the seller's hand; for if it is destroyed before possession, the sale is dissolved, and it returns to the previous ownership of the seller, so the buyer becomes a seller of another's property. And when possession occurs, the sale is completed, so he becomes a seller of his own property. Before possession, he does not know whether the sale is completed so he becomes a seller of his own property, or it dissolves so he becomes a seller of another's. So it is not valid, because uncertainty is present, so it is void, unlike the first sale; because this uncertainty does not occur in it; because if the buyer does not take possession and the sale dissolves by destruction, or he takes possession and the sale is completed, the seller in both cases is selling his own property, not another's, unlike our case. This is in al-Kifayah. (1) Tanwir al-Absar p.134. (2) al-Bahr al-Raiq 6:126. (3) al-Kifayah 6:136.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn statement 'It is not permissible to sell what has been purchased before taking possession except in real estate.' The commentator first explains why this rule is mentioned here (by way of digression, because these are sales with additional conditions like murabaha). Then he qualifies the exception for real estate: it is only allowed if the property is not in danger of destruction; if it is on a seashore or in a precarious location, it is treated like movables and sale before possession is not allowed. Finally, he clarifies the difference between this case (the buyer reselling before possession) and the original sale: in the original sale, the seller always sells his own property; but here, if the item is destroyed before possession, the first sale dissolves, and the second buyer would be selling someone else's property. This uncertainty (gharar) invalidates the second sale. The ruling is based on the prohibition and the rational cause of gharar.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Taking physical or constructive possession of the purchased item.
غرر (gharar): Uncertainty or risk that can lead to dispute; here it refers to the uncertainty of whether the sale will be completed or dissolved.
عقار (aqar): Real estate; immovable property such as land and buildings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what condition limits the exception for selling real estate before possession?
A) The real estate must be free of any debt.
B) The real estate must not be in a location where it is feared to be destroyed.
C) The real estate must be residential property.
D) The real estate must be sold at the same price it was purchased.

Key Takeaway
Selling before possession is invalid due to gharar; real estate is only excepted if it is not exposed to destruction.

Selling Before Possession: The General Prohibition and the Real Estate Exception
Ruling with proof on selling before possession with exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling a purchased item before possession, distinguish between movables and real estate, and explain the underlying ʿilla (gharar).

Core Idea
Selling before possession is invalid for movables due to gharar, but allowed for real estate because destruction is rare.

In the Hanafi school, the general rule is that a buyer cannot resell a purchased item before taking possession (qabd). This prohibition is rooted in the risk (gharar) that the item may be destroyed before the buyer takes delivery, potentially leaving the buyer selling what is no longer his. However, an important exception exists for real estate (ʿaqār) because its destruction is rare, making the gharar negligible. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the reasoning, and the intra-Hanafi difference on the exception.

Guided Path
1
State the General Ruling
Identify the base rule: selling a purchased item before possession is invalid (lā yajūz). This applies to movable (manqūl) items.
2
Locate the Exception
Note the exception: 'except in real estate' (illā fī al-ʿaqār). Real estate is excluded from the prohibition.
The exception is further qualified: if the real estate is in a dangerous location (e.g., seashore), it reverts to the rule of movables.
3
Trace the ʿilla (Effective Cause)
Analyze the reasoning: the prohibition is based on gharar – uncertainty whether the first sale will be finalized. If the item is destroyed before possession, the first contract is rescinded, and the buyer would be selling someone else's property. Before possession, this outcome is unknown, creating invalidating uncertainty.
For real estate, destruction is rare (nādir), so the gharar is negligible and the sale is valid.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that the exception does not apply; selling before possession is invalid for all items, including real estate, based on the general prohibition.
The majority (Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf) allow the real estate exception.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is the majority view: real estate can be sold before possession, provided it is in a safe location.
This is the operative ruling in the Hanafi school.

Source Text

A chapter on explaining disposal of the sold item and price before its possession. And it is not permissible to sell a purchased item before its possession except in real estate. [1] His saying: 'And it is not permissible... etc.' The reason these issues are mentioned here is digression, considering they are restricted by an additional condition beyond bare sale without attributes, like Murabaha and Tawliya; otherwise, mentioning them in the chapter on invalid sale would be more appropriate, as is obvious to anyone with sound taste. End quote. And he said in 'Al-Nihayah': Some of our companions said: in a place where that is not safe, it is not permissible in his view as in movable property. Its answer: in a place where it is not feared that it may become a sea, or that increase may prevail. End quote. [3] His saying: 'And the difference... etc.' Its clarification: Indeed the sale was not permitted before possession of the sold item if it is movable and transportable, due to the prohibition therein, and the cause is the gharar of dissolution of the first contract on the assumption of destruction of the sold item in the hands of the seller; because if it is destroyed before possession, the sale is rescinded and returns to the seller's former ownership, so the buyer would be selling someone else's property. And once he takes possession the sale is complete, so he becomes a seller of his own property. Before possession, he does not know whether the sale will be complete (so he becomes a seller of his own property) or will be rescinded (so he becomes a seller of someone else's), so it is not valid because gharar becomes inherent, and it is void. Unlike the first sale, because this gharar does not become inherent in it; because if the buyer does not take possession and the sale is rescinded due to destruction, or he takes possession and the sale is complete, in both cases the seller sells his own property, not someone else's, contrary to our case. Thus in 'Al-Kifayah'.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with legal reasoning from the Hanafi school. It begins by stating the general rule: it is not permissible to sell a purchased item before taking possession (qabd), except for real estate (ʿaqār). The commentator explains that these issues are mentioned here as a digression from the main topic of the book, because they involve an additional condition (like Murabaha and Tawliya) beyond a simple sale; otherwise, they would belong in the chapter on invalid sales. The exception for real estate is qualified: if the real estate is in a location where it is not safe from destruction (e.g., on a seashore where the sea may claim it, or a building that may collapse), then it is treated like movable property and cannot be sold before possession. This is based on several authorities (Al-Tanwir, Al-Bahr, Al-Ikhtiyar, Al-Nihayah). The core reasoning (ʿilla) for the prohibition is the gharar (uncertainty/risk) of the first contract being dissolved by destruction of the item before the buyer takes possession. If the item is movable and transportable (manqūl), destruction is frequent, so selling before possession creates a situation where the seller might be selling someone else's property (if the contract is rescinded). Once possession is taken, the first contract is finalized, and the seller is selling his own property. Before possession, it is uncertain, causing gharar. In contrast, for real estate, destruction is rare, so the gharar is negligible. The first sale (the original buyer-seller transaction) does not have this gharar because the original seller always owns the item, regardless of whether the sale is completed or rescinded. This distinction is the basis for the exception. The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah, and the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that selling before possession is invalid for movables and valid for real estate, as per the majority of the Hanafi scholars (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf).

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Taking physical or constructive possession of the sold item, which completes the sale and removes the risk of rescission.
غرر (gharar): Uncertainty or risk in a contract that can lead to invalidity; specifically here, the risk that the first contract may be rescinded due to destruction before possession.
عقار (ʿaqār): Real estate, immovable property such as land or buildings; treated exceptionally because its destruction is rare.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school permit selling a purchased item before possession in the case of real estate but not for movable property?
A) Because real estate is more valuable and easier to sell.
B) Because the gharar of contract dissolution due to destruction is rare in real estate.
C) Because the hadith only mentions movables.
D) Because the buyer already owns the real estate by contract.

Key Takeaway
Selling before possession is prohibited for movables due to gharar, but permitted for real estate because destruction is rare, as per the majority Hanafi view.
